SECTION 1003 - GENERAL MEANS OF EGRESS
SEC 1003.2 CEILING HEIGHT - "The means of egress shall have a ceiling height of not less than 7 feet 6
inches."
SEC 1003.6 MEANS OF EGRESS CONTINUITY - "The path of egress travel along a means of egress shall
not be interrupted by any building element other than a means of egress component..."
SEC 1003.7 ELEVATORS, ESCALATORS AND MOVING WALKS - "Elevators... shall not be used as a
component of a required means of egress... Exception: Elevators used as an accessible means of egress in
accordance with Section 1007.4" THE BUILDINGS ARE 5 STORIES IN HEIGHT. SELECTED ELEVATORS
SHALL BE USED AS REQUIRED MEANS OF EGRESS AND SHALL BE SERVED BY EMERGENCY
GENERATORS.
TABLE 1004.1.1 MAXIMUM FLOOR AREA ALLOWANCES PER OCCUPANT
THE FOLLOWING SPACES ARE INCLUDED IN THE PROJECT:
Accessory Storage Areas, Mechanical Equipment Room:
300 gross
Assembly without Fixed Seats, Unconcentrated
15 net
Business Areas
100 gross
Exercise Rooms
50 gross
Mercantile
60 gross
Parking garages
200 gross
Residential
200 gross
SECTION 1005 - EGRESS WIDTH
SEC 1005.1 MINIMUM REQUIRED EGRESS WIDTH - "The total width of a means of egress in inches shall
not be less than the total occupant load served... multiplied by 0.3 inches per occupant for stairways and 0.2
inches per occupant for other egress components.
SECTION 1007 - ACCESSIBLE MEANS OF EGRESS
1007.1 ACCESSIBLE MEANS OF EGRESS REQUIRED - "Where more than one means of egress are
required by Section 1015.1 or 1021.1 from any accessible space, each accessible portion of the space shall
be served by accessible means of egress in at least the same number as required by Section 1015.1 or
1021.1.
1007.2.1 ELEVATORS REQUIRED - "In buildings where a required accessible floor is four or more stories
above or below a level of exit discharge, at least one required accessible means of egress shall be an elevator
complying with Section 1007.4." SECTION APPLIES. SEE SECTION 1007.4
1007.3 STAIRWAYS - "In order to be considered part of an accessible means of egress, an exit access
stairway as permitted by Section 1016.1 or exit stairway shall have a clear width of 48 inches minimum
between handrails and shall either incorporate an area of refuge within an enlarged floor-level landing or shall
be accessed from either an area of refuge complying with Section 1007.6 or a horizontal exit. Exceptions: 2.
The clear width of 48 inches between handrails is not required... in buildings equipped throughout with an
automatic sprinkler system installed in accordance with Section 903.3.1.1..." 3. Areas of refuge are not
required at exit stairways in buildings equipped throughout with an automatic sprinkler system installed in
accordance with Section 903.3.1.1..." BUILDING IS EQUIPPED THROUGHOUT WITH AN NFPA 13
AUTOMATIC SPRINKLER SYSTEM. ALL STAIRS ARE MIN. 48" WIDE (HANDRAILS ENCROACH AS
ALLOWED.)
1007.4 ELEVATORS - "In order to be considered part of an accessible means of egress, an elevator shall
comply with the emergency operation and signaling device requirements of Section 2.27 of ASME A 17.1.
Standby power shall be provided in accordance with Chapter 27 and Section 3003. The elevator shall be
accessed from either an area of refuge complying with Section 1007.6 or a horizontal exit. Exceptions: 2.
Elevators are not required to be accessed from an area of refuge or horizontal exit in buildings and facilities
equipped throughout with an automatic sprinkler system installed in accordance with Section 903.3.1.1..."
BUILDING IS EQUIPPED THROUGHOUT WITH AN NFPA 13 AUTOMATIC SPRINKLER SYSTEM.
SECTION 1014 - EXIT ACCESS
SEC 1014.3 COMMON PATH OF EGRESS TRAVEL - "In occupancies other than Groups H-1, H-2 and H-3,
the common path of egress travel shall not exceed 75 feet. For common path of egress travel in Group A
occupancies... see Section 1028.8. Exceptions: 1. "Common path... in Group B, F and S shall not be more
than 100 feet provided that the building is equipped with an automatic sprinkler system... 2. Where a tenant
space in Group B, S and U occupancies has an occupant load of not more than 30, the length of a common
path of egress travel shall not be more than 100 feet. 4. ...in a Group R-2 occupancy shall not be more than
125 feet... with an approved automatic sprinkler system..." BUILDING IS EQUIPPED THROUGHOUT WITH
AN NFPA 13 AUTOMATIC SPRINKLER SYSTEM.

SECTION 1015 - EXIT AND EXIT ACCESS DOORWAYS
SEC 1015.1 EXITS OR EXIT ACCESS DOORWAYS FROM SPACES - "Two exits or exit access doorways
from any space shall be provided where... 1. The occupant load of the space exceeds one of the values in
Table 1015.1. 2. The common path of egress travel exceeds one of the limitations of Section 1014.3."
TABLE 1015.1 - SPACES WITH ONE EXIT OR EXIT ACCESS DOORWAY

SEC 1015.2.1 TWO EXITS OR EXIT ACCESS DOORWAYS - "Where two exits or exit access doorways are
required from any portion of the exit access, the exit doors or exit access doorways shall be placed a distance
apart... measured in a straight line betwen exit doors... Exception 2: Where building is equipped throughout
with an automatic sprinkler system... the separation distance... shall not be less than one-third of the length of
the maxiumum overall diagonal dimension of the area served."
SECTION 1016 - EXIT ACCESS TRAVEL DISTANCE
SEC 1016.1 TRAVEL DISTANCE LIMITATIONS - "Exits shall be so located on each story such that the
maximum length of exit access travel... shall not exceed the diatances given in Table 1016.1." DISTANCES TO
NEAREST EXIT NOTED ON PLANS MAY ALSO BE TO NEAREST EXIT ENCLOSURE OR EXIT
PASSAGEWAY.

SECTION 1022 - EXIT ENCLSOURES
1022.1 ENCLOSURES REQUIRED - "Exit enclosures shall have a fire-resistance rating of not less than 2
hours where connecting four stories or more... Exit enclosures shall have a fire-resistance rating not less than
the floor assembly penetrated, but need not exceed 2 hours. Exit enclosures shall lead directly to the exterior
of the building or shall be extended... with an exit passageway conforming to the requirements of Section
1023..."

DEVELOPMENT
GENERAL NOTES:
1.
THE INFORMATION CONTAINED HEREIN DESCRIBES THE OVERALL DESIGN INTENT OF STATION PARK
GREEN DEVELOPMENT IN ACCORDANCE WITH THE STATION PARK GREEN SPECIFIC PLAN AMENDMENT
AND STATION PARK GREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES AND DEVELOPMENT STANDARDS.  IT IS EXPECTED
THAT AS DESIGNS AND OTHER PROJECT ELEMENTS ARE APPROVED BY THE CITY, THERE MAY BE
MINOR MODIFICATIONS AND ADJUSTMENTS TO THE DESIGNS FOR OTHER INFRASTRUCTURE
DEVELOPMENT AS SHOWN HEREIN.  THUS, MODIFICATIONS AND ADJUSTMENTS SHALL BE ALLOWED.
SO LONG AS THE MODIFICATIONS AND ADJUSTMENTS ARE IN SUBSTANTIAL CONFORMANCE WITH
OVERALL DESIGN INTENT FOR THE INFRASTRUCTURE DEVELOPMENT AS SHOWN HEREIN.  SPECIFIC
INFORMATION INCLUDING, BUT NOT LIMITED TO CURB CUT LOCATIONS, STREET AND SIDEWALK
ELEVATIONS, UTILITY LOCATIONS AND ELEVATIONS, TEMPORARY CONSTRUCTION FACILITIES,
EASEMENT LOCATIONS, DRAINAGE FACILITIES, LANDSCAPING SIGNAGE AND STRIPING, STREET CROSS
SECTIONS ARE ILLUSTRATIVE:  THE PRECISE LOCATIONS, TYPES, QUANTITIES AND SIZES MAY BE
MODIFIED OR ADJUSTED AS APPROVED BY THE CITY PRIOR TO GRADING PERMIT APPROVAL.
2.
ARUP PERFORMED GEOTECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS WHICH ARE PRESENTED IN A DOCUMENT,
PRELIMINARY GEOTECHNICAL INVESTIGATION, STATION PARK GREEN, SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA DATED

GRADING NOTES:
1.
SURFACES SHALL BE GRADED TO PROVIDE POSITIVE DRAINAGE AWAY FROM STRUCTURES.
2. MINIMUM SLOPE AT PAVED AREA IS TYPICALLY 0.5%. MINIMUM SLOPE AT PLANTED AREA IS TYPICALLY
1%.  PAVEMENT SHALL TYPICALLY SLOPE AT 1% TO 2% MIN. AWAY FROM FACE OF BUILDING UNLESS
OTHERWISE NOTED.  CROSS SLOPES PERPENDICULAR TO THE PATH OF TRAVEL SHALL NOT TYPICALLY
EXCEED 2%.  RAMP SLOPES SHALL NOT EXCEED 1:12 SLOPE AT LANDING BETWEEN RAMPS SHALL NOT
EXCEED 2%.
3. EXISTING CLEAN TOPSOIL MAY BE AVAILABLE AND STOCKPILED FOR RE-USE.  TOPSOIL RE-USE TO BE
CONFIRMED PENDING THE RESULTS OF THE TOPSOIL ANALYSIS.
4. MAINTAIN EXISTING GRADE AT " EXISTING PLANT MATERIAL TO REMAIN AND BE PROTECTED".  DO NOT
DISTURB GRADE WITHIN DRIPLINE OF TREES INDICATED "EXISTING PLANT MATERIAL TO REMAIN AND
BE PROTECTED" UNLESS OTHERWISE NOTED. NO EQUIPMENT, STOCKPILING, OR DEBRIS MAY BE
STORED WITHIN TREE ROOT ZONE.
5. WHEN CONSTRUCTION OCCURS WITHIN DRIPLINE OF TREES INDICATED "EXISTING PLANT MATERIAL TO
REMAIN AND BE PROTECTED".  HAND CLEAR AND HAND EXCAVATE TO MINIMIZE DAMAGE TO ROOT
SYSTEM.  EXCAVATE BY HAND USING NARROW-TINE SPANDING FORKS AND COMB SOIL TO EXPOSE
ROOT SYSTEM.  SUPPORT AND PROTECT ROOTS FROM DAMAGE.  DO NOT BREAK OR CHOP ROOTS.
6. DRAWINGS SHOW FINISH GRADE UNLESS OTHERWISE NOTED.
7. PROPOSED GRADES ARE SHOWN TO ILLUSTRATE THE INTENT OF GRADING STRATEGY.  GRADES MAY
CHANGE DURING DETAIL DESIGN.
8. IF VISIBLY CONTAMINATED OR CHEMICALLY ODOROUS SOIL IS ENCOUNTERED DURING
CONSTRUCTION, THE CONTRACTOR WILL STOP WORK IN THE AREA AND CONTACT THE OWNER.
UTILITY NOTES:
1.
UNLESS EXTERNAL AGENCIES OR SITE CONDITIONS REQUIRE OTHERWISE, VAULTS, BOXES, ETC.
SHALL TYPICALLY BE FLUSH TO GRADES ALIGNED AND SET PARALLEL TO ADJACENT JOINTS, CURBS.
ETC.
2. IRRIGATION CONNECTIONS TO WATER MAIN, BACKFLOW PREVENTERS, METERS AND CONTROL
CABINETS TO BE LOCATED IN THE LANDSCAPE STRIP ARE SHOWN ILLUSTRATIVELY.

GENERAL NOTES:
1. DEMOLITION PLANS ARE INCLUDED FOR INFORMATION ONLY. THE
CONTRACTOR SHALL SUBMIT A SEPARATE DEMOLITION PERMIT
APPLICATION, WHICH WILL CONTAIN DEMOLITION PLANS, A DEMOLITION
SPECIFIC STORMWATER POLLUTION PREVENTION PLAN (SWPPP) AND
RECYCLING PLAN.
2. REFER TO L-0.2 FOR TREE REMOVAL PLAN.
DEMOLITION NOTES:
1. DEMOLITION WORK SHALL INCLUDE REMOVAL OF ALL EXISTING
BUILDINGS, ASPHALTIC CONCRETE, SITE UTILITIES, SIDEWALKS,
CONCRETE PAVEMENT, STEEL BRACING, CONCRETE FOOTINGS, TREES,
LIGHT FIXTURES, EXTERIOR WALLS, ROOFING SYSTEM, INTERIOR
PARTITIONS, CEILING, FLOOR FINISH MATERIALS, CASEWORK, EQUIPMENT,
SPECIALTIES, MECHANICAL AND ELECTRICAL SYSTEMS AND OTHER ITEMS,
UNLESS OTHERWISE NOTED.
2. DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR MUST SUBMIT NOTIFICATION FORM TO BAY
AREA AIR QUALITY MANAGEMENT DISTRICT FOR J# AT LEAST 10 WORKING
DAYS PRIOR TO COMMENCEMENT OF DEMOLITION.
3. ALL EXISTING EMERGENCY SYSTEMS TO BE MAINTAINED IN PLACE, (I.E.,
SPRINKLER SYSTEM, CORRIDORS, ALARM SYSTEM, EMERGENCY
LIGHTING), UNTIL IMMEDIATELY PRIOR TO DEMOLITION.
4. DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR SHALL CONTACT UTILITIES TO SHUT-OFF, CAP
OR REMOVE UTILITY SERVICE PRIOR TO REMOVAL OF UTILITY LINES.
5. NOTIFICATION OF WATER MAIN REMOVAL SHALL BE PROVIDED TO
CALWATER BY THE CONTRACTOR, PRIOR TO COMMENCEMENT OF
DEMOLITION.
6. A TANK REMOVAL PERMIT FROM CITY OF SAN MATEO FIRE DEPARTMENT
SHALL BE REQUIRED PRIOR TO REMOVAL OF THE UNDERGROUND TANK.
7. DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR SHALL ESTABLISH A SAFETY PROGRAM TO
MINIMIZE DANGERS OF FIRE, COLLAPSE, PANIC AND EXPLOSION.
8. TRASH, LUMBER, DEBRIS OR OTHER MATERIALS CONSIDERED TO BE
FLAMMABLE OR HAZARDOUS SHALL NOT BE STORED OR ALLOWED TO
ACCUMULATE IN QUANTITIES THAT ENDANGER LIFE OR PROPERTY.
9. DIRECT ACCESS TO FIRE HYDRANTS, FIRE DEPARTMENT CONNECTIONS
AND FIRE ALARM STATIONS MUST BE MAINTAINED AT ALL TIMES.
HYDRANTS, CONNECTIONS AND FIRE ALARM STATIONS MUST BE EASILY
VISIBLE AT ALL TIMES.  PROVIDE FIRE EXTINGUISHERS, NUMBER AND
TYPE SUITABLE FOR TYPE OF FIRE ASSOCIATED WITH THE HAZARDS
PRESENT.
10.DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR TO PROVIDE AND POST AN EMERGENCY FIRE
PROCEDURE PLAN FOR EVACUATION AND CONTAINMENT OF FIRE.
11.DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR MUST PROVIDE AND MAINTAIN ALL
TEMPORARY MEASURES TO CONTROL DUST, NOISE, AIR QUALITY,
EROSION AND SEDIMENT AND WASTE CONTROL  IN COMPLIANCE WITH
ALL APPLICABLE FEDERAL, STATE, LOCAL CODE AND REGULATIONS,
PROJECT CONDITIONS OF APPROVAL  AND MITIGATION  MONITORING AND
REPORTING PROGRAM.
12.DEMOLITION WORK MUST PROCEED IN PHASES SUCH THAT REMOVAL AND
ABATEMENT OF HAZARDOUS MATERIALS MUST OCCUR FIRST.
13. WORK PLAN SHALL BE SUBMITTED BY DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR
INDICATING SEQUENCE OF WORK, ACCESS TO SITE, ROUTES TO BE USED
BY TRUCKS AND OTHER VEHICLES, LOCATION OF SITE PARKING AND
STAGING AREAS, AND LOCATIONS OF FENCES AND BARRICADES.
14.CLEARING WORK SHALL NOT BEGIN UNTIL FENCES, BARRICADES,
EROSION-SEDIMENT CONTROL MEASURES, WARNING SIGNS AND OTHER
PEDESTRIAN CONTROL DEVICES ARE INSTALLED.
15.PRIOR TO DEMOLITION, CONTRACTOR SHALL COORDINATE WITH
ARCHITECT AND OWNER FOR ITEMS TO BE SALVAGED.
16.TREE DEMOLITION SHALL INCLUDE CHIPPING TREE DEBRIS AND
DIVERSION TO COGENERATION PLANT AS FUEL, WHERE FEASIBLE.
17.TREE DEMOLITION SHALL INCLUDE STUMP GRINDING.
18.EXISTING SUBSURFACE UTILITIES SHALL BE REMOVED, UNLESS NOTED
OTHERWISE.
19.HAZARDOUS MATERIALS ABATEMENT AND CONTROL SHALL INCLUDE PLAN
FOR HANDLING AND DISPOSAL OF ASBESTOS-CONTAINING MATERIALS,
LEAD-BASED PAINTS, LEAD-CONTAINING MATERIALS, EXISTING BALLASTS
CONTAINING PCB, MERCURY-CONTAINING LAMPS, MERCURY SWITCHES,
RADIOACTIVE ISOTOPE SMOKE DETECTORS, WASTE OIL FROM
EQUIPMENT, REFRIGERANTS FROM HVAC EQUIPMENT.
20.EXISTING PAVEMENT SHALL REMAIN AS NECESSARY UNTIL REQUIRED TO
BE REMOVED BY GRADING OPERATIONS.
CONSTRUCTION AND DEMOLTION DEBRIS
RECYCLING PROGRAM:
1. ALL NEW CONSTRUCTION AND DEMOLITION OF COMMERCIAL BUILDINGS
MUST COMPLY WITH CITY OF SAN MATEO ORDINANCE FOR
CONSTRUCTION & DEMOLITION DEBRIS DIVERSION.  CONTACT CITY OF
SAN MATEO RECYCLING SPECIALIST AT 650 522-7346.
2. DEMOLITION CONTRACTOR MUST FILL OUT CITY OF SAN MATEO FORM
FOR CONSTRUCTION & DEMOLITION PROJECTS-RECYCLING & WASTE
REDUCTION PLAN.  FORM AVAILABLE AT:
HTTP://WWW.CITYOFSANMATEO.ORG/DOWNLOADS/PUBWORKS.
3. "WEIGHT TAGS" ARE REQUIRED FROM DEBRIS BOXES AND SELF-HAUL
TRUCK LOADS, WHETHER RECYCLED OR GARBAGE.
4. DEBRIS BOX COMPANIES KEEP TAGS UNTIL PROJECT IS COMPLETED.
5. DIVERSION RATE REQUIREMENTS: MINIMUM RECYCLING RATE OF 60% IS
REQUIRED FOR ALL NEW CONSTRUCTION AND DEMOLITION.

GRADING AND
DRAINAGE PLAN
NOTES:
1. THE GRADING AND STORM DRAIN SYSTEM SHOWN IN THE COURTYARD
AND INTERNAL WALKWAYS IS ILLUSTRATIVE, TO BE CONFIRMED
DURING DETAIL DESIGN.
2. PERMEABLE/IMPERMEABLE AREA CALCULATION SUMMARIZES THE
PERMEABILITY OF THE BUILDINGS, PARKS AND STREETS. THE
STORMWATER TREATMENT AREAS SHOWN ON THIS PLAN ARE BEST
MANAGEMENT PRACTICES (BMPs) SIZED TO TREAT STORMWATER
RUN-OFF FROM THE PROJECT. THE POST-CONSTRUCTION PEAK
STORMWATER DISCHARGE RATE ENTERING THE CITY'S STORM DRAIN
SYSTEM FROM THE SITE WILL BE LESS THAN THE PRE-CONSTRUCTION
PEAK STORMWATER DISCHARGE RATE.
3. SUBDRAINS WILL BE INSTALLED ALONG THE INSIDE OF THE NORTHERN
AND WESTERN PROPERTY LINE TO CAPTURE RUNOFF FROM EVAE
PRIOR TO FLOWING TO POST OFFICE AND CALTRAIN PARKING LOT
4. EROSION CONTROL FACILITIES SHALL BE PROVIDED DURING
CONSTRUCTION AND WILL BE INSTALLED IN ACCORDANCE WITH THE
CITY OF SAN MATEO'S EROSION AND SEDIMENT REQUIREMENT
MANUAL.
5. ALL STORM DRAINS REQUIRED TO HAVE "NO DUMPING--DRAINS TO
BAY"  STENCILED IN ACCORDANCE WITH COUNTY OF SAN MATEO C.3
REQUIREMENTS
6. CUT-FILL ANALYSIS IS BASED ON BULK VOLUMES AND DOES NOT
INCLUDE BULKING/SHRINKAGE FACTORS, TRENCH/FOUNDATION
SPOILS, ETC. VOLUMES TO BE CONFIRMED DURING DETAIL DESIGN.

RE: Retail at Station Park Green, San Mateo 
 
 
Dear Zachary and Staff, 
 
Thank you for your time last week to discuss the retail space at Station Park Green, and for the follow-up 
responses provided by Simin. RRER has since reviewed the plans, visited the site, and spoken with the 
listing broker. Based on this due diligence, I would like to share several observations and 
recommendations for the City’s consideration in response to the property owner’s proposed 
amendment to their specific plan. 
 
Background 
Station Park Green is located at the intersection of Delaware and Concar in San Mateo. It is a mixed-use 
project with approximately 599 residential units, 25,033 square feet of retail, 7,991 square feet of office, 
and 3,034 square feet of amenity space. Retail parking is limited, with fewer than 2 stalls per 1,000 
square feet, and office/amenity parking being provided at 0.32 stalls per 1,000 square feet. 
 
The listing brokers, (Matt Sweeney and Sean O’Carrol of The Econic Company,)  have marketed the retail 
space since 2017. In 8 years of leasing efforts, they have leased 3 retail spaces, and all of the tenants 
have departed before their leases expired. Currently, there are no retail spaces occupied.  
 
The owner of the property has asked the City to approve an amendment allowing some existing retail to 
be used as additional office space and a reconfiguration of the ground floor commercial layout. The City 
has requested RRER’s assessment of the retail program, including whether a reduction is warranted and, 
if so, to what extent. 
 
Retail Leasing Challenges 
In reviewing both the broker’s letter (attached) and market feedback, it is clear that multiple design and 
locational factors have contributed to the lack of leasing success. These include: 
• 
Uninviting retail exteriors, with limited or no signage. 
• 
Uniform storefront design, which does not create the sense of variety that attracts pedestrians. 
• 
Extremely limited number of pedestrians in the area- maybe due to lack of construction of the 
approved residential units near the project. 
• 
Inconvenient parking: no street parking near the retail, with retail parking located only in a 
garage, with limited pedestrian egress. 
• 
Construction/design limitations: structural slabs prevent normal saw-cutting, leading to 
problematic sub-floors and poorly placed bathrooms. This causes a massive cost increase when 
a tenant needs to accommodate floor drains, and grease interceptors.

--- page 167 ---
• 
Inadequate loading and delivery access, especially for food users. 
• 
Weak/No retail synergy: the project is isolated, and the relocation of Trader Joe’s to an adjacent 
corner has not materialized. 
• 
Asking rents above market for this part of San Mateo. The landlord now cannot seem to lease 
space even at rents reduced by 50%. 
• 
High build-out costs:  shell delivery includes a bathroom and HVAC units, but all food tenants 
need to saw cut the floor which requires nearly a 100% price increase from a normal concrete 
floor. With the landlord’s TI allowance at approximately $60 per square foot, this causes an 
unrealistic financial gap for small independent tenants. 
These combined challenges suggest that the original retail allocation is not viable in this location or 
configuration. 
 
 
Recommendations 
RRER supports a reduction in the retail square footage. The owner has indicated its desire to use the 
former Beach Hut space as office space so as to create a continuous office space for their occupancy.  
That would leave retail spaces along Concar Ave, spaces shown as 6, 7, and 9 on the attached site plan.  
This is approximately 7200 square feet of retail space.  Note- Space # 8 is designated by the property 
owner as a Community Center, to be used by the community for free. 
 
RRER recommends the following actions: 
 
• 
Space 6 , approximately 2000 square feet, (former Starbucks) is equipped with valuable 
infrastructure, including floor drains and water hook ups that can easily be used by another 
coffee store.  While a grease trap/interceptor could not be seen within the space, it is unlikely 
Starbucks could have received County health department approvals, so the space may even 
have the required grease trap, making it suitable for another food tenant. 
• 
Space 7, approximately 1200 square feet, adjacent to the former Starbucks space may benefit 
from co-tenancy synergy. 
• 
Space 9, at nearly 4000 sf will be hard to lease for retail. While the space has bathrooms, there 
are many fewer tenants of that size in todays retail activity. And most tenants of that size will 
want more retail synergy  than what exists at Station Park Green.  Therefore, it is suggested that 
Space 9 be allowed for quasi-retail, or office. Maybe a non-profit within San Mateo looking for 
discounted space. 
• 
All spaces, will need signage help. Electricity should be brought to the existing canopies so a 
tenant can add a sign band that is back lit. Leniency should be given by the City in approving 
large amounts of sign square footage, so signs could be seen by cars as they exit the freeway on 
the adjacent freeway exist. 
 
Closing 
We appreciate the opportunity to provide input on this matter. Our recommendations are intended to 
help align the project with market realities while ensuring that the City continues to foster a balanced, 
sustainable mix of uses at Station Park Green. We would welcome the chance to discuss these findings 
in greater detail and to collaborate further with City staff and the property owner to identify solutions 
that best serve both the community and the long-term success of the project. 
 
Sincerely, 
RRER (Retail Real Estate Resources)

Memorandum 
 
Date:  
July 22, 2025 
 
To: 
 
Mr. Chris Kober 
 
From:  
Ethan Heckman, Shikha Jain 
 
 
 
Subject: 
Trip Generation Study for Station Park Green Office Use 
 
 
Hexagon Transportation Consultants, Inc. has completed a trip generation study for the proposed 
office use at Station Park Green development in San Mateo, California. Station Park Green is a mix 
of residential, retail, and office land uses located at the northwest corner of Delaware Street & 
Concar Drive near the Hayward Park Caltrain station. The project proposes to convert 25,000 
square feet of the existing 30,000 square feet of ground-floor retail space in building MU-1 to office 
space. 
Trip Generation 
The trips for the existing and proposed land uses were estimated using trip rates published in the 
Institute of Transportation Engineers’ (ITE) Trip Generation Manual, 11th Edition.  
Strip Retail Plaza (<40k) (Land Use 822) was used to estimate the trips generated by the retail 
uses, and General Office Building (Land Use 710) was used to estimate the trips generated by the 
office uses.  
Pass-by trip reductions published in the ITE Trip Generation Manual, 11th Edition were applied to 
the retail uses to account for existing trips on the nearby roadway network, such as those to school 
or work, which would visit the site when passing by.  
The existing site is also subject to a transportation demand management (TDM) program and 
therefore, trip reduction associated with it were applied to the trip generation estimates. The TDM 
program was developed by Nelson/Nygaard Consulting Associates in May 2010 when the specific 
plan for this site was approved. Per Nelson/Nygaard’s study, the trip reductions associated with this 
TDM program would be 32 percent. This trip reduction includes reductions based on density, mix of 
uses, transit service, pedestrian/bicycle facilities, and affordable housing. 
As shown in Table 1, the proposed conversion from retail to office uses would generate fewer trips: 
197 fewer daily trips, including 14 fewer trips  during the AM peak hour, and 22 fewer trips during 
the PM peak hour.

How to Use this Specific Plan 
1. Overview [03] 
The Overview is the introduction to this Specific Plan and a summary 
of Station Park Green, including its vision, program, and design 
intent. This chapter also discusses the Plan within the physical and 
policy setting of San Mateo. 
2. Existing Conditions [15] 
From location to site and edge conditions, to existing transit and 
utilities infrastructure, the Existing Conditions chapter describes the 
Plan Area in its current state. 
3. Guiding Principles [35] 
The Station Park Green Specific Plan is based upon a set of planning 
and design principles. The Guiding Principles chapter presents 
the basic precepts related to transit connectivity, respecting 
the neighborhood context, synergy of uses, publicly accessible open 
spaces, and sustainability. 
4. Development Standards [51] 
This chapter describes Station Park Green in detail, including 
proposed block pattern, land uses, urban design framework, publicly 
accessible open spaces, streets, transit and infrastructure improvements. 
5. Implementation [121] 
The subsequent steps necessary to realize The Station Park Green 
Specific Plan are listed in this Implementation chapter.

STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
Station Park Green  
is envisioned as a 
defining new  
neighborhood for San  
Mateo. It will build on 
the City’s strong  
tradition of distinctive, 
high-quality  
neighborhoods.  
Community parks and 
landscaped  thorough-
fares will  set the tone 
for the  area and pro-
vide  an inviting front  
door, welcoming the  
broader community to 
use and enjoy it.

--- page 193 ---
Introduction  [ 03 ] 
Vision and Goals  [ 04 ] 
Location  [ 06 ] 
General Description  [ 09 ] 
Graphic Note  [ 10 ] 
Legal Requirements  [ 10 ] 
Corridor Plan and General Plan Relationship  [ 11 ] 
Public Participation  [ 11 ] 
Overall Phasing  [ 11 ] 
3 
Chapter 1 
Overview 
The City of San Mateo (City) conducted a study 
of land use and transportation issues in a half-
mile radius around the Hillsdale and Hayward 
Park  Caltrain stations. That study resulted in 
the San  Mateo Rail Corridor Transit-Oriented 
Development  Plan (Corridor Plan), approved by 
the City Council  on June 6, 2005. The intent of 
the Corridor Plan  is to allow, encourage, and 
provide guidance  for the creation of a world 
class transit-oriented  development (TOD), while 
maintaining and  improving the quality of life of 
those who already  live and work in the area.

--- page 194 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
4 
The area (Plan Area) subject to  
the Station Park Green Specific  
Plan (Plan) is an approximately  
12-acre portion of the Hayward  
Park Station TOD Overlay Zone.  
The development permitted  
under this Plan follows directly  
from the policies set for the  
Hayward Park Station TOD Zone  
under the Corridor Plan:  multi-
family uses developed at transit-
supportive densities, with some  
neighborhood- and community-  
serving retail uses and services-
-including specialty uses that  
enhance neighborhood services  
within the TOD--and publicly  
accessible open space. 
This chapter gives an overview  
of the Plan. Chapter 2 describes  
existing conditions within the  
Plan Area. Chapter 3 explains  
design principles for the Plan.  
Chapter 4 specifies the framework 
for development within the  Plan 
Area. Chapter 5 describes  steps 
for implementing the Plan,  
including permits and phasing.  
Vision and Goals  
Station Park Green is envisioned  
as a defining new neighborhood  
for San Mateo. It will build  on 
the City’s strong tradition  
of distinctive, high-quality  
neighborhoods. Community parks  
and landscaped thoroughfares  
will set the tone for the area  
and provide an inviting front  
door, welcoming the broader  
community to use and enjoy it.  
The overarching purpose of  
Station Park Green is to fulfill 
the  goals of the Corridor Plan—
to  foster TOD and, thereby, to  
implement San Mateo’s chosen  
approach to growth in this area. 
To capitalize on existing  transit, 
Station Park Green  will be a 
compact mix of uses  structured 
on a walkable grid  near Hayward 
Park Station. The  residential, 
retail, commercial,  and open 
space program will  provide a 
complementary and  continually 
active mix of uses.  Goods and 
services will be  convenient and 
accessible to the  neighborhood 
and the wider  public.   
Integral to its transit-oriented  
purpose, this Plan combines  
sustainability measures and  
community amenities that 
reflect  a commitment to parks 
and  environmental stewardship.  
The Plan Area’s system of 
open  spaces and pedestrian-
oriented  streets will be safe and 
attractive,  bordered by active 
uses and  residential entries, with 
multiple  ‘eyes on the street.’  
This  commitment will ensure 
that  Station Park Green is not 
only safe  and attractive for its 
residents but  also a respectful 
and beneficial  neighbor to 
existing nearby  communities.  
Based upon the aims of the  
Corridor Plan, the Preliminary  
Application, the community  
process, and multiple Planning  
Commission study sessions,  as 
well as sound planning and  
design practice, the following  
principles guide this Plan:  
•   Implement the transit-oriented  
development aims of the  
Corridor Plan. 
•   Consider the area context in                
determining the location, height,  
and density of uses. 
•   Lead with landscape, investing  
in parks and streetscape to  
establish the character of the  
area. 
•   Facilitate station-to- 
neighborhood connectivity. 
•   Create a vital center with a  
vibrant mix of uses. 
•   Design for sustainability. 
These principles are further  
detailed in Chapter 3: Guiding  
Principles.

--- page 195 ---
CHAPTER 1 OVERVIEW
FIGURE 1.01 Bird’s-eye view of Station Park Green looking east to San Francisco Bay

--- page 196 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
FIGURE 1.02  Location of Station Park Green Specific Plan Area  
Location 
The Plan Area is located in the  
City of San Mateo, California,  
within the long-standing north-
south development corridor that  
runs between the Santa Cruz  
Mountains to the west and San  
Francisco Bay to the east. As  
shown in Figure 1.02, the Plan  
Area is situated adjacent to the  
Hayward Park Caltrain Station,  
midway between San Mateo’s  
downtown and Hillsdale stations.  
Less than one mile from the  
junction of State Route 92 (SR  92) 
and U.S. Highway 101, and  locat-
ed at an SR 92 exit, the Plan  Area 
is at a regional crossroads,  with 
convenient access to the  cities of 
San Francisco to the  north and 
San Jose to the south,  and to the 
East Bay. 
The 12-acre Plan Area lies at  
the northwest corner of Concar  
Drive and South Delaware Street,  
just north of SR 92, as shown 
in Figure 1.03. The property is 
currently occupied by retail and 
office uses—a Kmart store, offic-
es, a Michaels store, and a Shell 
station— and a large expanse of 
accessory  surface parking.   
A narrow, approximately three-
acre swath of land owned by the 
Peninsula Corridor Joint Powers 
Board (Joint Powers Board or JPB) 
separates the Plan Area from the 
Caltrain rail right-of-way to the west, 
run by the Joint Powers Board. To 
the north, the Plan Area is bounded 
by the offices of the California State 
Automobile Association (AAA) and 
a U.S.  Postal Service facility.   
6

FIGURE 1.03 Aerial view of the Plan Area in its existing condition

STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
FIGURE 1.04 Station Park Green illustrative site plan and program  
Program 
599 Residential Units 
25,000 to 30,000 square feet Retail 
10,000 to 15,000 square feet Office 
2.3 acres of Open Space  
(excluding streets and  sidewalks)  
8

--- page 199 ---
General Description  
The Station Park Green Specific 
Plan  enables, at its most basic 
level, a clustered mix of uses 
to support  transit usage. At 
another level, the Plan advances 
a sustainable, parks-oriented, and 
community-centered ethic. 
As a TOD, Station Park Green will  
contain a mix of uses to minimize  
car trips. The new neighborhood 
will include approximately 599 
residential units, 10,000 square 
feet of office space, 25,000 square 
feet of retail  space, and 2.3 
acres of publicly accessible open 
space, not including streets and 
sidewalks. The flexibility of this 
program is detailed in Chapter 4. 
Station Park Green favors either 
underground or ‘enclosed’ parking 
to enhance the vitality of the 
streets with attractive residential or  
retail ground-floor entrances. The  
preferred condition is for the Plan  
Area to operate in conjunction with 
the JPB property by creating a new 
street to connect to Garvey Way .
The office space and the 
neighborhood-serving retail uses 
will be located on pedestrian 
friendly streetscapes  
to maximize transit convenience 
and enhance the transit user’s 
experience as well as active the 
street life of the project. The Plan 
pairs ground-floor retail uses with 
a tight-knit residential character  
along The Park and Concar Drive, 
to  enliven streets with continuous 
daily activity. 
As shown in the illustrative site 
plan (Figure 1.04), the Plan has 
a walkable grid of primary and 
secondary streets, and bicycle 
connections, that link South 
Delaware Street and Concar Drive 
to the station. The connections 
through publicly accessible open 
spaces prioritizes the pedestrian 
experience. Hayward Park 
Station will be a quick walk from 
any point in the compact new  
neighborhood. Points of access to  
the neighborhood are aligned with  
existing local streets. The Plan is  
regionally and locally connected.  
It is the goal of this plan to 
integrate seamlessly with the 
adjacent JPB property by 
providing for direct connection of 
vehicular, bike and pedestrian 
ways through to the JPB 
property. Seamless integration 
and through connections 
between these two properties 
will help establish easy access 
to the transit stop and hence 
a successful transit-oriented 
neighborhood. The Plan 
envisions the JPB property 
not merely as a destination for 
people using Hayward Park 
Station but also as a through-
way for pedestrians and cyclists 
traveling in both directions 
across the railroad right-of-way 
to and from the Plan Area. The 
Emergency Vehicle  Access 
Easement (EVAE) shown 
throughout this document 
along the southwestern 
boundary of the Plan is merely 
a contingency  plan, to ensure 
required fire  safety access for 
the project.  The preferred 
condition is to develop the Plan 
Area in conjunction with the 
JPB property. 
Station Park Green will be a 
small  neighborhood with eight 
9 
diverse  open spaces—The Park, 
Park Corridor, Station Corridor, 
Gateway Park, Community Park, 
Greenway Pocket Parks, RE-3 
Courtyard, and Delaware/Concar 
Plaza—2.3 acres  in total. 
At the core of the Plan is The 
Park, a new, approximately 1.1-
acre neighborhood park, framed 
by residences and retail uses. 
Tree-lined streets will extend from 
The Park into the framework of 
walkable blocks. Green corridors  
and landscaped streets extending 
from The Park will link to open 
spaces within the Plan, as well 
as in the existing surrounding 
neighborhoods. This extension of 
The Park into the street network 
infuses the Plan with an identifiable 
and cohesive character. The 
Community Park and Gateway Park  
will connect to The Park through 
both visual and physical means. 
The Park Corridor will extend The 
Park to South Delaware Street, 
toward Nineteenth Avenue Park.  
The parks and streetscapes will also  
integrate stormwater management  
techniques.  
At the core of the Plan is The Park, a new, approximately 1.1-acre 
neighborhood park, framed by residences and retail uses. Tree-lined streets 
will extend from The Park into the framework of walkable blocks.  
CHAPTER 1 OVERVIEW

--- page 200 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
10  
The building massing and  
architecture of Station Park 
Green will be varied and 
articulated to create a high-
quality development with 
modulation and a cohesive,  
though non-uniform, identity.  
Smaller-scale residential 
buildings will create a smooth 
transition  to the existing 
residential  neighborhoods to 
the east. The concentration of  
buildings with greater height 
and density near the Caltrain 
Station aids transit ridership. 
In addition, the strategy of 
placing higher buildings 
along  Concar Drive and the 
Joint Powers Board property 
creates a buffer for The Park 
and the neighborhood from 
SR 92 and the Caltrain line.  
Graphic Note  
For the purposes of this Plan, all 
graphics addressing the Plan  Area 
at a regional or city scale are  shown 
with north oriented toward  the top 
of the document (Figure  1.05). 
However, all graphics at a local scale 
are oriented with north to the upper 
left of the document (Figure 1.06).  
Legal  
Requirements 
This Plan meets the legal  require-
ments for specific plans  estab-
lished under Section 65450 et  
seq. of the California Government  
Code and San Mateo Municipal  
Code Section 27.06.080. Specific  
Plans must include certain text  
and diagrams that specify land  
uses, utilities, provisions for  
conservation of natural resources,  
and implementation measures.   
Land uses, including residential  
and floor area densities, as well 
as  open space usable by the 
public, are described in Chapter 
4. Major infrastructure for the Plan 
is also described in Chapter 4 in 
the section entitled “Infrastructure  
Improvements.” A program of  
implementation measures is in  
Chapter 5.

General 
Description
(continued)
FIGURE 1.05 Orientation of all graphics at a regional 
and  city scale

--- page 201 ---
Corridor  
Plan and   
General Plan   
Relationship 
This Plan is not intended to  
supplant the Corridor Plan, but  
to implement it. Specifically, to  
further implement Policy 7.7 of  
the Corridor Plan, the Plan Area  
is planned in a comprehensive  
manner to ensure the creation of  
a transit-oriented development  
that is consistent with the  
Corridor Plan. This Plan, like the  
Corridor Plan, is consistent with  
the General Plan.  Development  
within the Plan Area must comply  
with this Plan, the Corridor Plan  
and the General Plan.  
Public   
Participation 
Through the City’s Preliminary  
Application process, preparation 
prior to submittal of this Plan  
included formal and informal  
neighborhood meetings. Many  
more public neighborhood  
meetings and Planning  
Commission and City Council  
hearings came after application.  
These sessions brought together 
a cross-section of San Mateo  
citizens and officials to shape,  
review, and discuss the Plan and  
its relationship to the goals of 
the  Corridor Plan.   
The City originally adopted this 
Plan in 2011. Three years later, 
after development had not 
proceeded, the City amended 
this Plan.  A new round of 
community outreach occurred at 
that time. 
Overall   
Phasing 
The Plan serves to assure the 
City and the owners of land 
within the Plan  Area that 
future development  will fulfill 
its intended form and  sense 
of place. The Plan does  this 
by:  setting the location and 
specifications for roads, 
utilities, open spaces, and other 
common areas; establishing a 
block and open space pattern; 
and setting land uses, densities, 
building heights, and lot 
coverage. 
In future years, the owners of  
land within the Plan Area must  
seek additional approvals from  
the City to develop that land.  
Specifically, a development may  
not commence construction of  
a building unless the Zoning  
Administrator, the Planning  
Commission, and, for buildings  
exceeding 55 feet in height,  
the City Council, approve an  
application for site plan and  
architectural review (SPAR). It is  
anticipated that it could take ten 
years for all SPAR applications to 
11 
be complete under the Plan.  
As provided under City code,  
to approve a SPAR application,  
the decision maker must review  
detailed drawings, including floor  
plans, of all proposed buildings.   
Concurrently with approval  of 
the Plan in 2011, the City and the 
owner of the land within the Plan 
Area entered  into a contractually 
binding  development agreement, 
as described in Chapter 5:  
Implementation (Development 
Agreement). The Development 
Agreement was still in effect at the 
time that the City amended this 
Plan in 2014.

Chapter 2 
Existing Conditions 
Station Park Green is located on approximately 
12 acres of developed land adjacent to 
the Hayward Park Station. The following 
chapter details the existing uses, character, 
transportation network, and utilities at the Plan 
Area and its edges.

--- page 206 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
FIGURE 2.01 Aerial view of Plan Area in its existing condition

--- page 207 ---
CHAPTER 2 EXISTING CONDITIONS 
Plan Area and  
Current 
Facilities 
The Plan Area is bounded by  
South Delaware Street to the east  
and Concar Drive to the south  (Figure 
2.01). Across Concar Drive to the 
south, SR 92 rises above grade to 
cross the Caltrain right-of-way. To the 
west, the Joint Powers Board owns 
a relatively narrow, approximately 
three-acre strip of property used for 
surface parking, which separates the 
Plan Area from the Caltrain tracks. 
To the north, Garvey Way, the Post  
Office, and the AAA office building  
sit between the Plan Area and  
residential properties along East 16th 
Avenue.  
The Plan Area is currently occupied  
by retail and office uses, including  
Kmart store, offices, Michaels store, 
a Shell gas station, and their surface  
parking areas. The Kmart store is  
located generally in the middle of  
the 12 acres, significantly set back  
from its primary vehicular access  on 
South Delaware Street. This vehicular 
access is opposite the entrance 
to the Nineteenth Avenue Park 
neighborhood across South  
Delaware Street.  
FIGURE 2.02 Kmart building and  
parking lot, facing east 
The Kmart building is  
approximately 37 feet tall and  
has about 148,000 square feet  
of retail space on two floors,  
plus office and storage space. 
A large expanse of surface 
parking surrounds the  store 
(Figures 2.02, 2.03, 2.04).  A 
narrow buffer of landscape  
separates the parking lot from  
South Delaware Street. Within  
the parking lot, small islands  
with mulch support small trees.  
Between the Shell station and  
the AAA building, the edge  
of the Plan Area along South  
Delaware Street is very open,  
with no building edge defining  
the space of the street.  
FIGURE 2.03 Plan Area with Kmart 
parking lot, facing southeast 
The Michaels store is located  
southeast of the Kmart building  
with frontage on Concar Drive.  
An arts-and-crafts supply store,  
Michaels occupies a building 
of approximately 19,000 square  
feet that is approximately 21-feet 
high. Vehicular access to Michaels  
exists opposite the access ramps  
to SR 92. Except for its eastern  
boundary with the Shell station,  
the Michaels property is also sur-
rounded by surface parking and 
rudimentary landscape. 
The Shell gas station sits at  the 
northwest corner of the intersec-
tion of Concar Drive and South 
Delaware Street. Its facilities in-
clude an auto service station, gas 
pump canopy, and car wash.  
17 
FIGURE 2.04 Kmart building and  
parking lot, facing northwest 
Currently, the Plan Area includes  
over nine acres of impermeable  
surface parking around the 
Kmart, Michaels, and Shell station 
facilities. At present, only about 
six percent of the entire Plan Area 
consists of permeable, vegetated 
surfaces in the form of parking lot 
islands.

Edge   
Conditions 
The immediate edges of the Plan 
Area are public streets, surface  
parking for the AAA building, 
and the Joint Powers Board 
property, which is also mostly 
surface parking. Because the 
existing Kmart, Michaels, and 
Shell station facilities are set 
back from the sidewalks, there 
is little streetwall definition 
along either the South Delaware 
Street or Concar Drive corridors. 
South Delaware Street, Concar 
Drive, and the Joint Powers 
Board property that surround 
the Plan Area have some 
streetscape improvements 
including sidewalks, street 
trees, low shrubs, tree grates, 
mulching, small lawn areas, and 
pedestrian lighting. Along all 
streets, the Plan Area edges are 
open and almost entirely paved, 
with predominant views of the 
surface parking lots and limited 
streetscape features within the 
Plan Area (Figures 2.05,  
2.06).  
As noted in Chapter 1, this Plan  is 
intended to implement the Corridor 
Plan. In turn, the intent of the Corri-
dor Plan is to allow, encourage, and 
provide guidance for the creation 
of  Transit Oriented Development 
(TOD). The area covered by this 
Plan is an approximately 12-acre 
portion of the Hayward Park Station 
TOD Zone. 
The remainder of the Hayward 
Park Station TOD Zone includes 
property owned and operated 
by the Joint Powers Board to the 
west, and AAA and the U.S. Postal 
Service to the north.  
Hayward Park 
Station TOD Zone

--- page 209 ---
CHAPTER 2 EXISTING CONDITIONS 
Neighborhood   
Setting  
Aspects of the Plan—including  
the land use program, massing,  
height, circulation pattern, and 
open space system—account  
for the possibility of future 
development of the remainder 
of the Hayward Park TOD 
Zone in conformity with the 
Corridor Plan. Specifically, the 
block and street system for 
the Plan Area is aligned with 
those of neighboring parcels,  
should redevelopment of the  
remaining land in the TOD 
Zone occur. 
Moreover, as discussed in 
Chapter 5, this Plan may 
be amended. Such an 
amendment could, if the City 
so approves, enlarge the 
Plan Area to encompass the 
entire TOD Zone. Details on 
the amendment process are 
provided in Chapter 5.  
The Plan Area lies amidst a 
mix of uses including service 
commercial and single- and 
multi-family residential uses. 
Mid- and high-rise commercial 
properties are located to the 
south and southeast of the 
Plan Area, along Concar Drive 
and SR 92. To the north and 
northeast, beyond the existing 
Post Office and AAA, the uses 
are predominantly single- and 
multi-family residential. Further  
implementation of the Corridor 
Plan and San Mateo City Zoning 
suggest that the area covered 
by this Plan will, in the future, be 
surrounded by TOD uses, except 
for Nineteenth Avenue Park to 
the northeast. Though the Plan 
Area is closest to the Nineteenth 
Avenue Park neighborhood, 
Hayward Park, Sunnybrae and 
Fiesta Gardens are nearby as 
well. The street networks  of 
these neighborhoods are shown  
in Figure 2.07.

Caltrain Station  
and Parking  
 
The Caltrain right-of-way runs 
north-south, west of the Plan 
Area. The rail is elevated slightly 
above the surrounding land-
scape, and low areas along the 
tracks function as ad-hoc storm-
water channels (Figure 2.08). 
Caltrain’s Hayward Park Station 
is located just north of the SR 92 
overpass, where Concar Drive 
intersects with Pacific Boulevard. 
At both ends of the station plat-
form there are at-grade pedestri-
an crossings  over the tracks. 
The Plan Area does not abut  
the Caltrain right-of-way; it is  
separated by property owned  
by the Joint Powers Board.  
This narrow, approximately 
three-acre property consists of 
surface parking for the station.  
Chain-linked fencing and narrow  
landscape strips buffer the Joint 
Powers Board property  from 
surrounding uses (Figure  
2.09). Across the tracks to the  
west of the rail, there is a mix of  
service commercial, community  
commercial and multi-family  
residential uses, which is also  
zoned for TOD.

Concar Drive 
To the south, Concar Drive  
separates the Plan Area from  
large commercial properties  
along the SR 92 corridor (Figure  
2.10). These commercial buildings  
are set back from Concar Drive  
and surrounded by surface  
parking lots. The elevated SR  
92 rises to approximately 35  
feet above grade behind these  
buildings to pass over the Caltrain  
right-of-way (Figure 2.11).  
Entrance and exit ramps for the  
SR 92 intersect with Concar Drive  
near the entrance to Michaels. 
As shown in Figure 2.12, the scale  
of the existing development on  
Concar Drive is higher than the  
residential uses to the north and  
northeast of the Plan Area. Within  
a half-mile of Station Park Green,  
there are buildings of between  
five and ten stories along Concar  
Drive and the SR 92 corridor. 
800 and 900 Concar Drive are  
each five stories plus mechanical  
penthouse; the Marriott Hotel is  
six stories; and the buildings of  
The Crossroads are ten stories,  
approximately 140 feet in height.

South Delaware Street and Nineteenth Avenue Park  
To the east, South Delaware Street  
separates the Plan Area from the  
Nineteenth Avenue Park neighborhood.   
A community developed by builder  
Joseph Eichler in the 1950s, the  
Nineteenth Avenue Park neighborhood  
consists of single- and multi-family  
residences. These houses are oriented  
away from South Delaware Street; they  
front on Eleanor Drive within the internal  
Nineteenth Avenue Park neighborhood  
street network. One access road, Charles  
Lane, leads into Nineteenth Avenue Park  
from South Delaware Street. 
The residences of Nineteenth Avenue  
Park are separated from the Plan Area by 
South Delaware Street. South Delaware  
Street has four travel lanes and one  
turning lane, for a total right-of-way  
width of approximately 70 feet, including  
sidewalks. In addition, as shown in  
Figure 2.16, a high wooden fence and  
streetscape with mature trees along the  
eastern side of South Delaware Street  
significantly buffer the homes within  
Nineteenth Avenue Park from traffic on  
South Delaware Street, as well as from  
the Plan Area beyond.

Figure 2.17 Public open space in San Mateo  
24
The City of San Mateo includes  
substantial park and open space  
acreage within its boundaries,  
especially along its boundary  
with San Francisco Bay. Notably,  
Central Park in downtown San 
Mateo sets a high standard for  
public open spaces in the City  
(Figure 2.17). 
Despite this existing acreage 
and  tradition of park design 
in the City, the Plan Area is 
located in  a part of the City 
with a relative dearth of public 
open space. Within one half-
mile of the Plan Area, there 
are three small parks. These 
are the Concar playground 
in Nineteenth Avenue Park, 
Sunnybrae playground on  
Folkstone Avenue, and Trinta 
Park on Palm Avenue, which 
has two baseball fields and a 
basketball  court.   
The Leslie Creek channel is 
zoned  as an open space. While 
the channel supports some 
natural features, it would require 
more improvement to become a 
natural trail.  
City Open Space System

--- page 215 ---
CHAPTER 2 EXISTING CONDITIONS 
Transportation  
Immediately adjacent to transit  
and the state highway system,  
the Plan Area is exceptionally  
well connected to the region’s  
transportation networks.  
Transit 
Caltrain, the north-south  
commuter rail line on the  
Peninsula, and SamTrans, the  
primary bus service for San 
Mateo  County, both provide 
transit  service to the Plan Area. 
Caltrain 
The Hayward Park Caltrain 
Station  provides local service. 
Also  during peak hours, a 
Caltrain  shuttle bus operates 
an hourly  service that is 
synchronized with  the train 
between the Hayward  Park 
Caltrain Station, various  local 
office buildings, and the  
Lakeshore and Fiesta Gardens  
neighborhoods. 
The Hillsdale Caltrain station,  
located approximately 1.5 miles  
south of the Plan Area, provides  
both local and express (Baby  
Bullet) train service. SamTrans 
and  several employee and 
residential   
shuttle services operate at the  
Hillsdale Station.  
SamTrans 
SamTrans Bus Routes 292 and 35 
run along South Delaware Street,  
providing direct bus service to  
the Plan Area. This route operates  
between Hillsdale Shopping  
Center and downtown San  
Francisco. 
Road Network 
The Plan Area is surrounded  
by readily accessible local  
arterials and major highways.  
The Plan accounts for possible  
reconfiguration of the SR 92  
interchange with Concar Drive, as  
well as the City’s plans to narrow  
South Delaware Street. 
•  State Route 92 (SR 92):  Just  
south of Concar Drive, SR 92  is 
a four- to six-lane, east-west  
freeway that extends from Half  
Moon Bay in west San Mateo  
County across the San Francisco  
Bay via the San Mateo Bridge  
to Hayward in Alameda County.  
Prior to intersecting US 101 to  
the east, SR 92 is four lanes. East  
of US 101, SR 92 becomes six  
lanes. 
•  Concar Drive:  This two- to four-
lane, east-west arterial borders  
the Plan Area to the south. SR 92  
westbound on- and off- ramps  
are located roughly at the north-
south access to the Plan Area at  
Concar Drive. 
•  South Delaware Street:  This  
is a two- to four-lane (with  an 
additional turning lane),  north-
south arterial bordering  the 
eastern edge of the Plan  Area. 
Eastbound SR 92 on- and  off- 
ramps are located at the  SR 
92/South Delaware Street  
interchange, south of the South  
Delaware Street/Concar Drive  
intersection and the Plan Area. 
•  East 16th Avenue:  Located  
north of the Plan Area, this is a  
four-lane collector which runs  
east-west between US 101 and  
the Caltrain tracks. 
•  US Highway 101 (US 101):  This  
is an eight-lane, north-south  
freeway less than one-half mile  
from the Plan Area. US 101  
extends northward through San  
Francisco and across the Golden  
Gate Bridge, and southward  
through San Jose toward Morgan 
Hill. Access to the Plan Area is  
provided via an interchange at  
SR 92.  
25 
Parking 
The Plan Area currently includes  
approximately 700 parking spaces  
in over nine acres of surface lots  
to serve the Kmart and Michaels  
retail stores. Separated from these 
parking lots by a chain-linked 
fence are approximately 200 
spaces at the Joint Powers  Board 
property to the west. 
No on-street parking is currently  
permitted along the Plan Area  
frontage. On-street parking of a  
half-dozen vehicles is allowed on  
the south side of Concar Drive  
west of the SR 92 westbound on-  
and off- ramps.

--- page 216 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
26 
San Mateo is situated on the San 
Francisco Peninsula, between  
the Santa Cruz Mountains and  
San Francisco Bay. Because it is 
positioned on the Bay side of  
the Peninsula, the City has all  
the typical physical and climatic  
features of lower-lying areas  
around the Bay. 
Climate 
The San Francisco Bay Area 
has drastically varying micro-
climates.  San Mateo’s location 
affords it a very mild climate. 
The average high temperature is 
roughly 65 degrees Fahrenheit, 
varying  from 55 degrees in 
January to 75 degrees in August. 
Average low temperatures range 
from 40 degrees to 55 degrees. 
The San Francisco Bay Area  
experiences distinct rainy and 
dry seasons. The rainy season 
generally occurs between  
October and April, with a total 
annual average rainfall of 19 
inches per year measured  
at nearby San Francisco  
International Airport. The 
dry season generally occurs 
between May and September 
with generally negligible rainfall.  
The general exposure of the Plan 
Area to prevailing winds, sun, noise 
from SR 92 and Caltrain, and storm 
drainage is shown in Figure 2.18.  
Topography 
The Plan Area’s existing high point  
is located near its center, at the  
finished floor level of the Kmart  
building, at approximately +107 feet 
(adjusted City datum). The Michaels  
store is located southeast of Kmart,  
and has a finished floor elevation  
of approximately +105 feet. From  
its center the Plan Area slopes  
downward in all directions toward its 
boundaries. The highest boundary  
elevation is along the western edge  
adjacent to the Joint Powers Board  
property, at an elevation of +106.2  
feet. The lowest boundary elevation  
is along the northeastern edge  
adjacent to South Delaware Street, 
at  +100.7 feet. 
Air Quality 
According to the California  
Environmental Protection  
Agency’s Air Quality and Land Use  
Handbook (April 2005), freeways  
and major arterial roads within  
1,000 feet upwind of residential  
developments are a source of  
air pollution that result in higher  
than average adverse health  
impacts. As shown in Figure 2.18,  
although the Plan Area is within  
1,000 feet of SR 92, the freeway  
is downwind, not upwind, of the  
Plan Area. Thus, this potential air-
quality issue is minimized. 
 
Soil 
At present, the Plan Area is almost 
entirely covered with buildings  
and solid pavement. Previously,  
the Kmart building was used  
by Montgomery Ward, another  
general retailer. The Michaels  
building was once an automobile  
service center. The third existing  
land use within the Plan Area 
is  a Shell gas station located at 
the  corner of Concar Drive and 
South  Delaware Street. A Phase 
I Environmental Assessment  
for the Kmart and Shell station  
properties (EarthTech, Inc., July  
2006) recommended no further  
investigation. 
The existing asphalt pavement  
that covers the Plan Area is  of 
varying thickness, averaging  
two inches where vehicles 
pass. There are some small 
landscaped areas, mainly 
islands of plantings scattered 
throughout the parking lot. 
Beneath the pavement is a 
layer of artificial fill that varies 
between 1.5- and six-feet thick.  
The classification of artificial fill is 
highly variable, containing poorly 
graded gravel, clayey gravel, 
poorly graded sand, silty sand, or 
clayey sand. Below the artificial 
fill is Bay Mud, which  varies from 
one-foot thick near  the western 
boundary of the Plan  Area to six-
feet thick near South  Delaware 
Street. The upper one to two feet 
of Bay Mud is highly organic and 
contains decaying vegetation. 
Below the Bay Mud, a medium-
stiff, lean clay layer covers 
the majority of the Plan Area.  
Alluvium was also found below 
the Bay Mud. Below the stiff-
lean clay, the alluvium deposit 
is mainly silt or clay with sand 
and gravel. Small zones of 
predominantly sand or gravel 
were also encountered. The depth 
to bedrock is unknown.

Infrastructure and Utilities  
The Plan Area contains a shallow  
groundwater table located above  
the Bay Mud material that varies  
from +96 to +103 feet (adjusted  
City datum). This groundwater 
table  fluctuates seasonally, and is 
probably  controlled by water levels 
in Marina  Lagoon and by seasonal 
rainfall. A  second groundwater 
zone may occur  in the Plan Area 
approximately 17 to  30 feet 
underground. If penetrated,  the 
static level of this confined  aquifer 
is approximately five feet  below 
existing grade.  
The Plan Area is not within an  
Earthquake Fault Zone, as 
defined  by the Alquist-Priolo 
Earthquake  Fault Zoning Act, and 
no known  active faults exist there. 
In a  seismically active area such 
as  Northern California, the remote  
possibility exists for future faulting  
in areas where no faults previously  
existed; however, the risk of surface  
faulting and consequent secondary  
ground failure is very low. (Source:  
San Mateo Corridor Plan and Bay  
Meadows Specific Plan 
Amendment  EIR 4.13-6.)  
The Plan Area lies within a part  
of San Mateo that has been  
developed for many decades, and 
it  is well served by infrastructure 
and utilities. 
Storm Drainage 
The Plan Area falls entirely 
within  the tributary area of 
Leslie Creek  channel (formerly 
known as the  16th Avenue 
Channel). Leslie  Creek 
channel flows northeasterly  to 
Marina Lagoon, which, in turn,  
discharges to San Francisco 
Bay.  The Marina Lagoon water 
surface  elevation is controlled 
by the City.  There are no offsite 
flows through  or onto the Plan 
Area. 
At present, the Plan Area is 
almost  entirely covered by 
impermeable  surfaces, including 
building roofs  and over nine 
acres of asphalt  parking lots. 
Approximately six  percent of the 
Plan Area consists  of permeable, 
vegetated surfaces,  in the form 
of parking lot islands.  Rainfall is 
captured on-site and  conveyed 
directly to the City storm  drains.  
The Plan Area has three distinct,  
on-site drainage systems, shown  
in Figure 2.19. These are the  
Western, Northern, and Eastern  
systems: 
•  Western system:  This captures  
and conveys flows from the  
parking lot to the west of the  
existing Kmart building. Catch  
basins located along the Joint  
Powers Board property line  
connect to an 18-inch storm  
drain located in Concar Drive. 
•  Northern system:  The roof drain  
from the existing Kmart building  
connects to a catch basin located  
at the southwestern end of  
Garvey Way. This catch basin  
drains a significant proportion  of 
the parking lot north of the  Kmart 
building, and it discharges  to an 
18-inch City storm drain,  which, 
in turn, discharges to the  Leslie 
Creek channel. 
•  Eastern system:  This system  
captures flows from the parking  
lots to the south and east of  the 
Kmart building via a series  of 
catch basins. It connects to a  
39-inch City storm drain in South  
Delaware Street at Charles Lane.  
Using a 51-inch pipe, this storm  
drain, in turn, connects to the  
Leslie Creek channel outfall.  
The 18-inch City storm drain in  
Concar Drive conveys flows from  
the south of the Plan Area to  the 
intersection of Concar Drive  and 
South Delaware Street, just  beyond 
the southeastern point of  the Plan 
Area. The Shell station  connects 
directly to this 18-inch  City storm 
drain in Concar Drive. 
The 39-inch South Delaware  
Street storm drain conveys  run-off 
from a small watershed  between 
Concar Drive and SR  92. The 
South Delaware Street  storm drain 
outfalls directly to  Leslie Creek 
channel in a 12-foot  by 6-foot box 
culvert that allows  Leslie Creek 
channel to flow  under South 
Delaware Street.

Sewers 
Collection and treatment of  
wastewater in the Plan Area is  
provided by the City. As shown  
in Figure 2.20, existing facilities  
serving the Plan Area include: 
33” Delaware Trunk 
This trunk sewer line runs along  
the eastern edge of the Plan  
Area in South Delaware Street. It  
collects flows from properties and  
areas adjacent to South Delaware  
Street, including the 845-acre  
San Mateo Village watershed  
catchment, Fiesta Gardens, the  
San Mateo County Exposition  
Center, the Bay Meadows Phase  
One redevelopment, and the  
City’s Mongini and Santa Clara lift  
stations. The trunk line discharges  
to the Dale Avenue Pump Station,  
from which the flow is pumped  
to the San Mateo Wastewater  
Treatment Plant.

CHAPTER 2 EXISTING CONDITIONS 
El Camino Real By-Pass 
In 2001 the City commissioned  
the City of San Mateo City-
Wide Sewer System Study  
which indicated that there is  
a significant capacity deficit 
in  all of the major trunks in 
the  southern trunk system due 
largely to high infiltration and 
inflow. Accordingly, a bypass 
from the El Camino Real trunk 
sewer line was constructed 
in recent years along Concar 
Drive, to alleviate surcharge 
of the El Camino Real trunk 
during storm events. This  
30-inch sewer line connects 
to the South Delaware Street 
trunk line at the intersection of 
Concar Drive. 
Plan Area 
Within the Plan Area itself, an 
8-inch line collects sanitary 
sewer flows from the existing 
Kmart and Michaels sites, 
and connects to the 30-inch 
El Camino Real bypass line in 
Concar Drive.  
Water 
Water service for the Plan Area  
is provided by the California  
Water Service Company 
(CWSC). CWSC obtains its water 
from  the San Francisco Public 
Utilities Commission (SFPUC) 
through connections in the 
northwest part of the City of 
San Mateo. Water distribution 
mains in the Plan Area include a 
10-inch and 8-inch main in South 
Delaware Street, an eight-inch 
main in Concar Drive, an eight-
inch main in Garvey Way, and 
a 24-inch high-pressure main 
in South Delaware Street. A 
12-inch water main, branching 
from the 10-inch South Delaware 
Street main, serves the Plan 
Area by looping around the 
Kmart building. The loop 
consists of a 12-inch pipe along 
the southern side of the building 
and an eight-inch pipe along 
the north. The eight-inch main 
in Garvey Way extends south 
into the property to intercept 
the building loop, creating a 
secondary loop.  
Electricity and Gas 
Pacific Gas and Electric Company  
(PG&E) provides electrical and 
natural gas service to the Plan 
Area. 
Electrical service is supplied by  
both overhead and underground  
lines. Low-voltage electrical lines  
are fixed to overhead utility poles  
that are located along the entire  
length of Concar Drive adjacent to  
the Plan Area. The service feeds to  
the existing buildings in the Plan  
Area are located underground. 
A two-inch natural gas line is  
located underneath the sidewalk on  
the northern side of Concar Drive  
and along the western edge of  
South Delaware Street. In addition,  
a high-pressure, 24-inch gas line 
is located in the center of South 
Delaware Street.

Chapter 3 
Guiding Principles 
By building upon a basic set of design principles,  this 
Plan establishes a vision of how Station Park  Green will 
look, feel, and operate when it is  completed. These 
basic principles range from ideas  about land use and 
built form, through vehicular,  bicycle and pedestrian 
access assumptions, to  ecological goals. The principles 
were established  based on existing conditions and 
constraints, such  as the Plan Area’s unique physical 
characteristics and  economic realities, as well as 
normative values of  placemaking.

37 
Implement the Corridor Plan 
This Plan further develops and expresses the objectives and policies of the Corridor  
Plan. Indeed, the central objective of this Plan is to implement the vision of that ear-
lier document, by enabling the development of a public-spirited and transit-oriented  
neighborhood. In this way, Station Park Green is envisioned as a place worthy of the  
Hayward Park Caltrain Station, the existing residential neighborhoods that surround  
the Plan Area, and the City of San Mateo as a whole. 
The vision of Station Park Green, established by this Plan, will realize the vision of the  
Corridor Plan within the Plan Area. The objectives and policies of the Corridor Plan  
are applied throughout, including:  
•   An interconnected street system  
that integrates with San Mateo’s  
surrounding residential fabric  
(Policy 4.1 and 6.32) 
•   Multi-family uses developed  
at transit-supportive densities  
(Policy 5.9) 
•   Diversity in architecture, building  
types, and unit types (Policies  
6.12 and 6.30 ) 
•   Fine-grained massing and  
articulation of buildings,  
constructed close to sidewalks  
(Policies 6.15, 6.16, and 6.33) 
•   Neighborhood- and commuter-
serving retail uses, including  
specialty uses that enhance  
neighborhood services (Policies  
5.12 and 5.13)  
•   Height limitations of 35 feet along  
South Delaware Street and 55 feet  
elsewhere (Policies 5.14 and 5.15).  
Height shall be measured per  
pages 66 and 67 of this Plan. 
•   Street designs that emphasize  
pedestrian comfort and safety  
while calming traffic (Policies 4.6, 
4.8, 6.4, 6.5, and 6.32) 
•   One-way “Park Streets”  that allow  
auto circulation around parks while  
minimizing pedestrian crossing  
distances (Policy 4.11) 
•   Placement and orientation of  
the park along the major visual  
and pedestrian link to the station  
(Policies 4.16 and 5.10)
•  Water-quality protection and  
environmental responsibility  
integrated into streetscapes,  
buildings, parking, and open  
space (Policies 6.11 and 6.35) 
•  Design of new buildings to  
be pedestrian-friendly and  
compatible with local styles  
    (Policy 6.2, 6.19, 6.30, 6.33, and  
6.34) 
•  Does not significantly increase  
traffic levels on residential streets  
in existing neighborhoods (Policy  
4.3, 4.12 and 7.11)

Consider the Context 
Station Park Green is not merely located within San Mateo but will be an integral part  
of the City. Its design reflects careful consideration of adjacent neighborhoods, the  
existing street network, ongoing urban planning efforts, and available transportation  
resources. The Plan does this by:  
•	 Providing for new 
neighborhood-serving retail 
uses. 
  
•	 Designing a street network that  
reflects San Mateo’s existing  
residential grid and integrates  
with existing streets 
  
•	 Ensuring residential densities  
and intensities of retail and 
office uses appropriate to the 
area’s  proximity to Caltrain
 
•	 Planning for seamless potential  
future expansion of the Plan  
Area to include the Joint 
Powers Board, AAA, and U.S. 
Postal Service properties  
 
•	 Reducing building heights  
along South Delaware Street to  
present a form compatible with  
the Nineteenth Avenue Park  
neighborhood 
•	 Locating the greatest building  
heights and use densities 
toward the southern and 
southwestern portions of the 
Plan Area, to mitigate traffic 
noise and prevailing westerly 
winds

Lead with Landscape  
The framework for Station Park Green is its network of landscaped, publicly accessible  
open spaces. In turn, the key to that open space network is an approximately 1.1-acre  
park at the very heart of the neighborhood, called “The Park.” The Park will extend out 
through the streets connected by the Gateway Park and Community Park. Together 
with the tree-lined streets within the Plan Area, these elements form a network of open 
spaces that comprises the public realm for Station Park Green. The Plan ensures the 
central importance and high quality of the public realm by:  
•  Setting The Park as the defining  
feature for Station Park Green,  
visible at its heart and from  
adjoining streets, and inviting  
enjoyment by residents, neighbors 
and station users alike
 •  Connecting Concar Drive to 
Garvey Way through a continuous 
set of green linear parks, hosting 
a variety of uses, from passive 
strolling to active play
•   Extending the influence 
of The  Park into all of the 
neighborhood’s  public spaces 
through related  materials and 
design treatments,  following 
City-approved guidelines   
•  Providing a more intimate 
courtyards  for local residents, 
with a more serene character 
befitting their location and scale 
•  Planning for the continuation 
of the emerging regional trail  
system through the Hayward  
Park Station TOD Zone  
 
•   Linking the publicly accessible  
open space network to and  
integrating it with private 
stoops,  porches, mews, and 
gardens  within the Plan Area  
 CHAPTER 3 GUIDING PRINCIPLES

--- page 233 ---
43 
Connect the Station to   
the Neighborhood  
Station Park Green presents an opportunity to reintegrate the station area, which 
is currently disrupted by auto-dominated uses, with the urban fabric around it. As a 
contingency, the Plan provides for an Emergency Vehicle Access Easement (EVAE) 
along the Plan Area boundary with the JPB property. However, the preferred approach 
would be to develop both properties together seamlessly, with active uses facing 
the Caltrain Station. The development of a vital, pedestrian-oriented neighborhood 
will facilitate station access for both new and existing residents, with clear, direct 
connections drawing  people to and through the Plan Area. The Plan does this by:  
•   Facilitating clear, direct 
pedestrian  and visual 
connections to the  Hayward 
Park Station, particularly  from 
Concar Drive and Charles  Lane 
•   Providing a densely 
interconnected  network for 
pedestrian circulation,  including 
generous sidewalks,  frequent 
crossings, and through-block 
pathways  
•   Using traffic-calming techniques  
to reduce vehicular speeds in the  
neighborhood 
•   Creating a street network that  
connects efficiently to existing  
streets, from Concar Drive in  
alignment with the SR 92 off-
ramp and from South Delaware  
Street in alignment with Charles  
Lane of Nineteenth Avenue Park  
•   Providing retail and services  
for commuters using the train  
station and nearby residents 
•   Parking will primarily be 
enclosed with retail and 
residential uses, to maximize the  
pedestrian orientation of streets  
and open space

Create a Vital Center 
Station Park Green will become a vibrant hub of activity where asphalt dominates today.  
Added activities and amenities will create a new meeting ground for commuters, 
residents, and shoppers. The Plan does this by:  
•   Providing a new transit-oriented  
neighborhood near the Hayward  
Park Station for the shared  
enjoyment of surrounding  
residents 
•   Concentrating density near the  
Hayward Park Station and around  
a large park and the larger streets 
•   Including a range of housing  
types, such as studios, 1-, 2-, and 
3-bedroom units, in  addition to 
retail and some office  uses, at 
densities sufficient to  support 
both transit ridership  and 
neighborhood vitality 
•   Drawing visitors to and through  
the neighborhood with clear  
connections and comfortable  
and attractive streets and open  
spaces 
•   Locating parking to avoid  
interference with building  
entrances, retail spaces, and  
pedestrian access points 
 
•   Providing for uses and  
activities that will keep the new  
neighborhood active into the  
evenings and weekends 
•   Orienting buildings toward  
streets and parks to provide  
emphasis and spatial definition  
to the public realm  
•   Creating and supporting a  
distinctive visual identity through 
excellent architectural, urban,  
and landscape design

--- page 237 ---
47 
Design for Sustainability 
Station Park Green exemplifies a sustainable pattern of development, which creates  
compact, diverse, vibrant places. It recycles underutilized land near existing transit  
facilities, converting an auto-dominated area of asphalt into a community-oriented 
place around people. By providing more services and amenities near mass transit, 
the  Plan facilitates access by proximity, reducing the need for travel and supporting  
transit ridership. Careful planning allows a thorough and proactive consideration  
of environmental factors from stormwater management to energy efficiency to  
transportation. The Plan is compatible with LEED for Neighborhood Development 
(LEED-ND) certification. The U.S. Green Building Council’s LEED-ND program 
emphasizes the creation of vibrant, walkable, mixed-use communities that reduce the 
impact of urban sprawl and contribute to healthier living. The Plan furthers the following 
sustainable practices:  
•   Recycling underused, auto-
dominated land 
•   Concentrating diverse activities  
close to one another 
•   Developing densities near mass  
transit—encouraging transit use  
by proximity 
•   Managing transportation  
demand through amenities that  
support transit use  
•   Facilitating safe, comfortable  
station access by pedestrians  and 
bicyclists 
•   Providing bicycle parking and  
addressing bicycle circulation in  
street design 
•   Incorporating the emerging  
     regional trail network  
 
•   Addressing solar access and wind 
impacts through sensitive site 
design 
•   Integrating stormwater  
management into site design 
•   Incorporating green building 
systems, materials, and  
technology wherever feasible

Chapter 4 
Development Standards 
Development of Station Park Green comes with both 
opportunity and responsibility. The opportunity is to 
deliver to the City a new mixed-use development that 
in its design and management is a proud addition 
to the family of remarkable living experiences in San 
Mateo. At the same time, there is a responsibility to 
respect and fully utilize the scarce resource of public 
transit accessibility, by clustering attractive, intense 
development at transit stations. San Mateo has 
identified only a few locations for such transit-oriented 
developments, and sets them out as showcases for 
good urban living.

Overview 
The development detailed in this  
chapter seizes the opportunities  
of the Plan Area through bold and  
distinctive design characterized by: 
•   A pattern of walkable blocks, 
narrow streets, and housing  
diversity 
•   An assurance of shared 
community amenities, most 
dramatically represented by The 
Park at the heart of the Plan Area 
and enhanced by Gateway Park 
and Community Park.   
•   A dedication to transit  
connectivity—providing shuttle  
service to downtown 
•   A focus on living experiences—an  
invitation to neighbors, a park  
down every block and outside  
most windows, and attractive  
routes for transit riders 
•   A commitment to get the cars 
“out of the living room” by 
requiring that above-ground 
parking be enclosed with active 
uses. This allows attractive front 
doors along nearly every corridor  
The sections that follow 
describe the development and 
design approach  through text, 
diagrams, illustrative  plans, and 
renderings, including:  
•      Concepts — key design  
foundations of the Plan 
•      Station Engagement — 
seamless  integration and 
development 
•      Regulatory Context—existing  
policies, laws, and regulations 
•      Block Pattern and 
Open Space—the basic  
framework for streets and  
connectivity, including an  
alternative configuration 
•      Land Use and Density—
land  uses, intensity of use, 
residential  density, and 
dwelling-unit types 
•      Heights and Typologies— 
designated building envelopes 
and characteristics 
•      Open Space System—design  
intent and standards for open  
space 
•  	 Black Standards— use and 
dimensional standards by 
block
52 
•      Streets and Circulation— 
streets and streetscape from a  
transportation perspective 
•      Infrastructure Improvements— 
stormwater, sewer, water,  
emergency services, other  
utilities, grading, and waste 
and  recycling

Concept 2 :  
Getting to Transit 
Open linkages to and from  the 
Hayward Park Caltrain  Station 
will facilitate easy  access to 
transit for the Plan  Area and the 
surrounding  neighborhoods. 
This diagram  uses blue arrows 
to show how  the east-west 
pedestrian and  vehicular routes 
pass through  the Plan Area 
along park  spaces (shown in 
green), and  lead toward the

at the Joint Powers  Board 
property adjacent to the station.

part of the Plan Area, interim  
connectivity is provided along 
Concar Drive and an EVAE is 
provided along the boundary  
between the two properties.  
The EVAE is intended solely  
as a contingency should  
coordinated development  of 
the two properties be  infeasible 
or alternative access  is not 
possible.

Concept 3 :  
A Green Network 
The Park, located in the center,  
is one of multiple open spaces in 
the Plan Area, including Gateway 
Park, Community Park, and other, 
smaller open spaces, all shown 
in darker green. This series 
of linked publicly accessible 
open spaces are connected 
by a network of high-quality, 
landscaped streets—including  
sidewalks—which are shown in 
lighter green.

center, this network of parks and 
streets will create a common 
identity and  experience for the 
entire  Plan Area. The landscape 
of the streets will unify the 
neighborhood, benefiting  every 
block, so that every  street 
feels like an extension  of 
The Park. The streets will also 
integrate a system of stormwater 
management strategies.

The figures in this Plan show

Plan  Area, along the boundary 
of  the adjacent JPB property. It 
is  the ultimate goal of this Plan to
