with the adjacent JPB property  
by providing for direct adjacency  
of buildings and the connection  
of vehicular, bike and pedestrian 
ways. Seamless integration and 
direct connection between these 
two properties will facilitate and  
improve the circulation for the 
entire Hayward Park TOD Station 
Zone. 
Integration of the properties  
may include the placement of  
buildings, as well as pedestrian  
and bikeway amenities fronting  
directly onto the JPB property.  
The location of street, pedestrian  
and bikeway amenities could  
straddle the Plan Area and the  
JPB property. However, at this 
time it is not known how the  
Plan Area and the JPB property  
will ultimately interface, so the  
EVAE is shown as a necessary  
contingency, providing the 
required fire safety access for 
the Plan Area solely within 
its boundaries. The interface 
of the Plan Area and the JPB 
property will be determined 
during SPAR for Blocks RE-2 
and RE-3. 
As stated above, the  
development standards in  
this chapter apply only to 
the Plan Area. However, as 
noted  throughout this Plan, 
the ultimate design intent 
for the Hayward  Park Station 
TOD Zone is for the Plan Area 
and the JPB property to work 
together seamlessly through 
coordinated access ways, view 
corridors, an active network of 
streets and open spaces, and 
attention to building design and 
use along pedestrian routes, 
to achieve a successful transit 
oriented neighborhood to  
facilitate pedestrian and bicycle 
traffic across the JPB property  
to and from all portions of the 
Hayward Park Station Area, 
and to increase use of transit 
services.

58 
Land Use   
Requirements 
This Plan implements objectives  
and policies of the Corridor  
Plan, which establish a land use  
program that supports a new  
transit-oriented village within the  
Plan Area. To guide the process  
of transit-oriented development,  
the Corridor Plan introduced  
the Hayward Park Station TOD  
Overlay Zone. The TOD Zone  
encompasses all the property  
covered by this Plan as well  
as some adjacent properties,  
including the JPB property. 
This Plan applies the policies for 
the Hayward Park Station TOD 
Overlay Zone set out in the  
Corridor Plan. Although existing  
land uses may remain in the  
TOD Zone, new development is  
highly encouraged to be transit-
oriented, allowing new land uses,  
higher densities, lower parking  
requirements, and transit- and  
pedestrian-supportive design  
standards.  
The TOD Zone allows residential  
uses, office, retail shops, and  
services. Civic uses, including  
publicly accessible open space  
areas and multi-modal transit  
facilities and access ways, are also 
permitted. Retail uses should be, for 
the most part, neighborhood- and 
commuter-serving (such as small 
grocery stores, personal services, 
shops, or restaurants), and should 
be convenience oriented, providing  
goods and services within easy  
walking distance for residents and  
commuters alike. Community-serving 
retail use is permitted on the ground 
floor in the blocks along the Concar 
Drive frontage.  
Requirements  
Residential Density    
The Plan Area lies within the  
“Station Area” of the TOD Zone,  
which has minimum as well as  
maximum density requirements.  
In the Station Area, the minimum  
residential density is thirty-five (35)  
dwelling units per acre and the  
maximum density is fifty (50) units  
per acre.  
FAR   
Requirements 
Recognizing the extraordinary  
resource of adjacent transit, the 
Corridor Plan calls for high density 
within the Hayward Park TOD Zone. 
The maximum, overall FAR in the 
Station Area is 3.0. Retail uses have a 
maximum FAR of 0.3 across the Station 
Area. 
Height Limits 
A maximum height of 55 feet  applies 
to the majority of the Plan Area. The 
exception is a 45-foot deep strip along 
the length of South Delaware Street, 
which has a height limit of 35 feet. 
Height will be measured as described 
on pages 67 and 68 of this Specific  
Plan. The 55-foot height zone is 
intended to meet the needs of transit-
supportive development densities, 
anticipating four- or five-story, multi-
family and/or mixed-use residential 
buildings, with enclosed parking.  
Regulatory Context

--- page 249 ---
FIGURE 4.06 Block pattern and open space
59 
Block Pattern  
and Open Space
The blocks within the Plan  
Area are defined by a grid of 
narrow streets that will be privately 
maintained, but  publicly accessible 
(Figure 4.06). The planned street grid  
corresponds to the surrounding  
neighborhood grids in both  scale and 
orientation. The grid  is oriented to 
minimize the effect of prevailing west 
winds. The block pattern and street 
network achieve a high degree  of 
connectivity by linking access  points 
with intersections at the  edges of the 
Plan Area. 
Three vehicular access points  connect 
with pre-existing  intersections:  the South 
Delaware Street entrance mirrors  the 
Charles Lane entrance to the Nineteenth 
Avenue Park neighborhood; the main 
Concar Drive entrance aligns with the  
ramp to SR 92; and, on the northern edge 
of the Plan Area, Garvey Way is extended 
into the Plan Area, along the Community 
Park. As a contingency, an EVAE is planned 
along the westerly boundary with the JPB 
property.  
 CHAPTER 4 DEVELOPMENT STANDARDS

--- page 250 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
Figure 4.07 Illustrative site plan   
Illustrative Site Plan 
The Plan includes four blocks. One 
will allow a mixed-use residential 
building (MU), and three will be 
residential (RE). There will also be 
eight areas that will be dedicated  as 
publicly accessible open space (OS). 
As shown in Figure 4.07, the blocks 
on the south side of The Park are 
deeper than those on the north side. 
This slight asymmetry around The 
Park responds to the environmental 
setting. Thus, Blocks MU-1 and RE-2  
provide greater distance between 
The Park (OS-5) and Concar Drive, 
buffering The Park from the noise 
and appearance of SR 92 as it rises 
over the Caltrain right-of-way. This 
asymmetry also allows the South 
Delaware Street frontage of Block 
MU-1 to hold the street wall on 
the corner of what the Corridor 
Plan establishes as an important 
intersection.

Land Use and Density  
Land Use 
The Plan Area comprises a  
combination of uses including  
publicly accessible open space, 
all-residential buildings, and 
a mixed-use building with 
residential units above non-
residential space. This section 
details the land uses by block 
(Figure 4.08). Each of the four 
blocks has been designated 
in Table 4.01 on page 64 with 
permitted (and preferred)  
land uses, in some cases with  
prioritized locations. 
The land use plan for the Plan  
Area fulfills the requirements of  
the Corridor Plan, and advances  
urban design and planning  
principles that will create a  
high-quality, vibrant, pedestrian-
oriented neighborhood that is  
respectful of surrounding uses. 
As shown in Figure 4.08, the  
interface of the Plan Area with  
the JPB property is intended to  
be seamless. However, an EVAE  
separating the two areas has been 
planned as a contingency.  
The land use plan: 
•  Permits residential uses in every 
block 
•  Concentrates commercial office 
space along Concar Drive, 
consistent with the other office 
uses on Concar Drive  
•  Concentrates retail uses along 
South Delaware Street and  
Concar Drive, and at park 
frontages, minimizing impacts 
to existing residences in the 
surrounding neighborhoods 
•  Priority Retail Locations: Identifies 
four locations in Block MU-1 
where retail is prioritized.  
•  Uses open space as an essential 
public amenity that gives the Plan 
Area its identity 
•  Organizes open space to create 
connections to transit, welcome  
residents from surrounding  
neighborhoods, and enhance the  
setting for ground-floor retail 
•  Extends a network of open space 
uses to improve views, light and  
air throughout the neighborhood

STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
Program 
Based upon the land use, density,  
and FAR parameters of the  
Corridor Plan, the development  
program for the Plan Area at full  
build-out includes: 
•   Up to 599 dwelling units (50 du/ 
acre), including Below Market  
Rate units. 
•   A minimum of 25,000 gross  
square feet (gsf) of Retail/
Services/Restaurant uses, and a 
minimum of 10,000 gsf of Office  
use. 
•   Approximately 2.3 acres of  
publicly accessible open space  
(excludes publicly accessible 
streets).  
Retail/Services/Restaurant uses  
are defined to include all such  
uses permitted under San Mateo  
Municipal Code Chapter 27.30  
(C1 Districts - Neighborhood  
Commercial), with the following  
conditions:  
•   At least 5,000 square feet of 
Retail/Services/Restaurant uses 
shall be neighborhood-serving 
Retail/Services/Restaurant 
uses, with a maximum size per 
establishment of 1,500 square 
feet.

Neighborhood-Serving Retail  
The neighborhood-serving 
retail uses in the Plan Area will  
align with the neighborhood  
commercial districts (C1) zoning  
in San Mateo as well as with the  
principles and objectives in the  
Corridor Plan. These retail uses  
are intended for neighborhood  
shopping that is compatible with  
surrounding residential areas. Per  
the Corridor Plan, they should be: 
•   Located in the ground floor of a 
mixed-use building, rather than  
as stand-alone retail 
•   Smaller-scale and 
convenience-oriented shops 
and services 
•   Within easy walking distance for 
the immediate neighborhood  
and transit users 
•   Built to the sidewalk to bring  
vitality to the street 
Examples of such retail uses  
include, but are not limited to:   
bakeries, delicatessens, book  
stores, grocery stores, drug  
stores, restaurants, outdoor  
dining areas, antique shops,  
apparel stores, hardware and  
garden supply stores, household  
furnishings, pet shops, barber 
shops and hair salons, dry  
cleaners, banks, fitness studios,  
health spas, photo processing  
and framing shops, real estate  
establishments, tailoring and  
repair stores, travel agencies,  
and parking, recycling, and  
community facilities.

Residential 
Density  
As required under the Corridor  
Plan, the minimum residential  
density for the Plan Area is 35  
dwelling units per acre and 
the  maximum is 50 dwelling 
units  per acre. Allocation 
of density  from one block 
to another within  the Plan 
Area is permitted (San Mateo 
Municipal Code, Section  
27.02.160). Therefore, density  
shall be calculated across the  
entire Plan Area.   
As shown in Figure 4.1 Relative 
Residential Density Diagram,  
residential density is intended  to 
be compatible with surrounding 
conditions. Instead,  the 
Plan allocates density to be  
compatible with surrounding  
conditions. The Residential  
Density Diagram indicates  four 
gradations of density. The streets 
and open space blocks do not 
have dwelling units. For the 
blocks, density is lowest along 
South Delaware Street.
More information on the ranges  
of residential units permitted by  
block is provided in the Block 
Standards in Chapter 4.

FAR 
Because the entire street system  
within the Plan Area will be  
privately built and maintained, it 
has been included in “lot area“  
for purposes of calculating overall 
FAR for the Plan Area. As shown  
in Figure 4.15, the overall FAR for  
the Plan Area is 3.0. Retail FAR is 
limited to 0.2, and office FAR is 
capped at 0.1. 
More information on the ranges  
of floor area by block is provided 
in the Block Standards on pages  
83-86.

Heights and Typologies   
Heights 
The Plan requires lower 
building heights along South 
Delaware Street in response to 
the Nineteenth Avenue Park  
neighborhood. Figure 4.16a 
shows  building heights by 
block.  
Heights along South Delaware  
Street, in Blocks MU-1 and RE-
4  within the 35-feet Maximum  
Height Zone, will be 25-feet  
to 35-feet tall. This approach  
maintains a comparable scale  
of development adjacent 
to Nineteenth Avenue Park. 
Buildings 35-feet to 55-feet 
high are permitted in the rest 
of the blocks. These heights 
will enable the density needed 
to support transit and retail 
uses, as well as protect open 
spaces against freeway and 
Caltrain noise and the visual 
mass of the elevated freeway.  
Except for areas in Blocks 
MU-1 and RE-4 withinthe 35-
foot Maximum Height Zone 
along South Delaware Street, 
building heights shall be 
measured from the Plan Area’s 
existing highest elevation (+ 
107 feet) to the primary roof 
plateline.  (See Figures 4.16b, 
4.16d, and 4.16e.)  For the 
35-foot Maximum Height Zone 
along South Delaware Street, 
height shall be the vertical 
distance measured from 
104.7 feet to the primary roof 
plateline.   (See Figures 4.16c 
and 4.16e.)

Measure P 
Measure P, approved in  
November 2004, amended Policy  
1.5 of the General Plan, requiring  
the City Council to make 
five  findings for General Plan  
amendments involving building  
height changes: 
1.  The building has high design  
quality, which is enhanced by  
additional building height; 
2.  Increased building heights are  
visually related to surrounding  
building heights and promote  
the creation of a coherent City  
image; 
3.  Increased building heights  
will still provide for a variety of  
building heights in the vicinity of  
the project and the surrounding  
areas; 
4.  Increased building heights are  
compatible with surrounding  
land uses, and will not create  
adverse shadow or visual impacts  
on surrounding residential uses;  
and 
5.  The City’s infrastructure is  
adequate to accommodate the  
proposed Plan program.  
With the June 2005 adoption of  
the Corridor Plan, the City Council  
adopted amendments to the  
General Plan establishing a new  
TOD land use category, modifying  
certain allowable heights and  
densities to the TOD category.   
These amendments resulted in  
changes to the allowable heights  
and densities in the Plan Area.

69 
Pedestrian Access at 
Ground Level 
Pedestrian access at ground level 
will activate the open spaces of 
the plan, softening the edges 
of the building with varied 
textures and forms at the street 
level.  Access at street level will 
take on three forms in response 
to context, program, and 
form: lobbies, unit stoops, and 
storefront:   
•   Lobbies will serve the whole 
building, creating access to key 
open spaces and highlighting 
important site connections. 
•   Residential stoops will allow 
for open circulation for ground 
floor units and increase the 
permeability of the building 
edge.   
•  Where allowed by grade and 
landscape setback, stoops will be 
utilized to activate park frontages
•  Storefront access will take on an 
urban form, allowing for visual 
and physical interaction between 
the streetscape and retail/office 
spaces.
•  Where grade does not allow for 
residential stoops, landscape will 
be used to soften the building 
edge and to provide a buffer 
from the street. 
FIGURE 4.17 Pedestrian Access at Ground Level

70  
Publicly accessible neighborhood  
parks are central to the design 
of the Plan Area. The existence  
of three large open spaces—
The Park, Community Park, and 
Gateway Park—will establish the 
identity of this new district. The 
feel of these new open spaces will 
reach out to South Delaware Street 
and Concar Drive and invite the 
public to share and enjoy it.   
Daily, the parks will delight  
walkers, joggers, bicyclists,  
and transit riders with a green  
backdrop, a colorful display, new  
foliage, or a place to rest. Color,  
variety, a focus on arts, and a  
sophisticated sustainable palette  
will distinguish the landscape  
design and the selection of trees  
and plant materials. The result  
will be an appealing place to live,  
visit, enjoy the convenience of  
local retail, or simply pass through  
on the way to the train.  
Figure 4.18 illustrates the  
proposed open space system. 
Table 4.06 lists the Open Space 
Standards for the public parks 
shown in Figure 4.18. 
Maintenance for the entire open  
space system in the Plan Area will 
be the responsibility of the Master 
Developer or owners’ associations.

The Park: A Green Entry and  
Signature Open Space  
An approximately 1.1-acre,  
publicly accessible park will  
connect South Delaware Street  
and surrounding neighborhoods  
to the Hayward Park Caltrain  
Station (Figure 4.19). With  
neighborhood-serving retail and  
attractive, varied residential uses  
facing The Park, its sense  of place 
will extend from building  wall to 
building wall. Figure 4.23  is an 
illustrative cross section of  The 
Park. 
Visitors will experience The  
Park together with The Park  
Corridor leading from South  
Delaware Street as a series of  
green outdoor rooms oriented 
toward the south side of The Park. 
This asymmetry complements 
surrounding uses. Some of  these 
landscaped rooms will be terraced 
or shaded by a ceiling of tree 
branches (Figure 4.20), while 
others will be completely open to 
the sun (Figure 4.21). 
Along the entire northern edge 
of The Park, a row of large trees 
will provide a subtle separation 
between the residences 
fronting onto The Park, and 
the commercial uses opposite 
them;  an example is shown in 
Figure 4.22. The eastern and 
western ends of The Park have 
plaza-like spaces. The eastern 
plaza is  intended to front ground-
level retailers, and is more formal, 
including a reflective sculpture. 
The western plaza is differentiated 
by an informal grove of trees that 
anchors the western end. A grove, 
an exuberant flower garden and 
pathway intersect diagonally at 
the center of the park, connecting 
the Community Park and the 
Gateway Park. The central lawn 
will be accessible from both ends 
via an ADA-compliant ramp at its 
southern edge and one flush with 
the Pavilion Plaza at the northern 
edge. 
The Park will have high-quality 
finishes, large trees, and 
coordinated lighting, seating, and 
furnishings. It will accommodate 
changing events or uses, large 
scale sculpture and a small pavilion 
and/or transit kiosk. The Park can 
also accommodate stormwater 
management functions. 
Figure 4.24 is an illustrative 
rendering of The Park.

Two linear parks connect The 
Park from Concar Drive to Garvey 
Way.  Welcoming pedestrians, 
cyclists, and vehicles from Concar, 
Gateway Park begins with colorful 
plantings and an entry sculpture.  
The plaza provides a sanctuary 
for having lunch or just sitting on

Garden subareas guide views 
across The Park at the center of 
the Plan Area to the Community 
Park, while a topographical play 
element terminates the view 
screening adjacent parking.

horticultural variety and planes 
of tilting grasses for children’s 
enjoyment.  Smaller garden 
spaces and paths encourage quiet 
enjoyment and vary the scale from 
park to residences.  Pedestrians 
can access the Plan Area using a 
path along the rear of Block RE-3, 
terminating in Community Park.

The Greenway Pocket Parks 
and RE-3 Courtyard
The Greenway Pocket Parks
The Greenway stretches from 
Concar to Garvey Way widening to 
three pocket parks along its length. 
Divided between crushed stone, 
concrete, and a planted center strip, 
the EVAE functions as a pedestrian 
way, bike path, and green front door 
to the residences. The pocket parks 
provide areas to stop along the 
Greenway and small scale garden 
spaces for residents. Specimen tree 
plantings within the parks connect 
them to The Station Corridor and 
create a contiguous greenspace. 
The pocket park closest to Garvey 
Way also provides stormwater 
management areas integrated into 
the overall design. 
RE-3 Courtyard
This small-scale, lush open space 
will soften the entry to Block RE-3, 
and provide quiet park space for 
the community.  The RE-3 Courtyard 
connects visually and physically 
with the linear Community Park.  
Plantings should provide a greater 
horticultural variety than can be 
appreciated by faster-moving 
pedestrians.

Green Corridors 
Two linear open spaces called  
“Green Corridors” will link South  
Delaware Street to The Park and  
The Park to the train station. The  
Green Corridors will create view  
corridors through the Plan Area. 
The Park Corridor is intended  to 
extend The Park experience  to South 
Delaware Street  (Figure 4.30). The 
Park Corridor  introduces an axis that 
begins  at the South Delaware Street  
entry and runs west along the  north 
side of The Park toward  the Caltrain 
station. This axis is wide enough 
to make it visually  inviting and 
accessible to the  community (Figure 
4.30). The  main axis will be reinforced 
by tall trees and landscape finishes 
that  will encourage pedestrians 
and  bicyclists from the surrounding  
community to use the public  spaces 
within the Plan Area and  the Caltrain 
Station beyond.  
The Station Corridor will run east-west 
to link The Park to the train station, 
and to extend the view corridor from 
South Delaware Street through the 
Plan Area. Specimen trees will create 
a focal point and improve way-find-
ing. A main pedestrian route will be 
kept open for commuters but alcoves 
with bench seating will provide relax-
ing spaces to sit among lush plantings

Multi-Purpose 
Neighborhood 
Network  
The open space system for the Plan  
Area comprises not only the parks 
and green corridors dedicated to 
public use, but private streets open 
to the public. The goal is a detailed 
network that is inviting to pedes-
trians and bicyclists, yet accommo-
dates essential auto use (Figure 
4.33). 
The open space system will provide  
public parks, shaded streets, and  
other amenities typical of tradition-
al  neighborhoods. It is also meant  
to provide wildlife habitat, detain  
stormwater, and integrate utilities  
in an unobtrusive manner. Plant  
materials will be selected to favor 
large, long-lived trees, and coastal  
native and climate-adapted plants to 
provide habitat and conserve water.  
Components of this attractive and  
high-performing landscape include:   
street trees, wide sidewalks, set-
backs, suitable plant selection, 
stormwater management, and street 
lighting. For each of these design  
components, the following pages  
provide design standards.  
77 
 CHAPTER 4 DEVELOPMENT STANDARDS

--- page 268 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
78  
Figure 4.34 Illustrative rendering of The Park, looking northwest through The Park and The Station Corridor, which leads to the Joint Powers Board property

--- page 269 ---
Street Trees 
The street trees required 
under the  Plan will help to 
establish a spatial  hierarchy that 
differentiates portions of the Plan 
Area from one another. 
A line of monumental, fast-
growing trees will announce The 
Park at South Delaware Street, 
and continue west along the edge 
of  The Park to the boundary of 
the  Joint Powers Board property 
and  the Caltrain station. A 
secondary  signature sidewalk 
tree will unify the  streetscape 
along the north side  of this main 
entry street that leads  from South 
Delaware. An example  of this 
concept is illustrated in  Figure 
4.35. Connector streets that do 
not face The Park will have trees  
of different species. 
South Delaware Street itself will 
be  planted with tall evergreen 
trees  to provide a buffer between 
the  Plan Area and that busy 
artery, as  well as the Nineteenth 
Avenue  Park neighborhood 
beyond.  Concar Drive, which has 
less traffic,  will be planted with 
deciduous trees and accented 
with large evergreens, thereby 
providing some transparency for 
the retail uses facing south.  
Sidewalks 
There will also be a hierarchy  
of sidewalk treatments  
commensurate with the nature 
of  the street:  from the primary 
entry  street at South Delaware 
Street  to the narrower, 
local residential  streets and 
pedestrian-oriented  streets. 
Sidewalks in mixed-use  areas 
are intended to allow  activity to 
spill out from adjacent  retailers 
and restaurants, like  tables 
and chairs for ground-floor  
restaurants. (Figure 4.36) 
All sidewalks should include  
paved pedestrian zones at least  
five-feet wide and planting  
areas at least four-feet wide.  
The planting program of a  
sidewalk should depend on the  
uses fronting on that sidewalk.  
Accordingly, there are at least  
three sidewalk treatments  
envisioned: 
a.  Mixed-Use Sidewalks facing 
The Park—Encouraged to have  
evenly-spaced street trees with  
tree grates or cobble pavers.  
The pedestrian surface in these  
locations should be either  
poured concrete or concrete  
pavers.
  
b. Residential Sidewalks— 
Encouraged to have a continuous  
planting strip with evenly-spaced  
street trees and low shrubs.  
The pedestrian surface in these  
locations should be concrete. 
c.  Commercial Sidewalks (along   
Concar Drive)—Encouraged to  
have evenly-spaced street trees  
and low plantings.  The pedestrian 
surface in these locations should  
be poured concrete.  
Residential Usable Open  
Space 
Residential usable open space is  
meant to enhance the experience  
of living in multiple-family  
buildings. These may include: 
a.  Private Usable Open Space–  
When residential open space 
is  required, it can be provided 
in  many forms, including decks,  
balconies or gardens adjacent to  
individual units. 
b.  Common Usable Open Space– 
Instead of private usable 
open  space, common usable 
space  could be provided, such 
as  atriums, courtyards or roof 
decks. 
79 
c.  Semi-Private Open Space –      
When  semi-private residential 
open  space is required, fences 
and  walls will be minimized to 
provide  semi-private transitional 
garden  spaces between 
residential units  and public 
sidewalks, parks and  podiums. 
d.  Transitional Garden Space–     
When a publicly accessible  
open space is surrounded by  
dwelling units, there should  
be a transitional garden space  
adjacent to the units to prevent  
public circulation against private  
living spaces. 
Setbacks 
Consistent with the Corridor  
Plan, buildings are not required  
to be set back from every  
street. Where they are included,  
setbacks should be landscaped  
primarily with plantings (Figure  
4.37). Stormwater management  
is recommended in setbacks  
when grading allows. Plantings  
in the setback area should be  
coordinated with adjacent street  
trees to avoid visual disharmony.

Plant Selection 
Plantings should be selected for  
attractiveness, longevity, drought  
tolerance, ability to support  
regional habitat (including  
pollinators and birds), and  
adaptability to post-construction  
conditions. Native plants are  
encouraged whenever possible,  
along with California vernacular  
plantings and other climate-
adapted plants.   
Stormwater 
Management   
Strategy 
The landscaping within the Plan Area 
accounts for stormwater treatment in 
accordance with San Mateo County 
C.3 Requirements. The objective is 
not only to meet basic County and 
City requirements, but to do so in a 
sustainable manner, as follows:  
a.  Three primary open spaces — 
The  Park, Gateway Park and 
Community Park — should be used 
to treat stormwater runoff without 
compromising their usability. They 
should collect water from their 
surrounding streets, sidewalks and 
roofs  
b. Other runoff that does not drain to 
the parks should collect in stormwater 
treatment areas that are integrated 
into sidewalk and open space designs. 
An example is shown in Figure 
4.38 and Figure 4.39. All Plan Area 
stormwater treatment areas should 
have a bypass for large storm events. 
c. Unit pavers should be used in large 
paved areas to slow the volume of 
runoff and provide treatment via 
filtration 
.

FIGURE 4.39 Example of stormwater management

Block and Open Space Standards—The tables on pages 83-90 detail the 
development standards for Station Park Green by block and open space 
areas. These standards include permitted land uses, block sizes, ranges 
for dwelling units and floor area, as well as building heights and open 
space requirements.   
Outdoor Lighting 
Outdoor lighting in the Plan 
Area  should consider vehicular 
and  pedestrian circulation, 
residential  and retail uses, 
the diversity of  open space 
typologies, design  and 
infrastructure coordination,  
and energy efficiency. Outdoor  
lighting should include:  
a.  Energy-efficient, color-balanced 
lighting at the lowest possible  
lighting levels that provide  
for safe pedestrian and auto  
circulation. Particular attention  
should be paid to preventing  
glare into dwelling units and 
light  pollution into the night sky 
b.  Street light standards (fixtures)  
coordinated with the design 
and  materials of street 
furnishings  and buildings in the 
Plan Area  
c.  Street light locations that are  
coordinated with street tree  
locations to ensure a continuous 
tree canopy 
d.  A variety of lighting effects to  
highlight important building  
elements, special plantings, art  
and sculptures

and seven-feet wide.  For every square foot of required residential private usable open space not provided may be 
substituted 1.5 square feet of residential common usable open space within the same block, measuring at least 20-feet 
deep and 20-feet wide.  All remaining required open space may be publicly accessible open space located within the 
Plan Area.

substituted 1.5 square feet of residential common usable open space within the same block, measuring at least 20-feet 
deep and 20-feet wide.  All remaining required open space may be publicly accessible open space located within the 
Plan Area.

--- page 281 ---
FIGURE 4.41 Example of Emergency Vehicle Access Easement (EVAE)  
High-Performance 
Landscape   
Designing a high-performance  
landscape means planning ahead  for 
a multi-layered, multi-purpose, robust 
landscape. This strategy should deliver 
the landscape qualities along streets 
and in open spaces described in Table 
4.06. At the same time, it should 
manage stormwater, meet grading 
needs, provide emergency access and  
accommodate parking and  garage 
access.  
a. Streets should serve multiple  
functions, so that in addition to  
being conduits for vehicles and  
utilities, they also contribute to  
achieving environmental goals.  An 
example is the Emergency Vehicle 
Access Easement (EVAE) shown in 
Figure 4.41  which functions as a 
walkable,  permeable, pedestrian-
oriented  open space. Streets are 
encouraged to accommodate large 
trees and permeable paving to 
optimize shade,  moderate possible 
“heat island” effects, intercept water 
runoff, and encourage bicycle and  
pedestrian activity.    
b. The extent to which streets can  
achieve environmental goals is related 
to the volume of uncompacted soil 
that can be provided while satisfying 
Plan Area and road engineering  
requirements.  
c.  When required for stormwater  
management, large soil-
filled, planters or rain gardens 
are encouraged as a part of 
sidewalk design, and should be 
coordinated with street trees, 
lighting and infrastructure in a fully 
integrated manner.  
91 
d. The EVAE along the Plan  
Area boundary with the JPB  
property is intended solely  
as a contingency in case  
development is not coordinated  
with the JPB or an alternative  
access is not possible.  
 CHAPTER 4 DEVELOPMENT STANDARDS

--- page 282 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
92
To create a walkable block  
pattern, the Plan Area has a  
network of pedestrian-oriented 
streets. Residential and  mixed-
use streets will provide  narrow 
pavement (curb-to-curb)  widths 
that help slow traffic and  
prioritize pedestrian safety. On-
street parking lanes will also help 
to calm traffic. Ample sidewalks  
with planting and lighting will  
create spacious and enjoyable  
pedestrian spaces. 
Streets will be consistent with  
the intent, character, and spatial  
proportions of the street sections 
for mixed-use and residential  
streets shown in the Corridor  
Plan. Sidewalk widths in mixed-
use areas will support restaurant  
and café uses. Sidewalks on  
residential blocks will also create  
buffers from the vehicular traffic  
through setbacks or raised  
building entrances. As provided  
in the Corridor Plan, streets  
surrounding The Park will be  
one-way to promote visibility and 
minimize distance from adjacent  
sidewalks.  
These approaches to the  
organization of rights-of-way  
will create streets that respond  
to adjacent uses, promote a  
safe and pedestrian-oriented  
environment, and physically  
and visually connect the entire  
neighborhood. 
Streets within the Plan Area will  
remain under private ownership.  
As shown in Figure 4.43, the  
existing sidewalks and crosswalks 
adjacent to the Plan Area will  
remain.  
Street Hierarchy 
Vehicular circulation is organized  
to connect to the existing  
hierarchy of surrounding city  
streets. South Delaware Street  
and Concar Drive (between  
the South Delaware Street  
intersection and the ramps for  
SR 92) are designated as arterial  
streets by the City. 
Garvey Way and Concar Drive  
between the SR 92 ramps and  
Pacific Drive are designated as  
local streets. 
There will be three vehicular  
access points to the Plan 
Area  (Figure 4.42). Two will be  
main entrances from arterials/ 
collectors: one is on South  
Delaware Street opposite Charles  
Lane at the existing signal; the  
other will be at the existing signal  
opposite the ramps for SR 92.  
There will be secondary vehicular 
access from Garvey Way.  
The Park, which is at the heart  
of the Plan Area, is surrounded  
by one-way streets, connecting  
from the main entrances on 
South  Delaware Street and 
Concar  Drive (Figure 4.42) 
classified as  local streets. 
Another new local street will 
extend Garvey Way to The Park 
and the rest of the open spaces 
and buildings. Street standards 
are listed in  Table 4.07.

“A” Street 
(Street Section 1) 
“A” Street serves as the primary 
entrance  to the Plan Area from SR 
92 to the one-way street around The 
Park. The geometry  of the existing 
SR-92 / Concar Drive  intersection 
at the “A” Street entry shall  be 
retained, but should be designed 
such  that the intersection meets San 
Mateo  Public Work standards. “A” 
Street has  a conventional two-lane 
cross section  with parking bays and 
sidewalks on  both sides. Pedestrians 
shall be served  by sidewalks on both 
sides of the street  buffered from 
the vehicular traffic by six-foot-wide 
planting strips. On the south side of 
The Park, the MU-1 and RE-2 blocks 
flank  “A” Street.

a two-lane street with bike lanes

as a publicly accessible open space 
as well as provide sidewalks and

“C” Street 
(Street Section 3) 
“C” Street circulates counter-clockwise 
around The Park,  providing a single 
travel lane and  a bike lane. On-street 
parking is  permitted along the north and

Sidewalks should be landscaped   
on both sides of the street. To  maximize 
the area of The Park,  and to extend the 
sense of The  Park across “C” Street to 
the  buildings, there is no curb around  
The Park edge. Instead, a three-foot-wide 
hardscape area, with  warning pavers and 
free of vertical  obstructions, should be 
provided around the edge of The Park 
to  signal the vehicular lane and make  
sufficient room for emergency  vehicle 
access. Residential blocks are on the 
north side of The Park,  the Mixed Use 
and Residential block are on the south 
side.

  EVAE 
    (Street Section 7)  
The EVAE is a twenty foot wide 
Emergency Vehicle Access 
Easement and pedestrian and 
cycling route. Running along 
the western border of the site 
bordering the Joint Board Powers 
Property, it connects Concar Drive 
and Garvey Way at the existing 
cul-de-sac. Divided into an 8.5 foot 
concrete bike lane, 3 foot planting 
strip, and 8.5 crushed stone 
jogging path, “The Greenway” 
will provide space for multiple 
uses simultaneously as well as 
a pleasant commuting path for 
residents with EVAE-facing stoops. 
Three pocket parks line “The 
Greenway” and provide small-scale 
park spaces and places to rest 
along the recreation path.
FIGURE 4.51 Section 7: “EVAE”at Block RE-3 looking south

--- page 293 ---
The “Station Corridor” extends the 
feeling the The Park to the western 
edge of the site and creates a clear 
pedestrian and cycling connection 
to the Hayward Park Station. The 
large specimen trees are visible 
from the entry at South Delaware 
and B Street and create clear 
wayfinding within the site. Bike 
racks encourage cycling to the 
station and benches and plantings 
will make it a pleasant place to stop 
as well as pass through. A width 
of 20 feet was maintained should 
future development on the JPB 
site require a seamless interface of 
vehicular roadway.

and draw pedestrians from

operation in front of MU-1 
will remain. Additionally,  the

been be widened to provide a

Pedestrian Connections  
The Plan will create a walkable,  
pedestrian-oriented community,  
benefiting both Plan Area 
residents and visitors. The intent  
is to create a sense of place that  
values the pedestrian experience  
and that is not dominated  by 
vehicular traffic, using the  
following strategies: 
•   All streets internal to the Plan  
Area should have continuous  
sidewalks. Landscape buffer  
strips between the travel lanes  
and sidewalk should be provided  
where appropriate. Zones  
offering on-street parking should  
afford additional protection for  
pedestrians. 
•   A pedestrian connection shall  
be established opposite Charles  
Lane, providing an inviting  
connection to The Park from  
South Delaware Street. This  
connection should also serve as a  
direct connection to the Hayward  
Park Caltrain Station, allowing  
regional commuters on foot  
and bicycles a more desirable  
alternative than using Concar  
Drive. The pedestrian connection  
along Concar shall remain.  
Existing pedestrian crossings  
of South Delaware Street and  
Concar Drive shall remain or be  
upgraded as needed.  
•   A pedestrian-only access to The 
Park shall be provided  from  
South Delaware Street  via a 
pedestrian way through  block 
MU-1(The Paseo). Its exact  
location shall be set in SPAR and  
is not part of this Plan. 
•   If the EVAE is necessary along  
the JPB property, a shared  
pedestrian/bike path shall be  
provided along the western  
property line adjacent to the  
Joint Powers Board property,  
providing north-south  
connectivity. 
•   Pedestrian crossings should be  
provided at street intersections  
within the Plan Area. Crossings  
should be provided where  
publicly accessible Park Corridors  
meet the streets, provided that 
a  logical pedestrian connection 
is  made.  
FIGURE 4.56 Example of a pedestrian connection

--- page 301 ---
Bicycle Trail System   
This Plan will enhance the San  
Mateo Regional Bike Trail System  
(Figure 4.56). Class II bike lanes 
on  the east-west streets shall 
connect South Delaware Street, at 
the intersection with Charles Lane, 
to the Caltrain Station. A Class 
I shared bicycle and pedestrian  
path provided along the western  
property line from the northern 
end of RE-3 to the southern end 
of RE-2 where the path meets  
Concar Drive shall also provide  
bicycle access to the Caltrain  
station.

STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
112  
Transit Improvements  
The Rail Corridor Plan “aims to  
achieve an overall reduction in  
new vehicle trips of at least 25  
percent corridor wide.” 
In order to implement the 
goal of an overall reduction 
in vehicle trips, the project 
proposes Transportation Demand 
Management (TDM)  measures 
that could include  the following: 
first-class telecommuting 
opportunities for  residents of 
the development;  carsharing; 
shuttle service;  inclusion 
of neighborhood-serving 
retail; bicycle storage  for the 
convenience of bicycle  riders; 
unbundled parking;  shared 
parking (between office  
residential and commercial 
uses); a transportation kiosk 
with up-to-date information 
on transit, ridesharing,  
carsharing, bicycling and other  
alternative transportation; and  
a transportation coordinator  
who will be responsible for 
coordinating and maintaining the 
TDM Program. 
The City of San Mateo and the  
Peninsula Traffic Congestion  
Relief Alliance (Alliance) have 
completed the process of forming 
a Transportation Management  
Association (TMA) for the Corridor 
Plan area. Since it was formed, 
Station Park Green has been 
required to participate in  the 
TMA. TMA  participation will help 
to verify if  the 25% trip-reduction 
target is  met. A representative 
for the Plan  Area will also be 
required to serve  on the TMA 
Board of Directors.  
Parking and Loading 
Station Park Green is intended as  
a pedestrian-friendly, mixed-use,  
transit-oriented development,  
which offers the opportunity to  
share parking stalls between uses  
with different parking demand  
periods.  The Residential, Office,  
and Retail/Services/Restaurant  
uses permitted under this Plan  
generate particular levels and  
patterns of parking demand.  
Parking facilities within the Plan  
Area will be able to accommodate  
these demand fluctuations 
more  efficiently than segregated  
supplies, by accommodating  
peaking uses with stalls left vacant  
by other uses. 
The Corridor Plan provides  that 
conditions of approval for  all 
developments within the  Corridor 
Plan Area will establish  minimum 
and maximum parking  standards 
for the development,  with a 
goal of achieving an overall  
reduction in new vehicle trips  
of at least 25 percent corridor-
wide.  Accordingly, trip reduction  
strategies for Station Park Green  
have been evaluated as part   
of the environmental review  
process.  Shared parking is one  
key strategy. 
A shared parking analysis  was 
performed by Nelson  Nygaard 
Consulting Associates  and 
Hexagon Transportation  
Consultants as part of the  
environmental review of this  
Plan (Station Park Green Shared  
Parking Analysis, May 12,  2010).  
The analysis considered  three 
maximized build-out  scenarios 
possible under the  Plan – 
maximum permitted retail,  
maximum permitted office, and  
maximized mix of retail and office  
– and accounted for how parking  
demand can be expected to vary  
by time of day, for both weekdays  
and Saturdays. The supply  
exceeded estimated demand  
under all scenarios. 
Pursuant to Section 27.90.050 of  
the San Mateo Municipal Code,  
the uses permitted under this  
Plan shall require off-street motor  
vehicle parking according to the  
schedule shown in Table 4.08.

--- page 303 ---

different buildings, structures

be provided collectively in the

total number of stalls so located 
together shall not be less 
than the  sum of the separate 
requirements  for each use, and 
that at least one  parking stall 
accessory to each  dwelling 
unit shall be located  on the 
same parcel as the use  served. 
During SPAR, the Planning  
Commission (and City Council) 
may  consider allowing required 
off-street parking stalls to be 
space-efficient, without the 
need for a  variance. 
Loading facilities shall comply  
with Sections 27.64.310 
through  27.64.390 of the 
San Mateo  Municipal Code. 
During SPAR, the Planning 
Commission (and City Council) 
may consider allowing  
required loading facilities to 
be  located on-street, without 
the need  for a variance.

114 
 
Infrastructure Improvements 
Storm Drainage  
The Plan Area will maintain 
western, eastern, and northern 
drainage watersheds similar to 
those existing. Run-off from the 
western portion of the Plan Area 
should  discharge to the existing 
storm drain located in Concar 
Drive. Run-off from the central 
and eastern watersheds should 
discharge to the drain in South 
Delaware Street. The northern 
portion of the Plan Area should 
discharge to the existing Garvey 
Way storm drain. The existing 
Plan Area is highly impervious. 
The Plan Area will have an overall 
imperviousness that is equal to or 
less than the existing condition. 
This will result in the total peak 
discharge being equal to or less 
than the existing discharge from  
the Plan Area. Plan Area detention  
facilities are therefore not required  
and will not be provided.
FIGURE 4.59 Proposed Storm Drainage System

--- page 305 ---
115 
Stormwater Quality 
The stormwater drainage system 
will be designed in accordance 
with the National Pollutant 
Discharge Elimination Systems 
(NPDES) required by the San Mateo 
County Water Pollution Prevention 
Program (C.3 Stormwater Technical 
Guidance, January 2013).  
Due to the predominantly clay soils 
and program, the C.3 guidelines 
deem infiltration and rainwater 
harvesting infeasible. Stormwater 
treatment via biofiltration and low 
impact development strategies 
is therefore appropriate. The 
stormwater quality design for 
the Plan Area shall meet the C.3 
requirements by utilizing Best 
Management Practices (BMPs) to 
reduce stormwater pollutants in 
the storm water discharge from the 
development.
Station Park Green presents specific 
constraints and opportunities which 
guides the selection of appropriate 
BMPs. The two categories of BMPs 
that will be implemented are likely 
to fall under treatment control and 
source control.
Treatment Control BMPs 
Treatment control BMPs will 
include planted stormwater 
treatment areas that will treat 
runoff from the Plan Area using 
one or more methodologies, such 
as detention/retention, infiltration/
filtration. Mechanical devices are 
non-preferred and will only be 
considered in areas where other 
BMPs are infeasible.
Source Control BMPs 
Source Control BMPs are 
operational practices that reduce 
pollutants released at their source. 
Examples of source control BMPs 
that may be implemented include:
• Provision of efficient irrigation   
systems and appropriate landscape 
design
• Use of porous pavement and 
other permeable materials where 
applicable
• Provisions for appropriate trash 
enclosures and recycling areas
• Use of alternative building 
materials that leach fewer pollutants
• Car washing within the internal 
garages
The selected BMPs should be 
integrated into the urban landscape 
wherever possible. For Station Park 
Green, the use of infiltrative water 
quality solutions such as infiltration 
trenches will be significantly 
constrained by highly impervious soils 
in the Plan Area and the high water 
table.
Specific stormwater quality design 
measures shall be identified during 
the Subdivision Map or SPAR approval 
processes.
 
 CHAPTER 4 DEVELOPMENT STANDARDS

--- page 306 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
116
Sewer 
The Plan Area will be served by 
a network of approximately six-
inch- to 15 inch-diameter pipes 
that would connect to the 
City’s Southern Trunk system.  
Connections are likely to be 
made  at one or more points 
along the newly completed El 
Camino Real by-pass in Concar 
Drive, the Delaware Trunk line 
at the intersection of Charles 
Way, and/ or the eight-inch 
sewer in Garvey Way. (Figure 
4.58) 
Water 
Water service to the Plan Area is  
planned through a looped network 
of approximately eight-inch to 12-
inch mains which would connect  
to the surrounding network in  
Garvey Way and South Delaware  
Street. An extension of the water  
main located in Concar Drive 
may be required to service the 
western portion of the Plan Area.  
All water supply and distribution  
systems within the Plan Area 
will be within a California 
Water Service Company 
(CWSC) easement, and will be 
maintained by the CWSC.  
FIGURE 4.60 Proposed Sewer System

--- page 307 ---
117
Emergency Services 
Emergency services will continue 
to be provided by the San 
Mateo  Fire Department and San 
Mateo Police Department, with 
Fire Station No. 23 serving as the 
Plan Area’s first responder.  
All streets are expected to be  
private, and emergency vehicle  
access easements (EVAEs) 
shall be provided for the Plan 
Area in accordance with the 
requirements of the San Mateo 
Fire Department. The details of  
the EVAE(s) will be finalized at 
the time of SPAR approval. 
Utilities 
Pacific Gas and Electric 
Company (PG&E) provides 
electrical and gas service to 
the Plan Area. It is anticipated 
that several telecommunication 
providers will provide service to 
the Plan Area. The utility systems 
shall be located within private 
streets, and it shall be the utility 
provider’s responsibility to 
maintain the service up to meter 
connection locations. All utilities 
are expected to share a joint 
trench.  
Grading 
The grading assumptions made  
for Station Park Green are: 
•   The contours for the Plan Area  
must match existing contours  
along South Delaware Street 
and  Concar Drive along the 
northeast  and southeast 
boundaries. 
•   The proposed contours 
along  the boundary of the 
Joint Powers  Board property 
should match  those existing, 
where feasible,  to maximize 
connectivity to the  station. 
•   The contours along the 
northern  property line at 
the AAA  property and the 
Post Office  may be raised 
slightly, relative  to existing 
levels, to provide  residential 
finished floors above  the 
minimum permitted finished  
floor elevation of +104.7 feet  
(adjusted City datum). 
The finished grades within the 
project area will be generally 
similar to the existing grades. 
A point near the western 
edge of The Park shall be the 
highest graded point on the 
property with an elevation of 
approximately +107 feet. The 
ground is expected to fall to 
the west and match the existing 
grade at the Joint Powers Board 
property line. The Park will 
gently fall to the east where the 
eastern edge of the park will be 
approximately +102 feet.
Building heights within the Plan 
Area, except within the 35-foot 
Maximum Height Zoning along 
South Delaware Street, shall 
be measured from the existing 
high point of the Plan Area, 
i.e., approximately +107 feet 
(adjusted City datum).”
Flood Plain  
Management 
In early 2012, the City of 
San Mateo completed levee 
improvements that resulted in 
revisions to the FEMA flood 
insurance mapping in October 
2012. These improvements 
removed the project site 
from the 100-year flood plain 
(1-percent chance annual flood). 
Parts of the site remain in Zone 
X, the 500-year flood plain (0.2 
percent chance annual flood).
Due to the site being removed 
from the flood plain, the 
requirements of San Mateo 
Municipal Code Chapter 23.33, 
which were included in the 
2011 Specific Plan and Design 
Guidelines documents, no longer 
apply.
Currently, portions of the Plan Area 
(below +104.7 feet adjusted City 
datum) are susceptible to flooding 
given the unlikely failure of the 
Crystal Springs Reservoir Dam 
(Corridor Plan EIR). 
Waste and Recycling 
The San Mateo Municipal Code  
requires the provision of recycling  
facilities for multi-family housing.  
Non-residential uses within the 
Plan Area shall also comply.

121 
Chapter 5 
Implementation 
Construction of Station Park Green may take an 
extended period of time. To achieve the goals of 
the Plan, the  implementation process requires 
a coordinated  approach. There are five primary 
mechanisms for  the implementation process: Design 
Guidelines,  a Development Agreement, Subdivision 
Maps,  Site Development Permits, and Site Plan and  
Architectural Review (SPAR) approvals. This chapter  
describes how each of these tools will be used  to 
ensure that the vision of this Plan is ultimately  realized.

--- page 312 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
122 
Concurrently with adoption 
of  this Plan, the City Council 
will approve Design Guidelines 
by resolution, after receiving 
the recommendations of the 
Planning Commission. The 
Design Guidelines shall be in 
substantial  conformity with this 
Plan. The Design Guidelines shall 
set criteria for each block and 
open space within the Plan Area, 
such as: 
Build-to Lines: The required 
location  of exterior walls to define 
streets and open spaces
Setbacks: The minimum distances  
between buildings and streets or 
open spaces
Parking and Access: The required 
separation and/or location of 
curb cuts for parking garages and 
loading areas  
Development   
Agreement  
Plant Species/Size: The range of  
permissible species and sizes for 
trees  and other vegetation along 
streets, in  parks, and within blocks 
Building/Paving Materials: The  
range of permissible materials for  
constructing buildings, streets, 
and  open spaces 
Sustainability: The strategies 
to  achieve the sustainability 
objectives  of the Plan  
The City and the owner of the 
land within the Plan Area entered 
into a contractually binding 
development agreement in 2011.  
State and City law authorize the 
City to enter into a development  
agreement to vest in a developer  
certain development rights for 
a  period of years set by contract,  
regardless of intervening changes  
in land use regulations (Cal. Gov.  
Code §§65854 et seq.; San Mateo  
Reso. No.120). In exchange, the  
City receives certainty about  
the public benefits that will be  
included as part of the Plan.  
The owner of the land within  the 
Plan Area may apply for  parcel 
maps and/or tentative  maps 
to create separate parcels  to 
facilitate the development  
described in this Plan. Ultimately,  
each parcel may be further  
subdivided through one or more  
additional subdivision maps,  
and any and all maps may be for  
condominium purposes.   
Parcels may be transferred into  
separate ownership, but all  
parcels within the Plan Area will  
remain subject to this Plan unless  
the Plan Area is redefined to  
exclude them.  
Design 
Guidelines  
Subdivision  
Maps

--- page 313 ---
CHAPTER 5 Implementation
Site Development 
Permit  
Grading and/or the removal of  
major vegetation within the Plan  
Area requires a Site Development 
Permit under the San Mateo Site  
Development Code.  Among  
the specific purposes of the  Site 
Development Code is to  protect 
public and private lands  from 
erosion, earth movement,  and 
flooding, and to ensure the  
maximum preservation of the  
natural scenic character of the  
City by establishing minimum  
standards and requirements  
relating to land grading,  
excavations and fills, and removal  
of major vegetation.   
123
Accordingly, no site development  
may commence without  
first obtaining approval of 
a  site development planning  
application followed by  
application for, and issuance of, a  
Site Development Permit, which  
allows construction to proceed.    
Construction of an individual  
building within the Plan  
Area requires approval of an  
application for SPAR under  
Section 27.08.030 of the San  
Mateo Municipal Code. The  
officer or agency approving a  
SPAR is the Zoning Administrator,  
the Planning Commission, or, for  
buildings exceeding 55 feet in  
height, the City Council. 
The City should also review a  
signage program for the project  
before approving the SPAR  
application. The sign program can  
consist of guidelines.   
To approve a SPAR application,  
the City must review detailed 
drawings to determine that the 
proposed construction will be in 
scale and harmonious with the 
character of the neighborhood.  
The City must also confirm that  
the SPAR is consistent with this 
Plan. It is anticipated that the 
approval of each subsequent 
SPAR application consistent with  
the Plan shall not require the  
preparation of new environmental 
documents, unless the SPAR  
substantially changes the  
assumptions of the environmental 
study for this Plan.  
Site Plan and Architectural   
Review (SPAR)

--- page 314 ---
STATIONPARKGREEN SPECIFIC PLAN  
124
Amendment  
Information 
Allocation of 
Land Uses 
Pursuant to the California  
Government Code and the 
San  Mateo Municipal Code, 
this  Plan may be amended 
following  public hearings before 
and  approval by both the 
Planning  Commission and the 
City Council.   Such amendment 
is a legislative  act subject to 
initiative and  referendum.  Under 
state law, a  specific plan may be 
amended  in the same manner as 
a general  plan, except that it may 
be  amended as often as deemed  
necessary by the legislative body.   
This Plan provides for 
overall  aggregate limits on 
development  for the entire 
Plan Area. Due  to the long-
term nature of  the anticipated 
build-out, it is  desirable to 
maintain flexibility  in the precise 
allocation of  the density and 
type of land  uses. However, 
the density of  development 
for a particular  block must be 
allocated at the  time that (1) a 
Subdivision Map  or (2) a SPAR 
for such block is  approved, 
whichever occurs first.

CHAPTER 1 INTRODUCTION 
31 July 2014  
City of San Mateo

--- page 319 ---
How to Use the Design Guidelines 
1. Introduction [ 03 ] 
The Introduction gives an overview of the purpose and content of the Design Guidelines,  and 
provides an overall vision of the urban design concept and the sustainable planning 
and design approach. 
2. Open Space and Streetscape Guidelines [ 15 ] 
This chapter details the open space, streetscape, and landscape design strategies
 and guidelines that provide a pedestrian-oriented network of public amenities. 
3. General Urban Design [ 83 ] 
This chapter elaborates upon the objectives and policies of the Corridor Plan to 
contextualize Station Park Green into the Hayward Park TOD Zone as well as the larger City 
Context.
  
4. Blocks and Building Elements [ 111 ] 
This chapter includes block guidelines that address uses, edges, access, form, heights,  and 
building expression, as well as building elements guidelines that address the design  intent 
and character of and the relationships between buildings within each block in the 
Plan Area. 
5. Sustainability [ 151 ] 
This chapter describes how stormwater management, green design, and sustainable de-
velopment features will be integrated into the Plan Area. 
Appendix [ 165 ] 
The appendix includes definitions of some of the more specialized terms used in the 
Design Guidelines.

Station Park Green 
is  a compact network  
of walkable  blocks 
framing parks,  public 
spaces, and  pedestrian-
oriented  streets. As a 
transit-oriented plan, 
the  block pattern and  
street network are  
designed to provide  
internal connectivity,  
complement the mix  
of uses, and reinforce  
links to the Hayward  
Park Station and the  
surrounding existing  
neighborhoods.

--- page 331 ---
3 
Chapter 1 
Introduction 
This document guides the physical design of Station Park Green. These  
guidelines are intended to facilitate the creation of high-quality spaces  in 
the public realm—the parts of Station Park Green that are seen and  used 
by the public—including the system of streets, open spaces and  linkages, 
and the building components that frame them.   
Introduction [ 03 ]
Relation to the Specific Plan [ 05 ]
Vision [ 06 ]
Examples [ 07 ]
Urban Design Concept [ 08 ]
Sustainable Planning and Design [ 10 ]

10,000 to 15,000 square feet of Office

(excluding streets and sidewalks)

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES

--- page 333 ---
5 
CHAPTER 1 INTRODUCTION 
Relation to the Specific Plan  
The Design Guidelines give  
detailed guidance to supplement  
the Station Park Green Specific  
Plan (Specific Plan).  By detailing  
the physical design of all  buildings, 
commercial spaces,  open spaces 
and travel areas,  these guidelines 
ensure that  development in the 
Plan Area will  serve good planning 
principles  and make a pleasing 
aesthetic  contribution to the 
whole.   
These Design Guidelines state  
minimum and maximum unit  
densities, off-street parking and  
loading standards, setbacks,  
building heights, floor area ratio  
requirements, and open space  
requirements.  Additionally,  
ranges of permissible materials  
for constructing buildings and  
open spaces and paving streets  
are specified. 
All projects within the area of  
the Specific Plan (Plan Area)   
must comply with these Design  
Guidelines.  Thus future Site Plan  
and Architectural Review (SPAR)  
applications will be reviewed for  
consistency with these Design  
Guidelines. 
Throughout these Design  
Guidelines, mandatory conditions  are 
specified by the terms  “shall” and 
“not permitted.” All  other conditions 
are voluntary  and are specified by the 
terms  “should,” “encouraged,” “not  
encouraged,“ and “permitted.”  A 
project may still be approved  despite 
a failure to comply  with voluntary 
conditions. The  term “should” does 
not create  a mandatory condition, but  
does demonstrate intent. An  applicant 
that does not comply  with a condition 
which “should”  be included must be 
prepared  to explain why compliance is 
problematic.

--- page 334 ---
6  
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
FIGURE 1.02 Illustrative rendering of “A” Street in the Plan Area, facing  north along 
Concar Drive   
•  Active uses, including multiple 
building entries and lobbies, are 
encouraged to foster activity and 
interaction between buildings and  
sidewalks;   
•  Building articulation, such as  
storefront display windows,  
window openings, and the  use of 
varied materials, are encouraged 
in order to reinforce the pedestrian  
scale and create architectural  
interest.  
Vision 
 
Station Park Green is envisioned  
as a compact network of 
walkable blocks framing parks  
and pedestrian-oriented streets.   
As a transit-oriented plan, the  
block pattern and street network  
are designed to provide internal  
connectivity, complement the  
mix of uses, and reinforce links  
to the Hayward Park Station  
and the surrounding existing  
neighborhoods. A system of  
interconnected parks, park  
corridors, and pedestrian streets  
infuses the plan with a high-
quality landscape character and  
integrates sustainable practices. 
These Guidelines apply only to  
the Plan Area. However, as noted  
throughout these Guidelines,  
the ultimate design intent for the  
Hayward Park Station TOD Zone  
is for the Plan Area and the JPB  
property to operate together  
seamlessly through coordinated  
access ways, view corridors, an  
active network of streets and  
open spaces, and attention to  
building design and use along  
pedestrian routes, to achieve   
a successful transit-oriented 
neighborhood, to facilitate  
pedestrian and bicycle traffic  
across the JPB property to and  
from all portions of the Hayward  
Park Station Area, and to 
increased use of transit services. 
These intended design elements 
are  further elaborated below: 
•  The appropriate building  
articulation, building materials  
and landscaping at the interface  
of the Plan Area with the  
adjacent JPB property should  
provide for seamless integration. 
•  Pedestrian and bikeway  
amenities shall be considered to  
promote safe, efficient, attractive 
connections to and through the  
Plan Area and the adjacent JPB  
property, mindful that Hayward  
Park Station is not merely a  
destination but also a through-
way for pedestrians and cyclists  
traveling in both directions  
across the railroad right-of-way. 
•  Along the westerly edges of  
Blocks RE-2 and RE-3,  which 
face the JPB property,  once 
direct connection to the  
station area is implemented,  
attractive, safe, and well-lit  
pedestrian and bicycle access  
should be provided;

--- page 335 ---
7 
CHAPTER 1 INTRODUCTION 
FIGURE 1.03 Rendering from the ‘Rail Corridor Plan’ illustrating clear view corridors 
and good connectivity of streets drawing people through the two properties  
The figures in these Guidelines  
show an EVA at the southwestern  
portion of the Plan Area, along  
the boundary of the adjacent JPB  
property. It is the ultimate goal  
of these Guidelines to integrate  
the Plan Area seamlessly with the  
adjacent JPB property by providing  
for direct adjacency of buildings  
and the connection of vehicular,  
bike and pedestrian ways through  
to the JPB property. Integration of 
the properties could include the 
placement of buildings, fronting  
directly onto the JPB property, as  
well as a new street through the 
JPB property to meet Concar Drive. 
The location of street, pedestrian 
and bikeway amenities would then 
straddle the Plan Area and JPB 
property. However, at this time it is 
not known how the Plan Area and 
the JPB  property will ultimately 
interface, so the EVA is shown as a 
necessary contingency, providing 
required  fire safety access for 
the Plan Area, solely within its 
boundaries. The interface of the 
Plan Area and the JPB property 
will be determined during SPAR for 
Blocks RE-2 and RE-3.
Examples 
The Design Guidelines are illustrated 
throughout with images of existing 
built environments. These illustrations 
are meant to serve as examples of the 
different forms of development that are 
possible under these guidelines. The 
examples must be treated as  only that 
-- examples. The Design Guidelines 
are expressly intended to allow a 
wide variety of architectural styles, 
not merely the styles depicted in the 
examples.

--- page 336 ---
8  
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
FIGURE 1.04 Bird’s-eye-view rendering of Station Park Green, looking east  
The bird’s-eye-view rendering in 
Figure 1.04 reflects an overall  
vision for Station Park Green. 
It illustrates the relationships 
among  the blocks and their built 
edges,  the open space system 
and its  access linkages, and 
locations of key architecture and 
landscape focal points.  
Open Space System 
Station Park Green is organized  
by an interconnected system of  
public parks, green corridors,  
and pedestrian-oriented streets.  
Located at the center of the  
plan area, The Park is the central  
open space “room” for four 
mixed-use and residential blocks.  
There are also three smaller 
parks interconnected into the 
plan, Community Park, Gateway 
Park and the Greenway Pocket 
Parks. The Park is connected to 
South Delaware Street by a linear 
landscape, the Park Corridor. The 
interior streets along the four 
blocks have a special streetscape 
character that unifies the space 
around The Park from building 
face to building face.  
Access and Connections 
There are three points of vehicular  
access to the plan area: at South 
Delaware Street opposite  
Charles Lane; at Concar Drive  
opposite the SR 92 ramps; and  
from Garvey Way. The primary  
transit access corridor from South  
Delaware Street to the Joint  
Powers Board property is planned  
along the north side of The Park, 
creating a visual link to Hayward 
Park Station. Pedestrians may pass 
through Block MU-1 from South 
Delaware Street using a publicly 
accessible path called “the 
Paseo.”
Building Edges 
Three types of building edges   
differentiate the mixed-use and  
residential blocks within Station  
Park Green. The character of a  
building’s edges is related to the  
uses of that building, as well as   
to the adjacent streets and open  
spaces. Accordingly, the block  
frontages along Concar Drive and  
the JPB property will have mostly 
full build-to  lines. Frontages 
on The Park extend into the 
surrounding blocks with articulated 
build-to lines. The lower heights 
and massing of the units along 
South Delaware Street will have 
a high degree of modulation and 
residential-scale expression.  
 Urban Design Concept

CHAPTER 1 INTRODUCTION 
Focal Points 
Five architectural focal points and five landscape 
focal points are planned within Station Park Green. 
Architectural: 
•  The intersection of Concar Drive and South 
Delaware Street will be emphasized in Block MU-1, 
per the Corridor Plan. 
•  Blocks MU-1 and RE-2 will each have a focal point to 
emphasize the corridor to Caltrain Station.
•  A focal point at the north end of RE-3 will announce 
the project to traffic entering from Garvey Way.
•  The corner of “A” Street and “C” Street on Block 
MU-1 will anchor The Park with an architectural 
feature. 
Landscape: 
•  A landscape feature should be provided in The Park, 
on axis with “A” Street, to create a visible landmark 
announcing this 1.1-acre space.
•  The focal point at the intersection of Concar Drive 
and South Delaware Street will serve as a gateway to 
the site.
•  A landscape berm at the north end of Community 
Park will provide a visual terminus for the connected 
open spaces.
•  Sculptural artwork and specialized plantings at the 
south end of “A” Street and the east end of The 
Park will welcome visitors to the Plan Area.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Station Park Green integrates  
sustainable planning in its location, 
transit-orientation,  mix and 
intensity of uses,  block pattern and 
orientation,  landscape materials, 
and  stormwater management  
strategies. The approach to  
sustainable planning and design  
for the Plan Area is incorporated  
in every chapter of this document.   
Chapter 5: Sustainability includes  
detailed guidelines for the 
Plan  Area regarding stormwater  
management and building 
components.  
Publications relied upon include:   
•  Constructed Wetlands in the

Government Commission  and US 
EPA, September 2003)

The MIT Press, 1993)

Development pilot version  
(United States Green Building  
Council, 2007) 
•  Multi-Family Green Building

Waste Management Authority,

1999)

•  Sustainable Landscape

•  Take Back Your Streets  
(Conservation Law Foundation,

Landscape Guidelines [ 62 ]
15
Station Park Green is organized around a system of interconnected 
open spaces and streets that provide a pedestrian-oriented network of 
public amenities. The design approach to the parks and streetscapes 
is based upon principles of sustainability, and examples from nearby 
communities. This chapter describes the open space and streetscape 
design strategies and guidelines for the Plan Area. It is divided into 
three sections: Open Space Guidelines, Streetscape Guidelines, and 
Landscape Guidelines.
Chapter 2
Open Space and
Streetscapes

--- page 344 ---

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Figure 2.01 Open Space and Streets
The open space program 
includes The Park, Gateway Park, 
Community Park, Park Corridor, 
Station Corridor, Greenway Park, 
and the Courtyards. These eight 
open spaces are shown in Figure 
2.01. 
The street network includes three 
mixed-use streets around The 
Park, “A”,”B”, and “C” streets, 
and one residential street, the 
Garvey Way Extension. 
Plantings for the open space and 
streetscape program is covered 
in the Landscape Guidelines, 
beginning on page 62.

Figure 2.03 The Park Illustrative Site Plan

deciduous, fast growing trees edging 
a park
The Park 
The Park gives Station Park 
Green its primary identity. The 
Park will have a semi-formal, civic 
character, expressed through 
materials that reflect its prime 
Peninsula setting, and will build 
upon the examples established in 
nearby communities. 
From The Park, green corridors 
will reach out to the surrounding 
streets, and establish visual 
and pedestrian connections 
to additional open spaces and 
public transit. Within The Park, 
two green “rooms” will face 
south toward the mixed-use areas 
and the last room will face south 
towards the residential area. 
Residential areas north of The 
Park will be buffered by a line of 
tall trees. The Park’s spaces will 
offer a variety of scales and sun 
conditions to accommodate a 
range of activities related to the 
adjacent buildings and uses.

--- page 347 ---
CHAPTER 2 OPEN SPACE AND STREETSCAPES 
19
Figure 2.05 Illustrative rendering of The Park, looking west from RE-2
Tree species such as native 
redwood, oak, and cottonwood 
trees are encouraged in The 
Park to reinforce the regional 
microclimate and help shape the 
different scales of built and open 
spaces. Trees will vary in size 
from ornamental to specimen. 
Provision has also been made for 
a potential sculptural pavilion to 
provide a focal point and space 
for gathering, retail uses, utilities, 
bike parking, or other new uses 
and programs that may develop.

for a range of activities

Eastern Plaza 
General Guidelines 
•   Establish the first impression for 
neighborhood visitors to The 
Park. 
•   Relate to Block MU-1. 
•   Provide a semi-formal plaza for 
outdoor events, dining, and 
vending for retail tenants. 
•   Offer shade and seating for 
residents and visitors. 
Planting 
a. Tall, fast-growing deciduous 
trees shall be planted on the 
northern side to orient the plaza 
toward Block MU-1. 
b. The plaza should contain 
a sculpture surrounded by a 
ring of trees. Native oaks are 
encouraged. 
c. The plaza should provide 
a welcoming space to guide 
pedestrians from The Paseo 
at MU-1. Low plantings are 
encouraged to maintain openness 
and visibility. 
Sculpture 
a. The sculpture is encouraged 
to have a polished surface to 
capture the reflection of the 
sky and the surrounding tree 
canopies.
b. The sculpture should avoid 
creating harmful glare into 
residential units or the path of 
automobile travel.
Paving 
a. Unit pavers and decomposed 
granite are encouraged. 
b. The plaza edge contains a 
portion of the EVAE around The 
Park and shall be curbless and 
marked with a flush band of 
warning pavers and bollards for 
safety. 
Furniture 
a. Built-in seating should be an 
integral part of the plaza design. 
b. Moveable tables and chairs 
provided by mixed-use tenants 
are permitted.

--- page 349 ---

Figure 2.10  Eastern Plaza Illustrative plan and section
c. Furnishings shall be compatible 
with the materials and finishes 
of site elements such as lighting, 
signage, metalwork and minor 
structures.
Lighting 
a. Light poles should mark the 
southeast corner of The Park, 
using greater height and mass 
to create a transition to taller 
adjacent buildings. 
b. Accent lighting should be 
integral to the design of a plaza 
sculpture.
c. Paths of travel or areas of high 
pedestrian activity should be 
illuminated during the hours of 
darkness to an average of one 
foot-candle.

The Pavilion 
General Guidelines 
•   Provide a sculptural, iconic 
structure. 
•   Create a focal point. 
•   Anchor the view from Concar

lantern at night.

Planting

trees shall be used on the

provide an enclosed, shady area, 
and frame adjacent open space. 
Evergreens are encouraged. 
c. Tree species such as Olea 
Europea ‘Swan Hill’ (Fruitless 
Olive) or Arbutus ‘Marina’ 
(Madrone crossed with Strawberry 
Tree) are encouraged. 
d. Low plantings are encouraged 
to maintain openness

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Park Promenade 
General Guidelines 
•   Provide a green pedestrian 
connection from South Delaware 
Street to the Hayward Park 
Station. 
•   Subtly separate The Park from 
RE-3 and RE-4 with a screen of 
trees. 
•   Include a rain garden with plants 
that withstand saturation and 
drought to treat stormwater 
from the streets around The 
Park. More information on rain 
gardens is provided in Chapter 5: 
Sustainability. 
Planting 
a. A row of large, fast-growing 
deciduous trees that can 
withstand both saturation and 
drought conditions shall be 
planted along the northern 
edge of The Park. Species 
such as Populus fremontii 
(Fremont’s Cottonwood) or 
Fraxinus americana ‘Empire’ are 
encouraged. 
b. Carex spp. or other species 
capable of withstanding 
saturation and drought conditions 
are encouraged for the Rain 
Garden.

Lighting

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Linear Parks
Commmunity and Gateway Parks 
connect Concar and Garvey Way, 
and invite the public to share 
and enjoy the heart of the Plan 
Area. Daily, the parks will delight 
walkers, joggers, bicyclists, 
and transit riders with a green 
backdrop, a colorful display, new 
foliage, or a place to rest. Color, 
variety, a focus on arts, and a 
sophisticated sustainable palette 
will distinguish the landscape 
design and the selection of trees 
and plant materials.

Gateway Park
Connecting to The Park from 
Concar Drive, The Gateway Park 
provides a plaza for seating 
and begins a grove of trees 
and flower gardens that carry 
through the linear park designs. 
A large sculpture and plantings 
will anchor the corner of A Street 
and Concar Drive providing 
wayfinding into the site for 
pedestrians and cyclists. An 
elongated bench provides places 
for gathering.

General Guidelines 
•   Create a continuous view 
corridor from Concar Drive to

trees and vibrant ornamental

across all three parks.
•   Anchor the view corridor from 
Concar Drive with a cluster 
of trees at the north end of 
Community Park. 
•   Mirror the character of the 
Western Plaza in The Park to tie 
the two parks together along 
one axis. 
Planting 
a. Specimen trees that are similar 
to those in the Western Plaza 
shall be used in the grove. 
b. Tree species such as Cedrus 
deodara (Deodor Cedar) and 
Sequoia sempervirens (Coast 
Redwood) are encouraged. 
c. Drought-tolerant plantings are 
encouraged. 
Paving  
a. Concrete unit pavers and 
decomposed granite are 
encouraged. 
b. Flush wood decking is 
permitted.
Furniture 
Built-in seating should be 
provided and should be located 
and installed with careful regard 
to the protection of specimen 
tree roots. 
Lighting 
a. Lights mounted in branches of 
trees are permitted to create a 
moonlight effect. 
b. Paths of travel or areas of high 
pedestrian activity should be 
illuminated during the hours of 
darkness to to an average of one 
foot-candle.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Community Park
Across The Park and C Street, The 
Community Park is the northern 
most open space. To terminate 
the long view from The Gateway 
Park, a six foot high topographic 
play feature will provide a visual 
barrier as well as a children’s 
play area.  Tilted gardens give 
horticultural variety and the seat 
walls provide casual seating. The 
RE-4 Courtyard and the pathway 
to South Delaware Street extend 
this park into the smaller scale 
gardens and stoop zones of the 
residential units

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
The Mount
General Guidelines 
•   Terminate park views and screen 
views of the adjacent Postal 
Service facility. 
•   Provide opportunities for play 
through topography and art.
•   Create more expansive views 
from the top of the mount which 
in an otherwise flat site.
Planting  
a. The southern side of the mount 
should be irrigated lawn to 
facilitate play while the other two 
sides may be low-maintenance, 
low-water grasses.
b. The northern edge of the 
park should include  stormwater 
management plantings and fast-
growing, water-loving trees to 
create a backdrop for The Mount 
and screen views. 
c. Buffer plantings to the east of 
The Mount should match species 
in the Grove.
Paving 
a. Secondary circulation around 
The Mount should be concrete. 
Lighting 
a. Uplighting at The Mount is 
encourage to create dramatic 
effect and terminal vista.
b. Paths of travel or areas of high 
pedestrian activity should be 
illuminated during the hours of 
darkness to a level of one foot-
candle on average.
