STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Station Corridor 
& Park Corridor
General Guidelines 
•   Create a gateway to the Plan 
Area. 
•   Strengthen the east-west axis 
along The Park toward Hayward 
Park Station. 
•   Extend space and character 
of The Park from the Eastern 
Plaza to South Delaware 
Street to open The Park to the 
surrounding neighborhoods. 
•   Continue The Park Promenade 
to South Delaware Street, 
by using the same trees, 
construction materials, lighting, 
and rain garden plantings. 
Planting 
a. Tall, fast-growing deciduous 
trees shall be planted to continue 
the line of regularly-spaced trees 
in The Park to South Delaware 
Street. 
b. Tree species Populus fremontii 
(Fremont’s Cottonwood) or 
Fraxinus americana ‘Empire’ are 
encouraged. 
c. A 21-foot-wide continuous bed 
of plantings at least three feet 
high shall be installed below the 
trees. 
d. Carex spp. or other species 
capable of withstanding 
saturation and drought conditions 
are encouraged for the rain 
garden. 
Paving 
Sandblasted concrete colored 
with lampblack is encouraged. 
Lighting 
a. Low-level path lights should be 
used to mark the main pedestrian 
axis and to augment street and 
building lighting. 
b. The general level of 
illumination should average 0.4 
foot-candles. 
c. Paths of travel or high 
pedestrian activity should be 
illuminated during the hours of 
darkness to an average of one 
foot-candle.

•   Create an green and attractive

between the Nineteenth Avenue

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
FIGURE 2.58 Street section location key
This section describes each street 
section in the Plan Area. Figure 
2.58 indicates the locations of 
the street sections shown on the 
following pages. Plan Area streets 
will facilitate vehicular movement, 
link to public transit, encourage 
pedestrian use, and support 
sustainability. Since street names 
will be determined later, streets 
are indicated as “A”, “B”, “C” 
and Garvey Way Extention for 
reference purposes. 
The hierarchy of street types will 
be reinforced by the selection of 
trees. Streets with different uses, 
character, and volumes will have 
distinct street tree species. The 
largest trees will reinforce the 
main axes, focal points, public 
spaces, and mixed-use areas. 
Smaller trees will be used on 
secondary streets and areas with a 
lower, residential scale. Street tree 
placement should have priority 
over utilities and lighting to allow 
adequate space for root growth 
and regular tree spacing.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Landscape 
Guidelines
The following pages describe all 
landscape elements for the open 
spaces and streetscapes of the Plan 
Area, including trees, plantings, 
paving, lighting, furnishings, fencing, 
public art, signage, and accessibility. 
Trees 
Figure 2.74 diagrams tree selection 
within the Plan Area. 
General Guidelines 
•   Reinforce the street hierarcy and 
block pattern of the Plan Area 
through the selection of trees of 
varying character, giving specific, 
function-related identity to streets, 
parks and open spaces. 
•   Provide a backbone of evergreen 
trees that will serve as a green 
framework in contrast to the 
changing character and transparency 
of deciduous trees which provide 
seasonal change, texture, flowers and 
fall color. 
•   Select trees for longevity, ease of 
management, and adaptability to 
salty soil conditions. 
•   Encouraged tree species are listed in 
Table 2.01. 
•   No invasive species shall be used.

Lighting
The lighting for the Plan Area is 
diagrammed in Figure 2.97. “A” 
Street, “B” Street, “C” Street, 
and Garvey Way Extension are 
anticipated to have 16 to18-
foot-high street lights. The 
Greenway/EVAE is anticipated 
to have 12-14-foot-high street 
lights. South Delaware Street 
and Concar Drive should have 
City of San Mateo standard 
street lights. The open spaces 
should provide a range of lighting 
types, including path-marking 
lights, “moonlights” in trees, 
diffuse in-ground lights, bollards, 
and accent lights on landscape 
features. 
All outdoor luminaires shall be 
fully shielded such that they emit 
no direct uplight into the night 
sky. Some low-level accent and 
directional lighting in public 
spaces shall be allowed.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
General Guidelines 
•   Implement a hierarchy of lighting 
types with street lights providing 
ambient light and visual rhythm. 
•   Illuminate open spaces and 
streets to provide clear and 
comfortable visibility at night. 
•   Make streets and sidewalks 
inviting at night. 
•   Reduce nighttime accidents. 
•   Facilitate vehicular, bicycle, and 
pedestrian circulation. 
•   Promote business and use of 
public facilities at night. 
Levels 
Lighting should be provided at 
the lowest levels which are in 
accordance with the Illumination 
Engineering Society of North 
America (IESNA) lighting 
guidelines and applicable codes. 
Open Space 
There should be accent lighting 
at focal points, sculptures, or 
pavilions; mood lighting in high 
branches of mature trees; and 
path lights or markers such as 
LED displays.

•   Paving materials should have a

Fencing
•   Fences shall have architectural-
quality detailing and materials 
that enhance the overall Plan 
Area. 
•   Fences should be built with 
consideration for permanence, 
and are encouraged to include 
wood, masonry, metal or a 
combination of those materials. 
•   Fences in transition areas 
between residential and public 
spaces should be primarily 
transparent. 
•   Sound walls, chain link, security, 
plastic and lightweight tube steel 
fences are not permitted.

•   Public art is encouraged to be 
used to reinforce or upgrade 
required landscape design

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES
Signage
•   All text and signage should be 
designed to provide uniformity 
and coherence throughout the 
Plan Area. 
•   Signage shall be building-
mounted or integrated into 
required landscape structures 
whenever possible. 
•   Independent, freestanding signs 
are discouraged, except where 
required for City requirements 
such as street or parking signs. 
•   If approved by the City, required 
signage is permitted to be 
customized to be integrated with 
wayfinding signage. 
•   Illuminated signage should be 
directed towards the pedestrian 
or intended audience, with 
no spill light or light pollution 
affecting adjacent and 
neighboring spaces. 
•   Billboards are prohibited. 
•   Animated, moving, flashing, 
blinking, reflecting, revolving or 
other similar signs, or signs that 
incorporate these elements, are 
prohibited. 
•   Pennants, wind, or inflated signs 
are prohibited.
•   Signs cannot be placed at 
intersection or locations where 
they could obstruct the visibility 
of drivers.

Station Park Green is designed as a transit-oriented neighborhood.  
The general urban design guidelines in this chapter elaborate upon the  
objectives and policies of the Corridor Plan to fit Station Park Green into 
the Hayward Park TOD Zone, as well as the larger City context.  
Chapter 3 
General Urban Design

--- page 412 ---
84
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Form Areas
 
Concar Drive 
Concar Drive frontages of 
Blocks MU-1 and RE-2
•  Buildings should be of 
appropriate height and mass to 
screen interior streets and The 
Park from SR-92 conditions and 
to achieve desired density.  
•  Building articulation should 
provide variety, visual interest, 
and points of reference along 
Concar Drive.
•  Buildings may share courtyards 
and transition areas with 
adjacent form areas.  
•  Open space at grade should 
further modulate building mass.  
The Park Frontages 
Frontages to larger open spaces  
•  Buildings should be minimum 
height of 35’ (as specified in the 
block diagrams in Chapter 4) and of 
sufficient mass to clearly define a built 
frame around The Park.  This frame 
should create a coordinated and 
identifiable “room” around The Park.
•  Buildings should provide a transition 
from South Delaware Street and 
Concar Drive into the heart of the plan 
area with appropriate modulation. 
•  Buildings may share courtyards, open 
space, and transition massing with 
adjacent form areas. 
•  Open spaces at grade should further 
modulate building mass.  
•  Upper stories should be modulated to 
create visual rhythm along roof lines, 
provide usable open space, include 
architectural and landscape elements, 
and increase sunlight in The Park.   
 •  Building entrances and setbacks 
should create appropriate 
transitions for residential units 
from the street, while supporting 
an active relationship with the 
street.  
•  Building mass should create an 
architectural feature at both ends 
of The Park. 
•  Spaces and structures should be 
aligned to create direct physical 
or visual connections across 
streets and The Park.
•  Building lobbies should be 
located to activate the street 
frontage and complement the 
composition of building forms
The Plan Area has five main form 
areas: Concar Drive, The Park 
Frontages, Delaware Street (MU-
1), Delaware Street (RE-4), and 
Hayward Park Station Edge. For 
block-specific form guidelines, 
refer to Chapter 4: Blocks and 
Building Elements. 
 
Building form within the Plan 
Area should address the internal 
and external contexts of Station 
Park Green.  Within the form 
areas, building heights should 
be modulated as contextually 
appropriate, to break up building 
mass, create variety and visual 
interest, and allow for penetration 
of sunlight and air.  Figure 3.01 
shows each form area.  The 
approach to form is shown in a 
three-dimensional perspective 
view in figure 3.02, with basic 
buildable envelopes.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Edge types and building placement 
guidelines create a hierarchy of 
streetwalls in the Plan Area. There 
are three major edge types: 
Build-to Block Lines, Limited 
Landscape, and Setback.  
•  Building placement and edge 
character should ensure clear 
block patterns, active sidewalks, 
and buffers for residential areas.  
•  Edge types should consider land 
uses and the surrounding context.
Build-to Block 
Lines 
Block MU-1 frontages
 
•  Buildings at Block MU-1 should 
clearly define the edge to activate 
and engage frontage for retail uses 
and building entries. 
•  Building edges should follow the 
block lines wherever possible, 
creating an urban street edge with 
frequent pedestrian entrances and 
hardscape.    
Limited Landscape 
Landscape buffers of zero to 
ten feet from the block line are 
permitted for blocks RE-2, RE-3, 
and RE-4 to buffer from pedestrian 
and vehicular traffic or other uses 
as detailed in Chapter 4: Blocks 
and Building Elements. Parking 
and loading areas should be 
screened.
•  Buildings fronting on The Park 
should allow for a landscaped 
transition at the building edge while 
still framing The Park and activating 
the ground level. 
•  Landscaped buffers should provide 
additional space for residential 
stoops at street level.  
•  Buildings fronting The Park, 
Delaware Street, and Concar 
Drive should have a continuous 
and articulated streetwall to 
define the blocks.
•  Buildings at the corners of 
intersecting streets should hold 
the corner, avoiding excessive 
setback from the block lines.
•  Reducing the Limited 
Landscape zone (up to zero 
feet) may be utilized at 
lobby locations, residential 
amenities, and when 
architectural articulation 
requires increased variation in 
depth.  Reduced landscaping 
should be minimized, used 
primarily as a means of the 
building reaching out to 
engage with the sidewalk, 
highlighting special spaces.
•  The building edge must 
remain within ten feet of 
the block line, ensuring 
engagement with the street 
and creating a more urban 
condition throughout Station 
Park Green.
Building Placement and Edges  
Setback
Setback conditions should be 
used for additional landscaping 
next to ground-floor units, 
and to allow for increased 
building articulation as the 
building mass steps in and out.  
Setback depth varies from each 
condition and reacts to the 
blocks contextual edge.
 
•  Setbacks should create a 
transition space along the 
EVAE and the Plan Area’s 
north edge to allow for 
stoops, pedestrian walkways, 
and landscaping to foster 
and appropriate residential 
character.
•  Setbacks should create 
appropriate transitions at site 
edges.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Solar Orientation  
The exposure of building  
façades to the sun is illustrated  
in Figure 3.11. Façades facing  
south and west have the most  
solar exposure, while northern  
and eastern façades will receive  
limited sunlight.  
•  Buildings should be designed  
with an understanding of their  
relationship to solar orientation.  
•  Buildings with significant  
exposure to the sun should  
incorporate façade treatments  
that address solar exposure;  
such treatments can reduce  
energy expenditures and create  
more comfortable indoor  
environments for working and 
living. 
•  Buildings with significant  
exposure to the sun should  
consider incorporating devices  
to harvest passive energy. 
•  Southern and western façades  
should incorporate elements  
such as brise-soleil, balconies, or  
loggias to shade against the sun. 
FIGURE 3.11 Solar orientation

--- page 421 ---
FIGURE 3.14 Vertical Expression
93 
Modulation of Built Form
Within Station Park Green, 
diversity of modulation 
creates a more successful 
street character and vibrant 
streetscape.   Through 
conscious variation, the Plan 
Area becomes a village of 
diverse architectural styles.  
Three particular forms are 
emphasized in the buildings’ 
massing: Vertical Expression, 
Horizontal Expression, and 
Residential-Scale Expression. 
(Figure 3.12) 
•  Integrating vertical and 
horizontal forms along The Park 
edge will create visual interest 
and varied ground-plane 
interaction.  
•  Each form should address 
ground-floor uses, access 
points, street character, and 
solar orientation into its design. 
•  The building facades within the 
Plan Area should be broken 
into segments through the 
variation in horizontal and 
vertical articulation.
•  Utilizing vertical and horizontal 
articulation for architectural 
focal points will create a variety 
of iconic forms as reference 
points throughout the Plan 
Area.  Heightened forms will 
allow for vertical expression 
(such as lobby and leasing 
locations) with horizontal 
masses anchoring both ends of 
The Park. 
•  Materials and openings should 
differentiate facade planes.
•  Facade step backs, recesses, 
and projections should 
continue above the ground 
floor to create visual interest 
and rhythm.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
FIGURE 3.18 Primary Vertical Expression  
FIGURE 3.17 Modulation of Built Form
Vertical Expression
Vertical Expression  
The use of Vertical Expression is one 
means of providing architectural 
diversity throughout the Plan Area. 
Creating a series of planes at the 
ground floor provides opportunities 
for landscaping, pedestrian niches, 
and defined entries.  Carrying these 
jogs in the building plane up to the 
parapet creates roof form variation 
that articulates the overall mass-
ing of the facade. Recesses used 
to define vertical building forms 
also create voids ideal for defining 
unit decks within the built mass.  In 
mixed-use conditions, continuing 
vertical lines from the upper resi-
dential expression through to the 
ground floor can inform glazing 
openings,  pedestrian access points, 
and breaks in plane while incorporat-
ing horizontal articulation to create a 
strong ground floor-base. 
1. Facade modulation: Facades 
should establish a vertical 
expression (see Figure 3.18).
•  Vertical modulation will create 
diverse facade depth as well as 
variety at the street level as the 
building wall meets the ground 
plane.
•  Vertical expression should relate 
to ground-floor facades to engage 
street-level activity.
•  Individual planes within the facade 
above the ground-floor should have 
proportions that use the width of a 
residential unit or room to create a 
vertical rhythm.
•  Irregularity of the vertical rhythm 
is encourage to achieve greater 
diversity and further integrate with 
the overall design strategy.
•  The depth of step backs and 
recesses should vary from one to ten 
feet.
Vertical Modulation / Rhythm  
Vertical  
Proportions 
Ground Floor Base  
Façade plane

--- page 423 ---
95 
2. Roof Modulation: Roof  
modulation should create  
attractive and varied rooflines  
(see Figure 3.19). 
•  Buildings should vary massing  
within the height limits. 
•  Modulated roof forms, integrated   
with mechanical equipment, are  
permitted. 
•  Roof shapes and treatment  should 
contribute to the overall  roofline 
appeal. 
3. Ground-Floor Interface: Ground-
floor  treatment should create 
a vibrant interface between the 
building and the sidewalk (see 
Figure 3.20).
 
•  Buildings should foster  interaction 
between the inside  and outside by 
using multiple  entrances and trans-
parent  façades.   
•  Architectural details such as retail 
entrances, residential or office 
lobbies, other active uses,  awnings, 
paving, or signage  should be used 
to create visual  appeal and interest 
at the  pedestrian level.  
•  Minor setbacks of ground-floor 
façades are permitted to create va-
riety and additional spatial interest. 
Please refer to Chapter 4: Blocks 
and Building Elements for further 
guidelines on ground-floor design.
4. Corners: The massing  of corners 
should create  distinctive façades 
(see Figure 3.21). 
•  Corners should address the  charac-
ter and scale of adjacent  streets. 
•  Façade treatment and materials  
should optimize orientation to  sun-
light. 
5. Distinctive Corners: Massing 
should create distinctive 
landmarks at important corners.  
• Block MU-1, at the corner of “C” 
street and “A” Street, should address 
The Park and highlight ground-floor 
uses. 
•  Block MU-1, at the corner of South 
Delaware Street and Concar Drive, 
should announce Station Park Green, 
anchoring the retail edge and the 
Delaware/Concar Plaza. 
•  Lobby entrance at RE-3 fronting 
Hayward Park Station should create a 
visual landmark for the pedestrian ac-
cess point and break up the longest 
block frontage.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Horizontal 
Expression
Horizontal expression creates a 
strong base at the ground level, 
which lends itself to retail uses.  This 
approach is used on Block MU-1 
along South Delaware Street to 
highlight the mixed-use component 
of Station Park Green.  Horizontal 
expression establishes an important 
visual line directing visitors in at the 
corner of “A” Street and Concar 
Drive.  Similar forms used along the 
north side of “C” street visually rein-
force the connection with the transit 
station.  Additionally, using horizon-
tal articulation in high sun exposure 
areas creates useful shade. 
1. Primary Horizontal Expression: 
Façades should establish a  primary 
Horizontal Expression  (see Figure 
3.29).  
•  The Horizontal Expression shall  be re-
lated to the components of  base, mid-
dle, and capital/cornice,  and framed by 
corner end-pieces.
•  Horizontal expression should be paired 
with landscaping and stoops at the 
ground level to provide variety where 
the building wall meets the ground 
plane.
•  In particular for larger blocks, corner 
end-pieces should be considered to  
frame the façade and to address  the 
character of adjacent streets. 
•  Ground floors should be designed as a 
base for the primary  Horizontal Expres-
sion  of buildings.
•  Buildings should comply with block 
edges established in Figure 3.05.  
•  Southerly facades should  incorporate 
shading devices  such as brise-soleil, 
balconies or  loggias that support the 
horizontal  rhythm. 
 
  
FIGURE 3.28 Modulation of Built Form
Horizontal Expression
FIGURE 3.29 Horizontal Expression framed by corner end-pieces

--- page 427 ---
99 
2. Secondary Expression:  
Secondary façades should  
create variety and rhythm  
within the primary Horizontal  
Expression (See Figure 3.30). 
•  Projections within required  setbacks 
and stepbacks should  be used to 
create secondary  modulation and 
façade interest. 
•  The secondary modulation may  
be horizontal and/or vertical. A  
secondary Vertical or Horizontal  
Expression is permitted to relate  to 
or engage roof treatments so that 
horizontal and vertical lines interplay 
dynamically, provided that reading 
of horizontality remains dominant. 
•  Corner end-pieces or side  façades 
should address the  immediate 
street and open  space context and 
orientation, as  well as the height 
and character  of  buildings on the 
facing block. 
3. Roof Modulation: Roof  
modulation should create  
attractive and varied rooflines  
(See Figure 3.31). 
•  Buildings should vary massing  
within the height limits. 
• Modulated roof forms, integrated   
with mechanical equipment, are  
permitted.  
•  Roof shapes and treatment  should 
contribute to the overall  roofline 
appeal.  
•  Roof shapes and treatment  should 
also consider their  southern orien-
tation to create  additional shade for 
façades or  private open space. 
4. Ground-Floor Interface: 
 Ground-floor treatments  should 
create an attractive  transition 
between the street  and 
residential units (See Figure 3.32). 
•  Ground-floor units should be  raised 
above street level while  ensuring 
ADA accessibility.  Please refer to 
guidelines for  ground-floor design 
in Chapter  4: Blocks and Building 
Elements. 
•  Setback areas should be  supple-
mented with treatments  that create 
visual interest for  pedestrians, such 
as stoops,  awnings, paving, and 
landscape. 
•  The ground floor should  be 
activated with building  entrances, 
secondary unit  entrances, or other 
active uses  that support residential 
use.

Housing 
Diversity
Parking and 
Loading  
•  The Plan Area must include at  
least one car-sharing stall for  ev-
ery 230 required off-street  motor 
vehicle parking stalls.   “Car-shar-
ing” means a service  that 
provides an integrated  network 
of neighborhood-based  automo-
biles available only to  members 
by reservation for  periods as short 
as one hour. 
•  Off-street motor vehicle facili-
ties  are encouraged to include  
charging stations for electric  
vehicles.  
Bicycle Parking Facilities 
The Plan focuses on pedestri-
ans  and bicyclists and provides 
non-motorized access to all parts  
of the Plan Area.  To support  this 
focus on bicyclists, bicycle  parking 
facilities shall be provided  in an 
amount equal to at least  five per-
cent (5%) of the required  number 
of off-street motor  vehicle parking 
stalls, and using  the following 
guidelines:   
•  Bicycle parking is permitted on  
streets and should be protected  
from encroachment by motor 
vehicles. 
Unit Types and Sizes 
•  Station Park Green should have  
multiple unit types, such as 
studio, 1-bedroom, 2-bedroom, 
and 3-bedroom.
•  Units should range in size among  
different unit types. 
Affordable Housing 
•  Pursuant to Municipal Code  
Section 27.16.050 and City  
Council Resolution 121 (2008),  
the Project shall provide BMR  
units in conformance with a  
BMR Agreement(s) between the  
building permit applicant and  
the City.  
Motor Vehicle Parking  
Facilities 
Off-street motor vehicle parking  
facilities shall be provided for  
each use in the amounts required  
under the Specific Plan, and using  
the following guidelines:  
•  All off-street parking facilities  
shall be designed and  main-
tained in compliance with  Sec-
tion 27.64.140 of the San  Mateo 
Municipal Code.
•  Off-street parking facilities acces-
sory to residential uses should be 
separated from parking dedicat-
ed to other uses.
 
•  During SPAR, the Planning  
Commission (and City Council)  
may consider allowing required  
off-street parking stalls to be  
space-efficient and loading  
facilities to be located on-street,  
without the need for a variance. 
 
•  Except as to space-efficient stalls, 
the stalls, aisles, turning radii,  
and turn-arounds for off-street  
parking facilities shall comply  
with Sections 27.64.120 through  
27.64.130 of the San Mateo  Mu-
nicipal Code.
•  Off-street bicycle parking should  
be in prominent locations near  
entrances to buildings, but shall 
not obstruct pedestrian access. 
•  Each bicycle shall be provided  a 
minimum of eighteen (18)  inch 
side clearance from the  center-
line of adjacent bicycles  and ten 
(10) inch clearance both  front 
and rear. 
•  Secured bicycle parking for each  
residential unit is encouraged. 
Loading 
Loading shall be provided for  
each use in the amounts required  
under the Specific Plan.

Chapter 4 
Blocks and Building  
Elements   
This chapter includes guidelines specific to each block in the Plan Area  as 
well as guidelines about the placement and character of building  elements. 
The block guidelines address uses, edges, access, form, heights, and 
building expression on the block level. The building elements guidelines 
address the design intent, character of, and relationships between buildings 
within each block, including their façades, roofs, ground floors, and 
residential usable open spaces. Diagrams and sample images shown in 
this chapter further illustrate  particular design characteristics, but are not 
intended to prescribe architectural style. Guidelines for the Plan’s open 
space blocks are provided in Chapter 2: Open Space. 
This chapter should be read in concert with Chapter 3: General Urban  
Design.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Form
Please refer to the “Building Elements” section in this chapter for additional guidelines.  
General
Primary massing shall be along Concar Drive, “A” Street, and “C” Street/The Park, and secondary massing along South Delaware Street. Please refer to 
the “Form” section of Chapter 3: General Urban Design.
Massing Articulation
Built mass should be broken into varying styles through perceptible changes in façade treatment and articulation, height, materials, etc.
Holding Corners
Buildings shall be built to the primary streetwall for a distance of at least 40 feet along each frontage at the corners of: Concar Drive/“A” Street; “A” 
Street/”C” Street; and “C” Street/”B” Street.
Stepbacks
Permitted above the ground floor, up to six-feet in depth beyond the primary streetwall, except within hold corner zone. Greater stepbacks are permitted 
for top floors.
Streetwall and Setback
Please refer to the “Building Elements” section in this chapter for additional guidelines.
Primary Streetwall
Primary streetwall shall be zero feet to ten feet from the block line. Additional setbacks, projections, stepbacks, modulation of built mass, and articulation of 
the façade shall be defined relative to the primary streetwall.
Buildings shall occupy at least 70% of the primary streetwall on Concar Drive and “A” Street, and 60% of the primary streetwall on “C” Street along The 
Park.
Concar Drive Frontage
Build to primary streetwall.
South Delaware Street and 
Concar Drive Corner
Building entry should be recessed to create a pedestrian plaza.
South Delaware Street:
Mixed-Use Frontage
Build to primary streetwall.  Five-foot setback is permitted on South Delaware Street. 
“A” Street Frontage
Build to primary streetwall. Setback of no more than five feet is permitted for providing building articulation, screening of ground-floor surface parking, utili-
ties, and garage entrance.
The Park/”C” Street Frontage
Setback up to five feet is permitted at the entrance to the The Paseo.
“B” Street Frontage
Alternate alignments along “B” Street are permitted to open views of The Park from South Delaware Street.
Height*
Excludes rooftop structures.
General
Except as noted below, building heights shall be no less than 40 feet and no more than 55 feet.
South Delaware Street
Building heights shall be no more than 35 feet to a depth of 45 feet from South Delaware Street.
Building Envelope

* Measured as described in the Appendix under Building Heights

CHAPTER 4 BLOCKS AND BUILDING ELEMENTS 
Element
Garage Entrance
Residential garage access shall be from “A” Street. Maximum width of curb cut shall be 35 feet. 
Retail garage access shall be from “C” Street. Maximum width of curb cut shall be 35 feet.
Garage entrances shall be designed to minimize disruption of the pedestrian realm. Portions of the garage visible from the public realm should reflect the 
same architectural character employed throughout the rest of the building.
Building service areas, including loading areas, trash and recycle dumpsters, and garage access locations, should be located where they will not negatively 
impact the pedestrian environment.
Parking
Parking should be enclosed. Secured parking for residential units is encouraged. 
The Paseo
An at-grade pedestrian pathway with at least 12 feet in vertical clearance shall be provided through the block, connecting South Delaware Street to The 
Park. The width of The Paseo, no less than 15 feet, shall be determined during SPAR with reference to safety, privacy, desirability of living spaces, and other 
relevant factors. 
The Paseo is encouraged to be open to sky or partially covered for no more than 50% of its length. It should include pedestrian amenities, including street 
furniture, and lighting. 
Building frontages shall be designed to address security and to activate The Paseo. Active uses, building entrances, window openings and balconies are 
encouraged where possible to enliven The Paseo. Landscaped transition should be used for non-active uses.
Signs shall be placed at the corner of South Delaware Street and Concar Drive to indicate the location of The Paseo.
The Paseo planting, paving, site furnishing, and lighting should match the adjacent sidewalk treatment along South Delaware Street.
OS-10
Block OS-10 is The Park Corridor, a landscaped pedestrian connection providing clear and direct access from South Delaware Street to The Park. Please 
refer to Chapter 2: Open Space and Streetscapes.
Building Entrances
Park Frontage
At least two building entrances other than residential lobbies, and at least one residential lobby, should be provided on The Park frontage.  
Concar Drive
At least two building entrances other than residential lobbies should be provided on Concar Drive. 
Additional building entrances are encouraged and should be located along frontages with active uses, particularly along Concar Drive and The Park.
South Delaware Street 
At least two building entrances other than residential lobbies should be provided on South Delaware Street. 
Circulation and Access

Chapter 3: General Urban Design.
Architectural expression along South Delaware Street is encouraged to distinguish active ground-floor uses. 
Architectural Feature
A distinctive architectural feature or treatment shall address the corner of South Delaware Street and Concar Drive.  
To hold the west end of The Park, an architectural focal point should be located east of “C” Street.

STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
FIGURE 4.41 Example of possible materials, openings and projections  
Building Elements
This section addresses  
fundamental building design  
principles and guidelines for  
major building elements, such  as 
façades, roofs, residential usable 
open space, and signage,  as well 
as the relationship of these  with 
publicly accessible open  spaces.   
Design Intent  
The following principles  
summarize the design intent for  
buildings in the Plan Area:
•  Advance a diverse, cohesive  
identity for the Plan Area through 
the expression of building  
façades, projections, and  
architectural elements. 
•  Address uses, open spaces,  
landscape treatments, and solar  
orientation in the design of  
building elements.
•  Incorporate architectural variety  
and authenticity in building  
design. 
•  A single building should not  
overuse multiple architectural  
expressions.  
The following guidelines  
encourage the design of building  
elements to fulfill the intent of  
Station Park Green as a model  
sustainable neighborhood.  
 
FIGURE 4.40 Buildings should not overuse multiple architectural expressions

--- page 465 ---
137
CHAPTER 4 BLOCKS AND BUILDING ELEMENTS 
FIGURE 4.42 Example of building  
transition  
FIGURE 4.43 Example of materials and  
colors  
Building Façades  
Design  
•  Façades along block perimeters  
should be articulated according  to 
their specific uses.  
•  Façade design should reference   
existing or designed façades for  
adjacent buildings in the Plan  
Area.  
•  Façades with southern  and 
western exposure are  encouraged 
to include passive  solar shading 
devices, including  overhangs, 
horizontal sun  shades, and vertical 
shading  (such as fins) to balance 
solar  access with the need to 
control  heat gain. 
•  Façades that transition between 
styles or form areas  should 
incorporate variations in height, 
recesses of the façade plane, 
or changes in materials  and/or 
openings, so these transitions are 
identifiable from street level. 
•  Tones and colors of materials  should 
be used to add visual  interest and 
also generally be in  keeping with 
those used in San  Mateo and the 
Bay Area. Accent  colors should be 
used with  moderation.  
•  Façades of courtyards should  
have greater diversity to address  
their intimate scale.  
Materials and colors  
•  Façades should have modulation  
and articulation to create  visual 
interest and architectural  diversity 
in the neighborhood.  
•  The façades of higher  buildings 
should generally adopt  a defined 
base, middle, and top  as a primary 
modulation.  
•  Timeless and durable materials  
are encouraged for building  
façades, including terra-cotta,  
stucco, clay brick, pre-cast or  
cast-in-place concrete, wood,  
steel, and glass.  
•  Large, uninterrupted expanses  of 
highly-reflective materials  which 
create glare, such as bright  
aluminum cladding, are not  
encouraged. 
 
•  The use of sustainable building  
materials, such as materials  with 
recycled content or those  regionally 
produced, is   encouraged.  
•  Materials should be located  in a 
manner that reflects their  traditional 
structural placement.  For instance, 
wood should be  placed above 
stone.  
•  The use of synthetic stucco, such  
as Dryvit, is not permitted.  
•  To maintain a pedestrian-friendly  
scale, building façades should  
take advantage of allowed  
setbacks or a change of material  
or fenestration at intervals of 75  
feet or less.  
•  Changes of materials and/or   
façade openings are encouraged  
to create diversity and to support  
the massing, transition, and  
modulation strategies.

--- page 466 ---
138
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
FIGURE 4.44 Example of projections  
Façade Openings,    
Projections, and    
Stepbacks   
Openings, projections, and   step-
backs are encouraged to   artic-
ulate façades and create  visual 
interest.  
Openings  
•  Tinted glazing is not permitted.   
•  Low-energy glazing is   
permitted.  
•  Main entrances should be   
located to encourage activity on  
the street. 
•  Building openings such as bay  
windows, loggias, and floor-
to-floor glazing, in addition to  
punched window openings, are  
encouraged to create diversity.  
•  Façades along streets and open  
spaces shall have windows.  
•  Shutters, when used, should  
be operable and not fixed or  
decorative.  
•  Window reveals should be a  
minimum of two-inches deep.   
  
•  Main entrances should be  
designed to create visual 
interest  for pedestrians  
•  The width of garages and  
parking entries should be  
minimized. Openings for  
garages and parking should  
be integrated into the façade 
design.   
  
•  For Blocks RE-2 and RE-3, a vertical 
clearance of 13.5-feet  shall be 
maintained above the  EVAE along 
the Joint Powers Board property. 
 
   
•  Unless specified differently in  
these guidelines, projections  shall 
maintain a minimum  distance of 
six feet from street  trees.   
Projections  
•  Façade projections such as  
bay windows or balconies are  
permitted above the ground-floor 
along all streets.   
•  Façade projections may extend  
up to four feet past the block  
line, provided that a minimum  
eight feet of vertical clearance is  
provided below.   
•  Projections in setback areas shall  
maintain a minimum seven-foot  
clearance from sidewall to lower  
edge of projection.  
•  Projections are encouraged  to 
respond to their immediate  
context to address issues such as  
noise levels, view corridors, and  
sunlight exposure
 
•  Purely decorative projections  such 
as cornices, eaves, sills, and  belt 
courses are permitted.  
Stepbacks   
•  Façade stepbacks such as loggias, 
balconies, terraces or other 
architectural treatments are 
permitted on all streets.  
FIGURE 4.45 Example of openings and  
recesses

--- page 467 ---
139
CHAPTER 4 BLOCKS AND BUILDING ELEMENTS 
FIGURE 4.46 Roof form example  
FIGURE 4.47  Roofs should integrate green  
design features   
FIGURE 4.48 Mixed-use ground-floor  
transparency example  
Roofs and Mechanical   
Equipment  
Design Intent  
Roofs are critical visual elements  
in creating successful buildings  
and should be designed as a  
“5th façade” integrated into the  
building’s overall design.  
•  Green design features that  
generate renewable energy are  
encouraged on building roofs.  
Any green design features  should 
be integrated into   the overall 
building and roof  design.  
•  Roof designs with architectural  
features are encouraged for  
Blocks MU-1. 
•  The roof should include volumes  
and surfaces varying in form,  
massing, and height, as well 
as  design components that are  
attractive from both street level  
and adjacent buildings.  
Roof materials  
•  Utilize the roof’s available surface  
as an additional opportunity for  
usable open space or sustainable  
design strategies.  
Roof design  
•  Modulation and articulation  of 
rooflines within the height  limits 
is encouraged to minimize  
massiveness and uniformity of  
buildings.  
•  Roofs should use non-reflective  
materials and low-intensity color  
to minimize the heat-island  effect. 
Dark materials should be  avoided. 
  
•  Renewable/energy-generating  
materials are encouraged.  
•  Materials with potential  negative 
impacts on stormwater  quality, such 
as copper, are not  permitted.  
Mechanical Equipment  
•  Height variations and roof  
shapes should be used to create  
attractive rooflines.  
•  Roofs with usable open spaces,  
such as roof gardens or terraces,  
are encouraged.  
•  Rooftop enclosures should  be used 
to screen mechanical  units and 
generally should be  set back from 
the parapet.  Mechanical equipment  
enclosures, mechanical and  elevator 
enclosures, exit stair  enclosures and 
other roofscape  elements should be 
considered  as sculptural forms that 
can add  to the overall composition 
and  visual interest of the building  
when viewed from a distance.  
•  All mechanical equipment,  
ventilation exhausts, and other  
HVAC components should be  
integrated into roof design to  
minimize their visual impact from  
street level and from elevated  
points in adjacent buildings.

--- page 468 ---
140
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Mixed-Use Ground   
Floors and Pedestrian   
Scale  
Design Intent  
Create an attractive pedestrian  
experience with treatment and  
materials at the ground floor that  
are sensitive to the pedestrian  
scale. Encourage interaction  
between building interiors and  
outdoor spaces.  
Façade design  
•  Multiple entrances are  encouraged 
to foster activity and  interaction 
between buildings  and sidewalks.
  
•  Blank building walls are not  
encouraged.  
•  Façades without multiple  entrances 
should create visual  interest with 
varied materials and  storefront 
display windows or  other forms of 
transparency.   
•  Façades should use architectural  
features such as canopies,  awnings 
or signage.   
•  Designs are encouraged to  clearly 
define the base and  maintain a 
clear separation  between the 
ground floor and  the floors above.  
FIGURE 4.49 Awning example  
•  Providing human-scaled  
architectural features is  
particularly important in areas  
where pedestrian activity is  
encouraged; the highest level  
of detail should occur close to  
pedestrian areas, near streets  and 
entries, and around the  ground 
floor.  
Materials  
•  Ground-floor façades should be  
predominantly transparent with  
clear glass on Block MU-1.  
•  Any retail façade shall be  
a minimum of 70 percent  
transparent.  
•  Office façades should be  
a minimum of 50 percent  
transparent.   
•  The base of storefront windows  
should be raised at least six  
inches above sidewalk finished  
grade.  
•  Retail storefronts should remain  
unshuttered at night and provide  
clear views of interior spaces.  
Setbacks  
•  Minor setbacks of retail façades  
are permitted to add variety 
and accommodate seating and 
outdoor dining along the sidewalk. 
Such  setbacks shall be no more 
than   
FIGURE 4.50 Materials and setbacks example  
    six feet deep and at least 10 feet  
but no more than 36 feet long.  
•  Recessed doorways for retail  
uses are encouraged. The depth  
of recessed doorways should  
be a minimum of two feet and a  
maximum of four feet.   
 • Paving materials used for floor  
areas in contact with sidewalk  
should be of equal or superior  
quality to that of the sidewalk.   
 • Ground-floor setback areas  
adjacent to mixed-use and non-
residential façades should be  
treated as sidewalk extensions,  
providing additional area for  
walking as well as for benches,  
café tables, or landscape.  
Awnings  
•  Awnings, canopies, or marquees  
are encouraged to create a  
comfortable scale and sheltered  
spaces for pedestrians.  
•  Awnings that project from  
building façades should not  
conflict with street trees.  
•  Awnings shall maintain a  
minimum vertical clearance of 
eight feet.  
•  Architectural detailing of awnings 
is encouraged to incorporate  
design features related to the  
identity of the ground-floor uses.

•  Retail and lobby entries are

Entries

and convey an inviting  image 
to the community. They  should

visual element of the  building

recessed  doorways.

Residential    
Ground Floors and   
Pedestrian Scale  
Design Intent  
Establish a comfortable pedestrian  
experience and provide adequate  
transition from the street for  
ground level units.  
Façade design  
•  Residential ground floors are  
encouraged to include building  
lobbies, direct individual unit  
entrances, and/or common areas  
near the street.  
•  Building lobbies and common  
areas, such as community rooms,  
gyms, residential offices and  
media centers, are encouraged to  
have a high level of transparency.   
•  Ground-floor units are encouraged 
to be raised 18 to 24 inches above  
finished sidewalk grade.  
•  Façade designs for ground-floor  
units are encouraged to provide  
privacy.  
•  Exterior entrances to individual  
dwelling units are encouraged  to 
have stoops.
•  The use of continuous plantings  
of a height between four and  
eight feet above sidewalk  
grade to create a hedge is  
discouraged; however, trees  
are permitted provided they  
maintain visibility beneath their  
respective canopies.  
•  Architectural elements 
employed  in setback areas, 
such as stairs,  railings, low 
walls, and planters,  should use 
materials and details  that are 
similar to those of the  rest of 
the building.  
•  Stoops are encouraged to be 
perpendicular to the sidewalk.    
•  Openings for garage entrances  
should be minimized and  
integrated into the ground-
floor façade design. Placement  
of garage entrances shall be  
coordinated with the streetscape.  
•  Residential units adjacent to  any 
street or easement should  be 
designed to provide a level  of 
privacy while maintaining  visibility 
to that street or  easement.  
Materials  
•  Please refer to the “Materials  and 
Colors” subsection of  Building 
Façades on page 137.  
Setbacks  
•  Building setbacks are  encouraged 
to have stoops,  building lobbies, 
unit entrances,  awnings, and 
landscape to create  a pedestrian 
scale and transition  to the street.  
•  Entrances to units fronting The 
Park are encouraged to be 
coordinated with The Park’s design 
to create active engagement 
between the building and the 
landscape.

Residential Usable

Design Intent  
Provide greenery and open space  for 
residents; create transitions  between 
buildings and their  adjacent public 
streets and open  spaces; and add 
visual interest to  building façades, 
roofs, and podium  areas.   
Private Usable Open   
Space  
•  Types of private usable open  
space are encouraged to include  
balconies, terraces, loggias,  patios, 
roof decks, and yards.  
•  Private usable open spaces  should 
be integrated into  façade design, 
roof design, and  landscape of 
adjacent streets  and park spaces. 
    
•  Screens, trellises, and minor  
architectural structures are  
encouraged to provide privacy  to 
private usable open spaces  where 
adjacent to common usable  open 
space.  
FIGURE 4.57 Common residential usable open space example  
Common Usable Open   
Space  
•  Types of common usable open  
space are encouraged to include  
roof decks, courtyards, and  
gardens.  
•  Common usable open spaces  
should be visually and/or  
physically linked to the publicly 
accessible open space system.  
•  Amenities not available in  
private usable open spaces 
are  encouraged for common 
usable  open spaces. Amenities 
such  as seating areas, gathering  
areas, planting areas, flower  
gardens, shade trees, lawn areas,  
sculptures, trellises, lighting,  
fountains, and barbecue facilities  
are encouraged.  
•  The on-site collection and  
treatment of stormwater from  
building roofs is encouraged in  
common usable open space.   
•  Any devices for the collection  and 
treatment of stormwater  should 
be integrated into the  design of 
the common usable  open space.

•  In general, only natural  
construction materials such 
as  wood, metals, ceramic, and  
stone should be used for signs.  
•  Contrasting colors should be  
used between the color of the  
background and the letters or

signs include vinyl or paint,  
applied to the inside face of

should consist of incandescent,  
halogen, LED, metal halide, or

pressure sodium, and fluorescent 
lighting types are discouraged.  
Signage  
Design Intent  
Signage helps to give the public  
realm its character.  The following 
signage guidelines are meant to  
create lively street frontages in  
order to encourage pedestrian  
activity and help way-finding.   
They apply to all permanent and  
temporary signs installed in the  
Plan Area that are visible from  
publicly accessible open spaces  
or streets, including interior signs  
designed or arranged to be  
primarily visible from such public  
areas, except those signs that  
are exempt from the permit and  
fee requirements of Title XXV of  
the San Mateo Municipal Code  
(Signs).  
All Signs  
•  Signs within the Plan Area should 
comply with Section 25.06.070(b)  
[Regulations in all Commercial  
(C) Districts (C1, C2, C3, C4 and   
CBD Support)] of the San Mateo  
Municipal Code.  
•  Signs on high-rise buildings  
within the Plan Area should  
comply with the restrictions set  
forth in Section 25.06.050(b)(3) of 
the San Mateo Municipal Code.

Chapter 5  
Sustainability  
In the Plan Area, sustainable principles and strategies should be  
incorporated into the planning and design of buildings, streets and  
sidewalks, and public and private open spaces. Development in the  Plan 
Area shall comply with the requirements of Chapter 23.70 “Green  Building 
Requirements” of the San Mateo Municipal Code. The San  Mateo County 
Sustainability Checklist, among other criteria, should also  be consulted 
during SPAR.   
The landowner has registered the Project in the LEED-ND Pilot Program,  
ensuring that certain sustainability criteria are applied to the Plan.

--- page 480 ---
152
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
LEED for Neighborhood Development (LEED-ND)  
•  Native and low water-consuming  
plantings should be used to help  
reduce water consumption.  
LEED, an acronym for Leadership  
in Energy and Environmental  
Design, is a program developed  
by the US Green Building  
Council (USGBC) to encourage  
and accelerate adoption 
of  sustainable development  
practices.  LEED has become a  
nationally recognized standard  
for the design, construction,  
and operation of high-
performance green buildings and  
developments.   
The USGBC selected Station Park   
Green for its LEED-ND Pilot 
Program;  the project achieved 
Gold Level  designation at stage 
one certification.   Feedback from 
participants in the  pilot program, 
including Station  Park Green, 
informed and helped  refine the 
LEED-ND rating system  which now 
has been endorsed and  adopted.  
Station Park Green had the  added 
distinction of inclusion in the  LEED-
In 2007, the USGBC, in  
conjunction with the Congress  
for New Urbanism (CNU) and  
the Natural Resource Defense  
Council (NRDC), launched 
a  pilot program for LEED for  
Neighborhood Development  
(LEED-ND).  LEED-ND is one of  
the first rating systems to measure  
sustainable developments based  
on principles of smart growth,  
urbanism and green building  
design.  
ND Focus Group.  This entitled   
Station Park Green to prioritized  
processing of its application for  
LEED-ND certification.  
Sustainability   
Measures   
Environmentally sustainable 
planning  and design principles 
should be  incorporated into the 
site design,  open spaces, and 
buildings of the  Plan Area.  This 
chapter covers  the integration 
of those measures  with the Plan 
Area’s approach to  sustainability 
in infrastructure. The  previous 
chapters include guidelines  for 
how building and landscape  
components address sustainable  
design.  
•  Trees, vegetation, and buildings  
should be placed to mitigate the  
potential of strong prevailing  winds 
creating a “wind tunnel”  effect in 
pedestrian and open  spaces.  
•  Passive landscape and  streetscape 
areas within the Plan  Area should 
be used for primary  treatment of 
stormwater using  drought-resistant 
plantings.  
•  Building design should  incorporate 
green building  practices, in 
accordance with the  city’s mandatory 
green building  program.  
The Plan Area’s broad sustainable  
measures include:  
•  Using the block pattern and  
generous pedestrian spaces 
to create an  inviting, walkable 
neighborhood.   
•  Stormwater management within  
the Plan Area shall comply with  
the City of San Mateo’s National  
Pollution Elimination Discharge 
System (NPDES) permit, and be        
designed not to affect adversely  
the existing storm sewer network  
or the watercourse in Leslie  
Creek.   
 •  Treatment of stormwater run-
off should employ biofiltration 
and low impact development 
techniques to the extent required 
to meet the requirements of 
the County of San Mateo’s C.3 
Stormwater Quality guidelines. In 
certain areas where biofiltration 
techniques are infeasible, 
alternative treatment methods 
such as tree wells and mechanical 
devices may be required to 
meet regulations and/or permit 
requirements
•  To help reduce the urban heat-
island effect to the maximum  
extent practical, pavement areas  
should be minimized, alternative  
and light-colored concrete and  
pavers should be used, and tree  
canopy over pavements should  
be maximized.

Stormwater Management   
Stormwater    
Conveyance   
The stormwater system for  
the Plan Area shall consist of  
underground pipes in the main  
street sections and open spaces  
that convey run-off from the  
building parcels, streetscape, and  
open spaces to the existing City  
storm sewer systems in Concar  
Drive, South Delaware Street, and  
Garvey Way.  The new stormwater  
facilities shall be designed and  
constructed such that the existing  
City drainage system, Leslie Creek 
and its associated watershed are  
not negatively impacted.  
The effective run-off coefficient,   
C, by block type, should not  
exceed the following:  
•  Mixed-use blocks (MU): C = 0.75  
•  Residential blocks (RE): C = 0.7  
Quality   
Stormwater    
The stormwater quality design  
shall comply with requirements of  
the National Pollution Discharge  
Elimination System (NPDES)  
regulations and the City of  San 
Mateo’s Water Pollution   
Prevention Permit.   
Best Management Practices  
such as biofilters, rain gardens,  
permeable paving, and swales  
shall be integrated into the Plan  
Area, as described below and on  
the following pages:  
Water Quality in   
Streetscape  
The run-off from “A,”  “B,”  
“C,” Streets and “Garvey Way 
Extension” shall be treated using 
the following (or equivalent) 
permissible biofiltration 
methods:

--- page 482 ---
154
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Biofiltration Planters in    
Landscape Strip  
Planters in the landscape strip  
between the roadbed and  
sidewalks shall be configured 
to  permit stormwater flow in 
the  gutter to enter the planter at 
the upstream end (Figure 5.01).   
Water shall filter through the soil  
media during low-flow events.   
During higher-flow events, water  
exits the planter, either via an  
overflow drain connected to the  
storm drain system within the  
planter, or returns to the gutter  
via an outlet.    
Treated water flows out of  the 
system via an under-drain  
connected to a storm drain pipe  
or other appropriate outfall.  
The planters are not permitted to 
be used as stand-alone drainage  
facilities. Traditional catch basins  
shall be located downstream for  
effective bypass of flows during  
higher-intensity rainfall events.  
Permeable Pavements  
Permeable pavements  are 
encouraged in limited  
applications within streets.  
Permeable paving shall be   
engineered to account for the  
sub-soil conditions.  Design  
recommendations include:  
under-drain connected to a storm  
drain pipe or other appropriate  
outfall.   
•  The bottom of the stone  reservoir 
shall not exceed 2%  slope with 
an under-drain system  to ensure 
infiltration through the  entire 
surface.   
•  Porous pavement should be  
located at least two to five  feet 
above the seasonally high  
groundwater table.  
Filterra® units (or equivalent)    
These units consist of a concrete  
box, a mulch layer, approximately   
1.5 to 3.5 feet of a unique soil  filter 
media, an observation/ clean-out 
pipe, an under-drain system and 
an appropriate type of plant, i.e., 
flowers, grasses, shrubs, or trees. 
The top of the unit should be 
recessed to permit the specified 
sidewalk material to be laid on top 
of the box.   
Stormwater run-off drains directly  
from the impervious right-of-way  
surfaces through an inlet structure  
in the concrete box and flows  
through the mulch, plant, and  soil 
filter media. Treated water  shall 
flow out of the system via an   
Filterra® units, or the like, shall  
not be placed in sump conditions.  
Filterra® cannot be used as a  
stand-alone inlet.  A downstream  
catch basin should be provided  for 
effective bypass of flows  during 
higher-intensity rainfall  events.  
Surface flow shall not be  directed 
to Filterra® in a head-on 
configuration. A cross-linear  flow 
configuration (left-to-right or  right-
to-left) in the gutter in front  of the 
unit is encouraged.  
Plant types and tree grates should  
be consistent with the adjacent  
streetscape.   
Water Quality in Open   
Spaces  
The Park, Community Park, 
Gateway Park, and Greenway 
Pocket Parks as well as Green 
Corridors and other open spaces 
should be self-treating areas that 
incorporate planted areas that 
treat the stormwater run-off they 
generate. Additionally stormwater 
treatment areas, such as rain 
gardens, swales and biofiltration 
planters, incorporated into 
the open spaces should aid in 
treatment of the streets and parcel 
water quality.  
Rain Gardens  
A rain garden is a shallow,  
depressed area in the landscape,  
planted with vegetation that can  
withstand periodic inundations of  
water (Figure 5.04). Rain garden  
examples are shown in Figures   
5.06-5.08. Requirements for rain-
gardens include:  
•  Rain gardens should be located  
away from buildings so water is  
not directed toward foundation  
structures.   
•  Amended soil should be used. To  
increase infiltration, compaction  
of soil should be avoided.  
•  A sub-drain shall be provided to  
promote infiltration.  
•  Surrounding land should be  
sloped towards the rain garden.  
•  Overflow should be directed  to 
a discharge point such as an  
overflow drain within the rain  
garden.

--- page 483 ---
155
Overflow   
bypass  
Disconnected    
leader  
Water Quality in Buildings– 
Blocks and Open Spaces   
Run-off from the building roofs 
and hardscape in at-grade areas 
shall be treated using  one 
or more of the following (or  
equivalent) methods:    
Planter Boxes   
Planter boxes should be located  
within the block setbacks or within  
courtyards to treat roof run-off  
(Figure 5.05).  Roof leaders shall  
discharge directly to the planter  
boxes.  
Swales  
The following strategies shall 
be  considered in the design of a  
swale:  
•  Swales should be located  away 
from active use areas and  
buildings so water is not directed  
toward  foundations.    
•  Sides of the swale should be  
gently sloped to reduce erosion.  
•  Swales should be sized to meet  
capacity requirements.   
•   Overflow catch basins along  the 
length of the swale should  be 
provided to permit bypass  of flow 
during higher-intensity  rainfall 
events.  
•  Swales should be sloped  towards 
a storm drainage system  structure 
such as a catch basin or  manhole. 
•  Compaction of swale subsoils  
should not be permitted during  
construction.  
•  Sub-drains should be considered  
to promote infiltration.  
Disconnected Roof Leaders  
Splash blocks shall be used to  
reduce the velocity and impact  
of water entering rain gardens,  
planter boxes, swales, etc., from  
roof leaders (Figure 5.03).  Splash  
blocks shall be constructed from  
concrete, stone, glass or other  
durable materials.   
The following strategies shall be  
considered:  
•  Ground should be sloped at  least 
2% to direct run-off away  from the 
buildings.  
•  Ponding water next to buildings  is 
not permitted.  
•  Roof drainage should be  
distributed to more than one  
downspout.  
Permeable Pavement  
Although not suitable for  sole 
treatment, the use of  permeable 
paving is encouraged  in 
driveways, courtyards, and  
walkways to reduce the amount of  
treatment required.  
Permeable paving shall be  
engineered to account for the  
sub-soil conditions.  The following  
design recommendations shall be  
considered:  
•  The bottom of the stone  reservoir 
should be completely  flat to 
ensure infiltration through  the 
entire surface.   
•  Porous pavement should be  
located at least two to five  feet 
above the seasonally high  
groundwater table.  
•   Rain gardens and Biofiltration 
planters may also be used to treat  
runoff from the buildings.
FIGURE 5.03 Planter box with  
disconnected leader

Green Roofs  
Buildings are permitted to use  
green roofs to filter and reduce  
the volume of stormwater run-off.  
Cisterns  
Installation of cisterns is permitted 
to reduce the rate of stormwater  
run-off from the buildings,  
provided that:  
•  Cisterns shall be integrated into  
the building design.   
•  If a cistern holds greater than  six 
inches of water, it shall be  covered 
securely, or have a  top opening of 
fewer than four  inches. 
 
•  Cistern design and maintenance  
should minimize the potential  
for clogging by leaves and other  
debris.

Flood Control Strategy  
In early 2012, the City of San Mateo 
completed levee improvements that 
resulted in revisions to the FEMA 
flood insurance mapping in October 
2012. These improvements removed 
the Plan Area from the 100-year flood 
plain (1-percent chance annual flood). 
Parts of the site remain in Zone X, 
the 500-year flood plain (0.2 percent 
chance annual flood).
Due to this change, the requirements 
of San Mateo Municipal Code Chap-
ter 23.33, which were included in the 
2011 Specific Plan and Design Guide-
lines documents, no longer apply.
Currently, portions of the Plan Area 
(below +104.7 ft Adjusted City Datum) 
are susceptible to flooding in the 
unlikely event of failure of the Crystal 
Springs Reservoir Dam (Corridor Plan 
EIR).

--- page 488 ---
160
STATIONPARKGREEN DESIGN GUIDELINES  
Site Preparation 
and Design  
The following principles are   
encouraged:   
•   Site disturbance: Minimize  
impact on pre-existing native  
vegetation and permeable  
surfaces, including during  
construction. 
Neighborhood Design  
and Community
 
•   Heat-Island effect: Increase  
the amount of pervious 
and/ or shaded areas to 
reduce  microclimate impact. 
Discourage  dark roof 
materials. 
•   Infrastructure, energy   
efficiency: Consider energy- 
efficient infrastructure 
equipment. 
 
•   Recycled content for 
infrastructure: Consider 
materials with recycled content 
for infrastructure.  
•  Site forestation: Encourage site  
greening through a high density  
of trees and plants.  
•  Light pollution: Design open-
space lighting to minimize  
impact on the night sky.  
•  Construction waste 
management: Promote the  
diversion of construction  
waste and demolition debris  
from disposal in landfills and  
incinerators.  
The following principles are   
encouraged:   
Compact development: 
Optimize the allowed density 
through a strategy that fits with 
the community.  
Walkable streets: Promote  
pedestrian activity and public health 
through increased physical activity, 
using:   
•  Frequent use of building entries  
Parking footprint: Minimize  
off-street parking impact by  
encouraging integration of 
parking in building cores and/ 
or underground, such that the  
majority of street frontages will 
favor pedestrian activity.   
•  Street proportions that ensure  
space definition  
•  Limited traffic speed  
•  Limited and coordinated  setbacks 
for buildings framing  streets 
 •  Mixed-use building façades  primarily 
contiguous to sidewalks  
•  High level of transparency for  
non-residential uses and ground-floor 
façades fronting streets  
•  Minimized blank walls  
•  Regular rhythm of street trees  
between travel lanes and  sidewalk 
walking areas   
•  Ground floor of residential units  
should be at least two feet above  
finished sidewalk grade

--- page 489 ---
161
Green Building Design  
Bicycle network: Encourage  
bicycle use by providing bicycle  
lanes and storage facilities.  
Access to publicly accessible  
open space: Foster walking,  
physical activity and time spent  
outdoors, as well as social  
interaction, through access to and 
connectivity of parks.  
Housing Diversity: Offer a range  
of housing types to enable a  
variety of residents to live in the  
Plan Area.  
Universal accessibility: Provide a  
high level of accessibility through  
general street grades and  
individual designs which meet  
applicable ADA requirements.  
Internet accessibility: Consider  
wireless access for the Plan Area.  
The following principles are  
encouraged to ensure sustainable 
building design:   
•   Construction waste should  
be recycled and reused when  
possible.  
•   Roofs should reduce heat-
island  effect through design and  
materials selection.   
•   Façades of buildings with  
residential uses should be  
designed to take advantage of  
natural lighting and optimize  
solar gain.  
•   Façades of buildings with  
retail and office uses should be  
designed to shield peak gains  
and optimize natural lighting.  
•   Building design should  
incorporate operable windows  
and open-air courtyards to  
encourage natural and cross  
ventilation as part of the HVAC  
system.  
•   Building design should include  
water-efficient fixtures and  
plumbing such as faucets,  shower 
heads, and toilets that  reduce water 
consumption.  
•   Energy-efficient lighting,  
appliances, and HVAC  equipment 
should be used to  reduce the 
energy required for  building 
operation.  
•   Recycled or locally-produced  
building materials should  be used 
where practical and  appropriate.
  
•   Buildings should use rating  
systems to evaluate design  
and pursue green design  
certification.  
•   Additional green design features  
such as on-site renewable energy  
sources and green roofs should  be 
considered.

Definitions  
ACTIVE USE:

residential entrances, or other

professional offices  
AMENDED SOIL:   
A soil mix that allows adequate  
drainage through the soil to  
remove pollutants and provide  
efficient drainage  
APARTMENT:  
A room or suite of rooms used or  
designed for use as a residence  
by a family, located in a building  
containing two or more such  
rooms or suites  
ARCADE:   
A length of sidewalk covered by  
enclosed building mass above,  
which is supported by a series of  
arches on columns or piers  
ARCHITECTURAL FEATURE:  
A visible treatment of building  
massing, fenestration, materials,  
or other distinctive details on a  
building  
ARTICULATION:   
BAY WINDOW:   
Variation in the massing, setback,  
height, or architectural features 
of  a building, such as changes 
in wall  plane, apparent height, 
materials  and colors, vertical 
recesses  and projections, floor 
levels,  roof forms, parapets or 
cornice  treatments, the location 
of garage  and residential 
entrances, or  window forms and 
patterns

BLOCK OPENING:   
A window or group of windows  
that project(s) from a building wall 
BIOFILTRATION PLANTER:   
A break in the frontage or  
streetwall to allow for a visual or  
physical connection to the interior 
of a block  
A landscape element that is  
designed to capture runoff from  
streets and pavement areas to  
permit water-quality treatment by

of  an upper floor that extends

below

or filter  
BLANK (BUILDING) WALL:   
A side of a building lacking any  
windows or architectural features  
BLOCK:   
A built area that is bounded by  
streets and/or open space  
BLOCK LINE:  
The boundary line that defines a  
block.  Typically, the block line is 
drawn at the back of sidewalk. 
BRISE SOLEIL:   
A horizontal projection extending  
from a building façade to shield

provide passive solar heating in  
the winter months  
BUFFER:

and plantings that screen one use  
or property from another, usually  
to mitigate noise or lights  
BUILD-TO BLOCK LINE:   
A line to which a building façade

may set a minimum percentage or  
length per façade

NEIGHBORHOOD MEETING SUMMARY 
 
ADDRESS: 
Station Park Green 
430 Station Park Circle #100 
San Mateo, CA 94402 
 
APN: 
035-200-220 
 
PLANNING  
APPLICATION: 
Specific Plan Amendment to Station Park Green Specific Plan 
PROJECT  
LOCATION: 
Within an existing Station Park Green mixed-use building at Northwest corner 
of S. Delaware Street and Concar Drive 
MEETING  
DATE/TIME: 
Wednesday, March 19, 2025 from 6 to 7 PM 
MEETING  
LOCATION: 
Virtual Zoom meeting 
ZOOM LINK: 
Zoom Link  Passcode: hLths%4u 
MEETING  
RECORDING LINK: 
Zoom Link  Passcode: hLths%4u 
ATTENDEES: 
12 members of the community signed in during the one-hour meeting 
APPLICANTS  
AND/OR TEAM: 
Chris Kober, Vice President – Essex Property Trust, Inc.  
Katia Kamangar – Consultant to Essex Property Trust, Inc. 
 
PURPOSE OF MEETING: 
 
Neighborhood meeting for residents living within 1,000-foot radius of Station Park Green.  The 
purpose was to share information about the proposed plans to convert vacant retail space in the 
building to office use, to describe the associated landscape enhancements, discuss timeline and 
parking, and to offer an opportunity for residents to ask questions and provide feedback and 
comments. 
 
INTRODUCTION: 
 
Chris Kober and Katia Kamangar opened the meeting and presented the proposed project, 
including a vicinity map, a site plan, and several renderings of the proposed exterior and landscape 
enhancements.  The Station Park Green apartments were constructed in 2018 and the ground floor 
retail space has struggled to attract and retain tenants.  The proposal is to amend the Station Park 
Green Specific Plan to permit some of the long-vacant retail to be converted to office use to help 
activate the currently dark and empty spaces along Concar and S. Delaware streets.  The Essex

--- page 504 ---
2 
 
team also invited everyone to come visit an open house with refreshments the following week at the 
Community Room at Station Park Green (March 25th from 5 to 7 pm) and to see the space San 
Mateo residents can use, free of charge, by reserving the space via email at 
stationparkgreen@essex.com. 
 
QUESTIONS & ANSWERS: 
 
1. 
Q. When will the update happen? Please keep it clean during work. 
 
A. Project construction will likely occur next year (2026) after project approvals.  We will pay 
attention to keep the community clean as we go. 
 
2. 
Q. For the 1,400 businesses approached, what was the most popular reasons that they did 
not want to rent retail space in Station Park Green? 
 
A. The main reasons were lack of parking in front of the businesses, and lack of significant 
foot traffic (compared to downtown as an example). 
 
3. 
Q. For the employees that will be working at SPF, where will they park? Are they going to be 
using the retail parking? 
 
A. They will park in the garage that exists in the building.  There are sufficient spaces in this 
garage to account for both the office and the retail uses so the parking would be done with 
off-street parking within the garage. 
 
4. 
Q. Please ask Bay Club and Crunch to either appreciate the gym we have or keep  Starbucks 
here, thank you. 
 
A. We acknowledged that having Starbucks here was great but unfortunately they vacated 
the space before their lease ended. We hope to get another coffee shop here. 
 
5. 
Q. We appreciate the upgraded CalTrain and SamTrans are quite an asset also. 
 
A. We agree that those are both amenities that both the residents and office employees will 
benefit from. 
 
6. 
Q. Will this affect the price per unit; I currently pay $3,500 per month? 
 
A. As mentioned during the presentation, questions related to property repair or rent related 
matters will be deferred to the leasing team to address, if needed. 
 
 
7. 
Q. With the influx of employees, one major concern is traffic jam.  How do you manage that? 
Currently the Station Park Green circle is often occupied by delivery vehicles and it’s already 
sometimes hard to get around. 
 
A. The project benefits from excellent nearby freeway access.  At any given day, there would 
be about 40 to 50 people here given a flexible work schedule.  People are not arriving nor

--- page 505 ---
3 
 
departing at a given time.  Traffic was studied as part of original project approvals and will 
be studied under city guidance again for this application. 
 
8. 
Q. There are 46 retail parking spaces.  Are there other parking spaces in the building not 
being used? 
 
A. Yes, in fact, in the original entitlements some ground floor and some upstairs parking 
spaces were allocated for office and retail use.  Those will all be part of what’s available. 
 
9. 
Q. USPS is our next door neighbor; we all appreciate upgrades, please. 
 
A. The post office is part of federal government so it’s not something we control. We are not 
aware of any plans related to the post office but can try to find out.  
 
10. 
Q. Will Foster City Essex be included? 
 
 
A. Essex team asked for added clarification on this question but none was noted. 
 
11. 
Q. Security has always been an issue, package theft has been plaguing SPG, we need extra 
security measures to protect the residence area.  Are there any plans to do that? 
 
A. No response but attendees were told that these types of questions/comments would be 
forwarded to the leasing & property management teams as feedback and to address. 
 
12. 
Q. Will Essex be upgrading the plaza also? 
 
A. Yes, as part of the proposal, Essex will upgrade the corner plaza with new furniture, string 
lights and upgraded landscaping.  Goal is to refresh this space and to hopefully attract a 
restaurant or café type of tenant here, next to Beach Hut Deli. 
 
13. 
Q. The structure by Beach Hut Deli was projected to be a water structure; will that return? 
 
A. Yes, it is a water retention area so that rainwater collected on site goes into this area that 
we have as a planter.  The landscaping is the aesthetic part of it and the functional part is 
that the water goes back into the water table so it doesn’t run off into the Bay.  So that part 
exists today and will remain in function going forward. 
 
14. 
Q.  Thanks for the help. 
 
 
A. n/a

Q. See you next week, thanks again. 
 
 
A. n/a 
 
16. 
Q. What happens next?

A. The next step in the process is the formal application to the city for the proposal.  
Whenever there’s an amendment to the specific plan, as the first step we do a 
neighborhood meeting to introduce the project.  The step after that is the filing of the 
application itself.  The application will be reviewed by the city and go through the formal 
public hearing process of the planning commission and city council later this year.  After 
that there will be tenant improvements (“TI”s), improvements inside the building spaces,

Station Park Green
430 Station Park Circle
(APN 035-200-220)
Addendum to the Station Park Green (PA 07-030) Initial Study/Mitigated 
Negative Declaration State Clearinghouse No. 2010082076 and to the 
Addendum for Station Park Green SPAR, SDP, and TM Application (PA 14-055)
A. INTRODUCTION
This environmental document is a second Addendum to the Station Park Green Project Initial Study/Mitigated 
Negative Declaration (State Clearinghouse No. 2010082076), adopted on January 18, 2011, by the City Council 
of the City of San Mateo (the “MND”). The MND tiered off of the Rail Corridor Plan EIR (State Clearinghouse 
No. 2003042170). The program studied in the MND matched that described in the Specific Plan and Design 
Guidelines, and authorized under the Development Agreement, all of which were also studied and approved 
by the City Council on January 18, 2011. That program includes:
•
Up to 599 dwelling units;
•
A minimum of 25,000 gross square feet (“gsf”) and up to 60,000 gsf of retail / services / 
restaurant uses;
•
A minimum of 10,000 gsf and up to 45,000 gsf of office use; and
•
Approximately 2.0 acres of privately maintained open space open to the general public.
In 2014, an application (PA 14-055) for a Site Plan and Architectural Review (SPAR), Site Development Planning 
Application (SDPA), Vesting Tentative Parcel Map, and Amendments to the Station Park Green Specific Plan 
and Design Guidelines was submitted. Along with the application, an Addendum to the MND was provided. 
The City Council approved the application on February 2, 2015, and the approved program included:
•
599 dwelling units (1-BR, 2-BR and 3-BR Dwelling Units);
•
26,000 gross square feet (“gsf”) of retail / services / restaurant uses;
•
11,000 gsf of office uses; and
•
More than 2.0 acres of privately maintained open space open to the general public.
The project has since been completed. However, since the adoption of the Station Park Green Specific Plan 
and Design Guidelines and MND, the applicant proposes changes to the previously approved project, including 
modifying the minimum and maximum amounts of retail and office space that is permitted on the site. 
B. BACKGROUND
The City of San Mateo prepared the MND to address potentially significant environmental impacts resulting 
from the project. The range of potential environmental effects assessed included aesthetics, agriculture and 
forestry resources, air quality, biological resources, cultural resources, geology and soils, greenhouse gas 
emissions, hazards and hazardous materials, hydrology and water quality, land use and planning, mineral 
resources, land use and planning, noise, population and housing, public services, recreation, 
transportation/traffic, and utilities and service systems. The MND found that all impacts have been mitigated 
to below a level of significance through implementation of mitigation measures. In approving the original and 
subsequent projects, the City of San Mateo imposed a combined Conditions of Approval & Mitigation 
Monitoring Program.

--- page 508 ---
C. PURPOSE OF ADDENDUM
Once a project has been approved, the lead agency’s role in project approval is completed, unless further 
discretionary approval of that project is required (CEQA Guidelines 15162(c).) The Revised Project, however, 
does require further discretionary approval, including amendments to the Specific Plan and Design Guidelines. 
Accordingly, the City, as the lead agency, must determine whether a Subsequent EIR or MND is required for 
the Revised Project.
CEQA Guidelines Sections 15162 and 15164 set criteria for that determination. If the following criteria are all 
true, then a Subsequent EIR or MND is not required, and an Addendum is the appropriate document:
•
No new significant impacts will result from the project or from new mitigation measures.
•
No substantial increase in the severity of environmental impact will occur.
•
No new feasible alternatives or mitigation measures that would reduce impacts previously found not 
to be feasible have, in fact, been found to be feasible.
D.PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The proposed revisions include the following:
•
Changing the allowable use ranges from 25,000 - 30,000 gross square feet (gsf) of 
retail/services/restaurant uses, and 10,000 - 15,000 gsf of office uses to 3,000 - 30,000 gsf of 
retail/services/restaurant uses and 10,000 - 35,000 gsf of office uses. 
•
Changing the floor area ratio (FAR) values for retail from 0.2 to 0.1 and changing office FAR from 0.1 
to 0.2. 
•
Expanding the office space allowances to in addition to retail/services/restaurants on the ground floor 
to support occupancy of this existing non-residential space and support increase economic vibrancy.  
For a detailed comparison of the current project to the original and previously approved projects please refer 
to Section E.2 below.
D.1PROJECT LOCATION AND SETTING
The project site comprises approximately 12 acres located at the southwesterly corner of the intersection of 
South Delaware Street and Concar Drive in the City of San Mateo (the “Site”). The completed development 
contains four structures with residential uses. The development is located adjacent to Hayward Park Caltrain 
Station and has interior streets enclosing a park. Building MU-1, located at the corner of S. Delaware Street 
and Concar Drive, features four levels of residential units above ground-floor retail and office spaces.  The Site 
is located adjacent to a variety of uses, including: commercial and office uses directly to the west (including 
the Delaware Street Post Office); multi-family and single-family uses to the north and east; retail uses to the 
east along the State Route 92 corridor; office uses directly to the south adjacent to the highway off-ramp; and 
the Hayward Park CalTrain Station, rail line and park-and-ride lot directly to the south. Both the City’s General 
Plan and its zoning code designate the entire Site for Transit Oriented Development (“TOD”).

--- page 509 ---


--- page 510 ---
